He\r\nwas learning the sheep-keeping business, so as to follow on at the\r\nflock when the other should die, but had got no further than the\r\nrudiments as yet\xe2\x80\x94still finding an insuperable difficulty in\r\ndistinguishing between doing a thing well enough and doing it too well.\r\nSo earnest and yet so wrong-headed was this young dog (he had no name\r\nin particular, and answered with perfect readiness to any pleasant\r\ninterjection), that if sent behind the flock to help them on, he did it\r\nso thoroughly that he would have chased them across the whole county\r\nwith the greatest pleasure if not called off or reminded when to stop\r\nby the example of old George.\r\n\r\nThus much for the dogs. On the further side of Norcombe Hill was a\r\nchalk-pit, from which chalk had been drawn for generations, and spread\r\nover adjacent farms. Two hedges converged upon it in the form of a V,\r\nbut without quite meeting. The narrow opening left, which was\r\nimmediately over the brow of the pit, was protected by a rough railing.\r\n\r\nOne night, when Farmer Oak had returned to his house, believing there\r\nwould be no further necessity for his attendance on the down, he called\r\nas usual to the dogs, previously to shutting them up in the outhouse\r\ntill next morning. Only one responded\xe2\x80\x94old George; the other could not\r\nbe found, either in the house, lane, or garden. Gabriel then remembered\r\nthat he had left the two dogs on the hill eating a dead lamb (a kind of\r\nmeat he usually kept from them, except when other food ran short), and\r\nconcluding that the young one had not finished his meal, he went\r\nindoors to the luxury of a bed, which latterly he had only enjoyed on\r\nSundays.\r\n\r\nIt was a still, moist night. Just before dawn he was assisted in waking\r\nby the abnormal reverberation of familiar music. To the shepherd, the\r\nnote of the sheep-bell, like the ticking of the clock to other people,\r\nis a chronic sound that only makes itself noticed by ceasing or\r\naltering in some unusual manner from the well-known idle twinkle which\r\nsignifies to the accustomed ear, however distant, that all is well in\r\nthe fold. In the solemn calm of the awakening morn that note was heard\r\nby Gabriel, beating with unusual violence and rapidity. This\r\nexceptional ringing may be caused in two ways\xe2\x80\x94by the rapid feeding of\r\nthe sheep bearing the bell, as when the flock breaks into new pasture,\r\nwhich gives it an intermittent rapidity, or by the sheep starting off\r\nin a run, when the sound has a regular palpitation. The experienced ear\r\nof Oak knew the sound he now heard to be caused by the running of the\r\nflock with great velocity.\r\n\r\nHe jumped out of bed, dressed, tore down the lane through a foggy dawn,\r\nand ascended the hill. The forward ewes were kept apart from those\r\namong which the fall of lambs would be later, there being two hundred\r\nof the latter class in Gabriel\xe2\x80\x99s flock. These two hundred seemed to\r\nhave absolutely vanished from the hill. There were the fifty with their\r\nlambs, enclosed at the other end as he had left them, but the rest,\r\nforming the bulk of the flock, were nowhere. Gabriel called at the top\r\nof his voice the shepherd\xe2\x80\x99s call:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOvey, ovey, ovey!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNot a single bleat. He went to the hedge; a gap had been broken through\r\nit, and in the gap were the footprints of the sheep. Rather surprised\r\nto find them break fence at this season, yet putting it down instantly\r\nto their great fondness for ivy in winter-time, of which a great deal\r\ngrew in the plantation, he followed through the hedge. They were not in\r\nthe plantation. He called again: the valleys and farthest hills\r\nresounded as when the sailors invoked the lost Hylas on the Mysian\r\nshore; but no sheep. He passed through the trees and along the ridge of\r\nthe hill. On the extreme summit, where the ends of the two converging\r\nhedges of which we have spoken were stopped short by meeting the brow\r\nof the chalk-pit, he saw the younger dog standing against the sky\xe2\x80\x94dark\r\nand motionless as Napoleon at St. Helena.\r\n\r\nA horrible conviction darted through Oak. With a sensation of bodily\r\nfaintness he advanced: at one point the rails were broken through, and\r\nthere he saw the footprints of his ewes. The dog came up, licked his\r\nhand, and made signs implying that he expected some great reward for\r\nsignal services rendered. Oak looked over the precipice. The ewes lay\r\ndead and dying at its foot\xe2\x80\x94a heap of two hundred mangled carcasses,\r\nrepresenting in their condition just now at least two hundred more.\r\n\r\nOak was an intensely humane man: indeed, his humanity often tore in\r\npieces any politic intentions of his which bordered on strategy, and\r\ncarried him on as by gravitation. A shadow in his life had always been\r\nthat his flock ended in mutton\xe2\x80\x94that a day came and found every shepherd\r\nan arrant traitor to his defenseless sheep. His first feeling now was\r\none of pity for the untimely fate of these gentle ewes and their unborn\r\nlambs.\r\n\r\nIt was a second to remember another phase of the matter. The sheep were\r\nnot insured. All the savings of a frugal life had been dispersed at a\r\nblow; his hopes of being an independent farmer were laid low\xe2\x80\x94possibly\r\nfor ever. Gabriel\xe2\x80\x99s energies, patience, and industry had been so\r\nseverely taxed during the years of his life between eighteen and\r\neight-and-twenty, to reach his present stage of progress that no more\r\nseemed to be left in him. He leant down upon a rail, and covered his\r\nface with his hands.\r\n\r\nStupors, however, do not last for ever, and Farmer Oak recovered from\r\nhis. It was as remarkable as it was characteristic that the one\r\nsentence he uttered was in thankfulness:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank God I am not married: what would _she_ have done in the poverty\r\nnow coming upon me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOak raised his head, and wondering what he could do, listlessly\r\nsurveyed the scene. By the outer margin of the Pit was an oval pond,\r\nand over it hung the attenuated skeleton of a chrome-yellow moon which\r\nhad only a few days to last\xe2\x80\x94the morning star dogging her on the left\r\nhand. The pool glittered like a dead man\xe2\x80\x99s eye, and as the world awoke\r\na breeze blew, shaking and elongating the reflection of the moon\r\nwithout breaking it, and turning the image of the star to a phosphoric\r\nstreak upon the water. All this Oak saw and remembered.\r\n\r\nAs far as could be learnt it appeared that the poor young dog, still\r\nunder the impression that since he was kept for running after sheep,\r\nthe more he ran after them the better, had at the end of his meal off\r\nthe dead lamb, which may have given him additional energy and spirits,\r\ncollected all the ewes into a corner, driven the timid creatures\r\nthrough the hedge, across the upper field, and by main force of\r\nworrying had given them momentum enough to break down a portion of the\r\nrotten railing, and so hurled them over the edge.\r\n\r\nGeorge\xe2\x80\x99s son had done his work so thoroughly that he was considered too\r\ngood a workman to live, and was, in fact, taken and tragically shot at\r\ntwelve o\xe2\x80\x99clock that same day\xe2\x80\x94another instance of the untoward fate\r\nwhich so often attends dogs and other philosophers who follow out a\r\ntrain of reasoning to its logical conclusion, and attempt perfectly\r\nconsistent conduct in a world made up so largely of compromise.\r\n\r\nGabriel\xe2\x80\x99s farm had been stocked by a dealer\xe2\x80\x94on the strength of Oak\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npromising look and character\xe2\x80\x94who was receiving a percentage from the\r\nfarmer till such time as the advance should be cleared off. Oak found\r\nthat the value of stock, plant, and implements which were really his\r\nown would be about sufficient to pay his debts, leaving himself a free\r\nman with the clothes he stood up in, and nothing more.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI\r\nTHE FAIR\xe2\x80\x94THE JOURNEY\xe2\x80\x94THE FIRE\r\n\r\n\r\nTwo months passed away. We are brought on to a day in February, on\r\nwhich was held the yearly statute or hiring fair in the county-town of\r\nCasterbridge.\r\n\r\nAt one end of the street stood from two to three hundred blithe and\r\nhearty labourers waiting upon Chance\xe2\x80\x94all men of the stamp to whom\r\nlabour suggests nothing worse than a wrestle with gravitation, and\r\npleasure nothing better than a renunciation of the same. Among these,\r\ncarters and waggoners were distinguished by having a piece of whip-cord\r\ntwisted round their hats; thatchers wore a fragment of woven straw;\r\nshepherds held their sheep-crooks in their hands; and thus the\r\nsituation required was known to the hirers at a glance.\r\n\r\nIn the crowd was an athletic young fellow of somewhat superior\r\nappearance to the rest\xe2\x80\x94in fact, his superiority was marked enough to\r\nlead several ruddy peasants standing by to speak to him inquiringly, as\r\nto a farmer, and to use \xe2\x80\x9cSir\xe2\x80\x9d as a finishing word. His answer always\r\nwas,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am looking for a place myself\xe2\x80\x94a bailiff\xe2\x80\x99s. Do ye know of anybody who\r\nwants one?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGabriel was paler now. His eyes were more meditative, and his\r\nexpression was more sad. He had passed through an ordeal of\r\nwretchedness which had given him more than it had taken away. He had\r\nsunk from his modest elevation as pastoral king into the very\r\nslime-pits of Siddim; but there was left to him a dignified calm he had\r\nnever before known, and that indifference to fate which, though it\r\noften makes a villain of a man, is the basis of his sublimity when it\r\ndoes not. And thus the abasement had been exaltation, and the loss\r\ngain.\r\n\r\nIn the morning a regiment of cavalry had left the town, and a sergeant\r\nand his party had been beating up for recruits through the four\r\nstreets. As the end of the day drew on, and he found himself not hired,\r\nGabriel almost wished that he had joined them, and gone off to serve\r\nhis country. Weary of standing in the market-place, and not much\r\nminding the kind of work he turned his hand to, he decided to offer\r\nhimself in some other capacity than that of bailiff.\r\n\r\nAll the farmers seemed to be wanting shepherds. Sheep-tending was\r\nGabriel\xe2\x80\x99s speciality. Turning down an obscure street and entering an\r\nobscurer lane, he went up to a smith\xe2\x80\x99s shop.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow long would it take you to make a shepherd\xe2\x80\x99s crook?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwenty minutes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo shillings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe sat on a bench and the crook was made, a stem being given him into\r\nthe bargain.\r\n\r\nHe then went to a ready-made clothes\xe2\x80\x99 shop, the owner of which had a\r\nlarge rural connection. As the crook had absorbed most of Gabriel\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmoney, he attempted, and carried out, an exchange of his overcoat for a\r\nshepherd\xe2\x80\x99s regulation smock-frock.\r\n\r\nThis transaction having been completed, he again hurried off to the\r\ncentre of the town, and stood on the kerb of the pavement, as a\r\nshepherd, crook in hand.\r\n\r\nNow that Oak had turned himself into a shepherd, it seemed that\r\nbailiffs were most in demand. However, two or three farmers noticed him\r\nand drew near. Dialogues followed, more or less in the subjoined form:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere do you come from?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNorcombe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a long way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFifteen miles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s farm were you upon last?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis reply invariably operated like a rumour of cholera. The inquiring\r\nfarmer would edge away and shake his head dubiously. Gabriel, like his\r\ndog, was too good to be trustworthy, and he never made advance beyond\r\nthis point.\r\n\r\nIt is safer to accept any chance that offers itself, and extemporize a\r\nprocedure to fit it, than to get a good plan matured, and wait for a\r\nchance of using it. Gabriel wished he had not nailed up his colours as\r\na shepherd, but had laid himself out for anything in the whole cycle of\r\nlabour that was required in the fair. It grew dusk. Some merry men were\r\nwhistling and singing by the corn-exchange. Gabriel\xe2\x80\x99s hand, which had\r\nlain for some time idle in his smock-frock pocket, touched his flute\r\nwhich he carried there. Here was an opportunity for putting his dearly\r\nbought wisdom into practice.\r\n\r\nHe drew out his flute and began to play \xe2\x80\x9cJockey to the Fair\xe2\x80\x9d in the\r\nstyle of a man who had never known a moment\xe2\x80\x99s sorrow. Oak could pipe\r\nwith Arcadian sweetness, and the sound of the well-known notes cheered\r\nhis own heart as well as those of the loungers. He played on with\r\nspirit, and in half an hour had earned in pence what was a small\r\nfortune to a destitute man.\r\n\r\nBy making inquiries he learnt that there was another fair at Shottsford\r\nthe next day.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow far is Shottsford?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTen miles t\xe2\x80\x99other side of Weatherbury.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWeatherbury! It was where Bathsheba had gone two months before. This\r\ninformation was like coming from night into noon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow far is it to Weatherbury?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFive or six miles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba had probably left Weatherbury long before this time, but the\r\nplace had enough interest attaching to it to lead Oak to choose\r\nShottsford fair as his next field of inquiry, because it lay in the\r\nWeatherbury quarter. Moreover, the Weatherbury folk were by no means\r\nuninteresting intrinsically. If report spoke truly they were as hardy,\r\nmerry, thriving, wicked a set as any in the whole county. Oak resolved\r\nto sleep at Weatherbury that night on his way to Shottsford, and struck\r\nout at once into the high road which had been recommended as the direct\r\nroute to the village in question.\r\n\r\nThe road stretched through water-meadows traversed by little brooks,\r\nwhose quivering surfaces were braided along their centres, and folded\r\ninto creases at the sides; or, where the flow was more rapid, the\r\nstream was pied with spots of white froth, which rode on in undisturbed\r\nserenity. On the higher levels the dead and dry carcasses of leaves\r\ntapped the ground as they bowled along helter-skelter upon the\r\nshoulders of the wind, and little birds in the hedges were rustling\r\ntheir feathers and tucking themselves in comfortably for the night,\r\nretaining their places if Oak kept moving, but flying away if he\r\nstopped to look at them. He passed by Yalbury Wood where the game-birds\r\nwere rising to their roosts, and heard the crack-voiced cock-pheasants\r\n\xe2\x80\x9ccu-uck, cuck,\xe2\x80\x9d and the wheezy whistle of the hens.\r\n\r\nBy the time he had walked three or four miles every shape in the\r\nlandscape had assumed a uniform hue of blackness. He descended Yalbury\r\nHill and could just discern ahead of him a waggon, drawn up under a\r\ngreat over-hanging tree by the roadside.\r\n\r\nOn coming close, he found there were no horses attached to it, the spot\r\nbeing apparently quite deserted. The waggon, from its position, seemed\r\nto have been left there for the night, for beyond about half a truss of\r\nhay which was heaped in the bottom, it was quite empty. Gabriel sat\r\ndown on the shafts of the vehicle and considered his position. He\r\ncalculated that he had walked a very fair proportion of the journey;\r\nand having been on foot since daybreak, he felt tempted to lie down\r\nupon the hay in the waggon instead of pushing on to the village of\r\nWeatherbury, and having to pay for a lodging.\r\n\r\nEating his last slices of bread and ham, and drinking from the bottle\r\nof cider he had taken the precaution to bring with him, he got into the\r\nlonely waggon. Here he spread half of the hay as a bed, and, as well as\r\nhe could in the darkness, pulled the other half over him by way of\r\nbed-clothes, covering himself entirely, and feeling, physically, as\r\ncomfortable as ever he had been in his life. Inward melancholy it was\r\nimpossible for a man like Oak, introspective far beyond his neighbours,\r\nto banish quite, whilst conning the present untoward page of his\r\nhistory. So, thinking of his misfortunes, amorous and pastoral, he fell\r\nasleep, shepherds enjoying, in common with sailors, the privilege of\r\nbeing able to summon the god instead of having to wait for him.\r\n\r\nOn somewhat suddenly awaking, after a sleep of whose length he had no\r\nidea, Oak found that the waggon was in motion. He was being carried\r\nalong the road at a rate rather considerable for a vehicle without\r\nsprings, and under circumstances of physical uneasiness, his head being\r\ndandled up and down on the bed of the waggon like a kettledrum-stick.\r\nHe then distinguished voices in conversation, coming from the forpart\r\nof the waggon. His concern at this dilemma (which would have been\r\nalarm, had he been a thriving man; but misfortune is a fine opiate to\r\npersonal terror) led him to peer cautiously from the hay, and the first\r\nsight he beheld was the stars above him. Charles\xe2\x80\x99s Wain was getting\r\ntowards a right angle with the Pole star, and Gabriel concluded that it\r\nmust be about nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock\xe2\x80\x94in other words, that he had slept two hours.\r\nThis small astronomical calculation was made without any positive\r\neffort, and whilst he was stealthily turning to discover, if possible,\r\ninto whose hands he had fallen.\r\n\r\nTwo figures were dimly visible in front, sitting with their legs\r\noutside the waggon, one of whom was driving. Gabriel soon found that\r\nthis was the waggoner, and it appeared they had come from Casterbridge\r\nfair, like himself.\r\n\r\nA conversation was in progress, which continued thus:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe as \xe2\x80\x99twill, she\xe2\x80\x99s a fine handsome body as far\xe2\x80\x99s looks be concerned.\r\nBut that\xe2\x80\x99s only the skin of the woman, and these dandy cattle be as\r\nproud as a lucifer in their insides.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy\xe2\x80\x94so \xe2\x80\x99a do seem, Billy Smallbury\xe2\x80\x94so \xe2\x80\x99a do seem.\xe2\x80\x9d This utterance was\r\nvery shaky by nature, and more so by circumstance, the jolting of the\r\nwaggon not being without its effect upon the speaker\xe2\x80\x99s larynx. It came\r\nfrom the man who held the reins.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s a very vain feymell\xe2\x80\x94so \xe2\x80\x99tis said here and there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, now. If so be \xe2\x80\x99tis like that, I can\xe2\x80\x99t look her in the face. Lord,\r\nno: not I\xe2\x80\x94heh-heh-heh! Such a shy man as I be!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94she\xe2\x80\x99s very vain. \xe2\x80\x99Tis said that every night at going to bed she\r\nlooks in the glass to put on her night-cap properly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd not a married woman. Oh, the world!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd \xe2\x80\x99a can play the peanner, so \xe2\x80\x99tis said. Can play so clever that \xe2\x80\x99a\r\ncan make a psalm tune sound as well as the merriest loose song a man\r\ncan wish for.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cD\xe2\x80\x99ye tell o\xe2\x80\x99t! A happy time for us, and I feel quite a new man! And\r\nhow do she pay?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat I don\xe2\x80\x99t know, Master Poorgrass.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn hearing these and other similar remarks, a wild thought flashed into\r\nGabriel\xe2\x80\x99s mind that they might be speaking of Bathsheba. There were,\r\nhowever, no grounds for retaining such a supposition, for the waggon,\r\nthough going in the direction of Weatherbury, might be going beyond it,\r\nand the woman alluded to seemed to be the mistress of some estate. They\r\nwere now apparently close upon Weatherbury and not to alarm the\r\nspeakers unnecessarily, Gabriel slipped out of the waggon unseen.\r\n\r\nHe turned to an opening in the hedge, which he found to be a gate, and\r\nmounting thereon, he sat meditating whether to seek a cheap lodging in\r\nthe village, or to ensure a cheaper one by lying under some hay or\r\ncorn-stack. The crunching jangle of the waggon died upon his ear. He\r\nwas about to walk on, when he noticed on his left hand an unusual\r\nlight\xe2\x80\x94appearing about half a mile distant. Oak watched it, and the glow\r\nincreased. Something was on fire.\r\n\r\nGabriel again mounted the gate, and, leaping down on the other side\r\nupon what he found to be ploughed soil, made across the field in the\r\nexact direction of the fire. The blaze, enlarging in a double ratio by\r\nhis approach and its own increase, showed him as he drew nearer the\r\noutlines of ricks beside it, lighted up to great distinctness. A\r\nrick-yard was the source of the fire. His weary face now began to be\r\npainted over with a rich orange glow, and the whole front of his\r\nsmock-frock and gaiters was covered with a dancing shadow pattern of\r\nthorn-twigs\xe2\x80\x94the light reaching him through a leafless intervening\r\nhedge\xe2\x80\x94and the metallic curve of his sheep-crook shone silver-bright in\r\nthe same abounding rays. He came up to the boundary fence, and stood to\r\nregain breath. It seemed as if the spot was unoccupied by a living\r\nsoul.\r\n\r\nThe fire was issuing from a long straw-stack, which was so far gone as\r\nto preclude a possibility of saving it. A rick burns differently from a\r\nhouse. As the wind blows the fire inwards, the portion in flames\r\ncompletely disappears like melting sugar, and the outline is lost to\r\nthe eye. However, a hay or a wheat-rick, well put together, will resist\r\ncombustion for a length of time, if it begins on the outside.\r\n\r\nThis before Gabriel\xe2\x80\x99s eyes was a rick of straw, loosely put together,\r\nand the flames darted into it with lightning swiftness. It glowed on\r\nthe windward side, rising and falling in intensity, like the coal of a\r\ncigar. Then a superincumbent bundle rolled down, with a whisking noise;\r\nflames elongated, and bent themselves about with a quiet roar, but no\r\ncrackle. Banks of smoke went off horizontally at the back like passing\r\nclouds, and behind these burned hidden pyres, illuminating the\r\nsemi-transparent sheet of smoke to a lustrous yellow uniformity.\r\nIndividual straws in the foreground were consumed in a creeping\r\nmovement of ruddy heat, as if they were knots of red worms, and above\r\nshone imaginary fiery faces, tongues hanging from lips, glaring eyes,\r\nand other impish forms, from which at intervals sparks flew in clusters\r\nlike birds from a nest.\r\n\r\nOak suddenly ceased from being a mere spectator by discovering the case\r\nto be more serious than he had at first imagined. A scroll of smoke\r\nblew aside and revealed to him a wheat-rick in startling juxtaposition\r\nwith the decaying one, and behind this a series of others, composing\r\nthe main corn produce of the farm; so that instead of the straw-stack\r\nstanding, as he had imagined comparatively isolated, there was a\r\nregular connection between it and the remaining stacks of the group.\r\n\r\nGabriel leapt over the hedge, and saw that he was not alone. The first\r\nman he came to was running about in a great hurry, as if his thoughts\r\nwere several yards in advance of his body, which they could never drag\r\non fast enough.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, man\xe2\x80\x94fire, fire! A good master and a bad servant is fire, fire!\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nmane a bad servant and a good master. Oh, Mark Clark\xe2\x80\x94come! And you,\r\nBilly Smallbury\xe2\x80\x94and you, Maryann Money\xe2\x80\x94and you, Jan Coggan, and Matthew\r\nthere!\xe2\x80\x9d Other figures now appeared behind this shouting man and among\r\nthe smoke, and Gabriel found that, far from being alone he was in a\r\ngreat company\xe2\x80\x94whose shadows danced merrily up and down, timed by the\r\njigging of the flames, and not at all by their owners\xe2\x80\x99 movements. The\r\nassemblage\xe2\x80\x94belonging to that class of society which casts its thoughts\r\ninto the form of feeling, and its feelings into the form of\r\ncommotion\xe2\x80\x94set to work with a remarkable confusion of purpose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop the draught under the wheat-rick!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Gabriel to those nearest\r\nto him. The corn stood on stone staddles, and between these, tongues of\r\nyellow hue from the burning straw licked and darted playfully. If the\r\nfire once got _under_ this stack, all would be lost.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet a tarpaulin\xe2\x80\x94quick!\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel.\r\n\r\nA rick-cloth was brought, and they hung it like a curtain across the\r\nchannel. The flames immediately ceased to go under the bottom of the\r\ncorn-stack, and stood up vertical.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStand here with a bucket of water and keep the cloth wet.\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nGabriel again.\r\n\r\nThe flames, now driven upwards, began to attack the angles of the huge\r\nroof covering the wheat-stack.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA ladder,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Gabriel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe ladder was against the straw-rick and is burnt to a cinder,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\na spectre-like form in the smoke.\r\n\r\nOak seized the cut ends of the sheaves, as if he were going to engage\r\nin the operation of \xe2\x80\x9creed-drawing,\xe2\x80\x9d and digging in his feet, and\r\noccasionally sticking in the stem of his sheep-crook, he clambered up\r\nthe beetling face. He at once sat astride the very apex, and began with\r\nhis crook to beat off the fiery fragments which had lodged thereon,\r\nshouting to the others to get him a bough and a ladder, and some water.\r\n\r\nBilly Smallbury\xe2\x80\x94one of the men who had been on the waggon\xe2\x80\x94by this time\r\nhad found a ladder, which Mark Clark ascended, holding on beside Oak\r\nupon the thatch. The smoke at this corner was stifling, and Clark, a\r\nnimble fellow, having been handed a bucket of water, bathed Oak\xe2\x80\x99s face\r\nand sprinkled him generally, whilst Gabriel, now with a long\r\nbeech-bough in one hand, in addition to his crook in the other, kept\r\nsweeping the stack and dislodging all fiery particles.\r\n\r\nOn the ground the groups of villagers were still occupied in doing all\r\nthey could to keep down the conflagration, which was not much. They\r\nwere all tinged orange, and backed up by shadows of varying pattern.\r\nRound the corner of the largest stack, out of the direct rays of the\r\nfire, stood a pony, bearing a young woman on its back. By her side was\r\nanother woman, on foot. These two seemed to keep at a distance from the\r\nfire, that the horse might not become restive.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a shepherd,\xe2\x80\x9d said the woman on foot. \xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94he is. See how his\r\ncrook shines as he beats the rick with it. And his smock-frock is burnt\r\nin two holes, I declare! A fine young shepherd he is too, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhose shepherd is he?\xe2\x80\x9d said the equestrian in a clear voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t know, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t any of the others know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNobody at all\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ve asked \xe2\x80\x99em. Quite a stranger, they say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young woman on the pony rode out from the shade and looked\r\nanxiously around.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think the barn is safe?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cD\xe2\x80\x99ye think the barn is safe, Jan Coggan?\xe2\x80\x9d said the second woman,\r\npassing on the question to the nearest man in that direction.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSafe now\xe2\x80\x94leastwise I think so. If this rick had gone the barn would\r\nhave followed. \xe2\x80\x99Tis that bold shepherd up there that have done the most\r\ngood\xe2\x80\x94he sitting on the top o\xe2\x80\x99 rick, whizzing his great long arms about\r\nlike a windmill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe does work hard,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young woman on horseback, looking up at\r\nGabriel through her thick woollen veil. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish he was shepherd here.\r\nDon\xe2\x80\x99t any of you know his name.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever heard the man\xe2\x80\x99s name in my life, or seed his form afore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe fire began to get worsted, and Gabriel\xe2\x80\x99s elevated position being no\r\nlonger required of him, he made as if to descend.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaryann,\xe2\x80\x9d said the girl on horseback, \xe2\x80\x9cgo to him as he comes down, and\r\nsay that the farmer wishes to thank him for the great service he has\r\ndone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMaryann stalked off towards the rick and met Oak at the foot of the\r\nladder. She delivered her message.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is your master the farmer?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Gabriel, kindling with the\r\nidea of getting employment that seemed to strike him now.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tisn\xe2\x80\x99t a master; \xe2\x80\x99tis a mistress, shepherd.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA woman farmer?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, \xe2\x80\x99a b\xe2\x80\x99lieve, and a rich one too!\xe2\x80\x9d said a bystander. \xe2\x80\x9cLately \xe2\x80\x99a came\r\nhere from a distance. Took on her uncle\xe2\x80\x99s farm, who died suddenly. Used\r\nto measure his money in half-pint cups. They say now that she\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nbusiness in every bank in Casterbridge, and thinks no more of playing\r\npitch-and-toss sovereign than you and I do pitch-halfpenny\xe2\x80\x94not a bit in\r\nthe world, shepherd.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s she, back there upon the pony,\xe2\x80\x9d said Maryann; \xe2\x80\x9cwi\xe2\x80\x99 her face\r\na-covered up in that black cloth with holes in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOak, his features smudged, grimy, and undiscoverable from the smoke and\r\nheat, his smock-frock burnt into holes and dripping with water, the ash\r\nstem of his sheep-crook charred six inches shorter, advanced with the\r\nhumility stern adversity had thrust upon him up to the slight female\r\nform in the saddle. He lifted his hat with respect, and not without\r\ngallantry: stepping close to her hanging feet he said in a hesitating\r\nvoice,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you happen to want a shepherd, ma\xe2\x80\x99am?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe lifted the wool veil tied round her face, and looked all\r\nastonishment. Gabriel and his cold-hearted darling, Bathsheba Everdene,\r\nwere face to face.\r\n\r\nBathsheba did not speak, and he mechanically repeated in an abashed and\r\nsad voice,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you want a shepherd, ma\xe2\x80\x99am?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII\r\nRECOGNITION\xe2\x80\x94A TIMID GIRL\r\n\r\n\r\nBathsheba withdrew into the shade. She scarcely knew whether most to be\r\namused at the singularity of the meeting, or to be concerned at its\r\nawkwardness. There was room for a little pity, also for a very little\r\nexultation: the former at his position, the latter at her own.\r\nEmbarrassed she was not, and she remembered Gabriel\xe2\x80\x99s declaration of\r\nlove to her at Norcombe only to think she had nearly forgotten it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured, putting on an air of dignity, and turning again to\r\nhim with a little warmth of cheek; \xe2\x80\x9cI do want a shepherd. But\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s the very man, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the villagers, quietly.\r\n\r\nConviction breeds conviction. \xe2\x80\x9cAy, that \xe2\x80\x99a is,\xe2\x80\x9d said a second,\r\ndecisively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe man, truly!\xe2\x80\x9d said a third, with heartiness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s all there!\xe2\x80\x9d said number four, fervidly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen will you tell him to speak to the bailiff?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba.\r\n\r\nAll was practical again now. A summer eve and loneliness would have\r\nbeen necessary to give the meeting its proper fulness of romance.\r\n\r\nThe bailiff was pointed out to Gabriel, who, checking the palpitation\r\nwithin his breast at discovering that this Ashtoreth of strange report\r\nwas only a modification of Venus the well-known and admired, retired\r\nwith him to talk over the necessary preliminaries of hiring.\r\n\r\nThe fire before them wasted away. \xe2\x80\x9cMen,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, \xe2\x80\x9cyou shall\r\ntake a little refreshment after this extra work. Will you come to the\r\nhouse?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe could knock in a bit and a drop a good deal freer, Miss, if so be\r\nye\xe2\x80\x99d send it to Warren\xe2\x80\x99s Malthouse,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the spokesman.\r\n\r\nBathsheba then rode off into the darkness, and the men straggled on to\r\nthe village in twos and threes\xe2\x80\x94Oak and the bailiff being left by the\r\nrick alone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now,\xe2\x80\x9d said the bailiff, finally, \xe2\x80\x9call is settled, I think, about\r\nyour coming, and I am going home-along. Good-night to ye, shepherd.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you get me a lodging?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Gabriel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat I can\xe2\x80\x99t, indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, moving past Oak as a Christian edges\r\npast an offertory-plate when he does not mean to contribute. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you\r\nfollow on the road till you come to Warren\xe2\x80\x99s Malthouse, where they are\r\nall gone to have their snap of victuals, I daresay some of \xe2\x80\x99em will\r\ntell you of a place. Good-night to ye, shepherd.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe bailiff who showed this nervous dread of loving his neighbour as\r\nhimself, went up the hill, and Oak walked on to the village, still\r\nastonished at the reencounter with Bathsheba, glad of his nearness to\r\nher, and perplexed at the rapidity with which the unpractised girl of\r\nNorcombe had developed into the supervising and cool woman here. But\r\nsome women only require an emergency to make them fit for one.\r\n\r\nObliged, to some extent, to forgo dreaming in order to find the way, he\r\nreached the churchyard, and passed round it under the wall where\r\nseveral ancient trees grew. There was a wide margin of grass along\r\nhere, and Gabriel\xe2\x80\x99s footsteps were deadened by its softness, even at\r\nthis indurating period of the year. When abreast of a trunk which\r\nappeared to be the oldest of the old, he became aware that a figure was\r\nstanding behind it. Gabriel did not pause in his walk, and in another\r\nmoment he accidentally kicked a loose stone. The noise was enough to\r\ndisturb the motionless stranger, who started and assumed a careless\r\nposition.\r\n\r\nIt was a slim girl, rather thinly clad.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-night to you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel, heartily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-night,\xe2\x80\x9d said the girl to Gabriel.\r\n\r\nThe voice was unexpectedly attractive; it was the low and dulcet note\r\nsuggestive of romance; common in descriptions, rare in experience.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll thank you to tell me if I\xe2\x80\x99m in the way for Warren\xe2\x80\x99s Malthouse?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nGabriel resumed, primarily to gain the information, indirectly to get\r\nmore of the music.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite right. It\xe2\x80\x99s at the bottom of the hill. And do you know\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d The\r\ngirl hesitated and then went on again. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you know how late they keep\r\nopen the Buck\xe2\x80\x99s Head Inn?\xe2\x80\x9d She seemed to be won by Gabriel\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nheartiness, as Gabriel had been won by her modulations.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know where the Buck\xe2\x80\x99s Head is, or anything about it. Do you\r\nthink of going there to-night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d The woman again paused. There was no necessity for any\r\ncontinuance of speech, and the fact that she did add more seemed to\r\nproceed from an unconscious desire to show unconcern by making a\r\nremark, which is noticeable in the ingenuous when they are acting by\r\nstealth. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are not a Weatherbury man?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, timorously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not. I am the new shepherd\xe2\x80\x94just arrived.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly a shepherd\xe2\x80\x94and you seem almost a farmer by your ways.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly a shepherd,\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel repeated, in a dull cadence of finality. His\r\nthoughts were directed to the past, his eyes to the feet of the girl;\r\nand for the first time he saw lying there a bundle of some sort. She\r\nmay have perceived the direction of his face, for she said coaxingly,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou won\xe2\x80\x99t say anything in the parish about having seen me here, will\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94at least, not for a day or two?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t if you wish me not to,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d the other replied. \xe2\x80\x9cI am rather poor, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwant people to know anything about me.\xe2\x80\x9d Then she was silent and\r\nshivered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ought to have a cloak on such a cold night,\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel observed. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwould advise \xe2\x80\x99ee to get indoors.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO no! Would you mind going on and leaving me? I thank you much for\r\nwhat you have told me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will go on,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; adding hesitatingly,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cSince you are not very\r\nwell off, perhaps you would accept this trifle from me. It is only a\r\nshilling, but it is all I have to spare.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I will take it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the stranger gratefully.\r\n\r\nShe extended her hand; Gabriel his. In feeling for each other\xe2\x80\x99s palm in\r\nthe gloom before the money could be passed, a minute incident occurred\r\nwhich told much. Gabriel\xe2\x80\x99s fingers alighted on the young woman\xe2\x80\x99s wrist.\r\nIt was beating with a throb of tragic intensity. He had frequently felt\r\nthe same quick, hard beat in the femoral artery of his lambs when\r\noverdriven. It suggested a consumption too great of a vitality which,\r\nto judge from her figure and stature, was already too little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut there is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, no! Let your having seen me be a secret!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well; I will. Good-night, again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young girl remained motionless by the tree, and Gabriel descended\r\ninto the village of Weatherbury, or Lower Longpuddle as it was\r\nsometimes called. He fancied that he had felt himself in the penumbra\r\nof a very deep sadness when touching that slight and fragile creature.\r\nBut wisdom lies in moderating mere impressions, and Gabriel endeavoured\r\nto think little of this.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII\r\nTHE MALTHOUSE\xe2\x80\x94THE CHAT\xe2\x80\x94NEWS\r\n\r\n\r\nWarren\xe2\x80\x99s Malthouse was enclosed by an old wall inwrapped with ivy, and\r\nthough not much of the exterior was visible at this hour, the character\r\nand purposes of the building were clearly enough shown by its outline\r\nupon the sky. From the walls an overhanging thatched roof sloped up to\r\na point in the centre, upon which rose a small wooden lantern, fitted\r\nwith louvre-boards on all the four sides, and from these openings a\r\nmist was dimly perceived to be escaping into the night air. There was\r\nno window in front; but a square hole in the door was glazed with a\r\nsingle pane, through which red, comfortable rays now stretched out upon\r\nthe ivied wall in front. Voices were to be heard inside.\r\n\r\nOak\xe2\x80\x99s hand skimmed the surface of the door with fingers extended to an\r\nElymas-the-Sorcerer pattern, till he found a leathern strap, which he\r\npulled. This lifted a wooden latch, and the door swung open.\r\n\r\nThe room inside was lighted only by the ruddy glow from the kiln mouth,\r\nwhich shone over the floor with the streaming horizontality of the\r\nsetting sun, and threw upwards the shadows of all facial irregularities\r\nin those assembled around. The stone-flag floor was worn into a path\r\nfrom the doorway to the kiln, and into undulations everywhere. A curved\r\nsettle of unplaned oak stretched along one side, and in a remote corner\r\nwas a small bed and bedstead, the owner and frequent occupier of which\r\nwas the maltster.\r\n\r\nThis aged man was now sitting opposite the fire, his frosty white hair\r\nand beard overgrowing his gnarled figure like the grey moss and lichen\r\nupon a leafless apple-tree. He wore breeches and the laced-up shoes\r\ncalled ankle-jacks; he kept his eyes fixed upon the fire.\r\n\r\nGabriel\xe2\x80\x99s nose was greeted by an atmosphere laden with the sweet smell\r\nof new malt. The conversation (which seemed to have been concerning the\r\norigin of the fire) immediately ceased, and every one ocularly\r\ncriticised him to the degree expressed by contracting the flesh of\r\ntheir foreheads and looking at him with narrowed eyelids, as if he had\r\nbeen a light too strong for their sight. Several exclaimed\r\nmeditatively, after this operation had been completed:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, \xe2\x80\x99tis the new shepherd, \xe2\x80\x99a b\xe2\x80\x99lieve.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe thought we heard a hand pawing about the door for the bobbin, but\r\nweren\xe2\x80\x99t sure \xe2\x80\x99twere not a dead leaf blowed across,\xe2\x80\x9d said another. \xe2\x80\x9cCome\r\nin, shepherd; sure ye be welcome, though we don\xe2\x80\x99t know yer name.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGabriel Oak, that\xe2\x80\x99s my name, neighbours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe ancient maltster sitting in the midst turned at this\xe2\x80\x94his turning\r\nbeing as the turning of a rusty crane.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s never Gable Oak\xe2\x80\x99s grandson over at Norcombe\xe2\x80\x94never!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as\r\na formula expressive of surprise, which nobody was supposed for a\r\nmoment to take literally.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy father and my grandfather were old men of the name of Gabriel,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the shepherd, placidly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThought I knowed the man\xe2\x80\x99s face as I seed him on the rick!\xe2\x80\x94thought I\r\ndid! And where be ye trading o\xe2\x80\x99t to now, shepherd?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m thinking of biding here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Oak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKnowed yer grandfather for years and years!\xe2\x80\x9d continued the maltster,\r\nthe words coming forth of their own accord as if the momentum\r\npreviously imparted had been sufficient.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94and did you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKnowed yer grandmother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd her too!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLikewise knowed yer father when he was a child. Why, my boy Jacob\r\nthere and your father were sworn brothers\xe2\x80\x94that they were sure\xe2\x80\x94weren\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nye, Jacob?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, sure,\xe2\x80\x9d said his son, a young man about sixty-five, with a\r\nsemi-bald head and one tooth in the left centre of his upper jaw, which\r\nmade much of itself by standing prominent, like a milestone in a bank.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut \xe2\x80\x99twas Joe had most to do with him. However, my son William must\r\nhave knowed the very man afore us\xe2\x80\x94didn\xe2\x80\x99t ye, Billy, afore ye left\r\nNorcombe?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, \xe2\x80\x99twas Andrew,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jacob\xe2\x80\x99s son Billy, a child of forty, or\r\nthereabouts, who manifested the peculiarity of possessing a cheerful\r\nsoul in a gloomy body, and whose whiskers were assuming a chinchilla\r\nshade here and there.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can mind Andrew,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak, \xe2\x80\x9cas being a man in the place when I was\r\nquite a child.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy\xe2\x80\x94the other day I and my youngest daughter, Liddy, were over at my\r\ngrandson\xe2\x80\x99s christening,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Billy. \xe2\x80\x9cWe were talking about this\r\nvery family, and \xe2\x80\x99twas only last Purification Day in this very world,\r\nwhen the use-money is gied away to the second-best poor folk, you know,\r\nshepherd, and I can mind the day because they all had to traypse up to\r\nthe vestry\xe2\x80\x94yes, this very man\xe2\x80\x99s family.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, shepherd, and drink. \xe2\x80\x99Tis gape and swaller with us\xe2\x80\x94a drap of\r\nsommit, but not of much account,\xe2\x80\x9d said the maltster, removing from the\r\nfire his eyes, which were vermilion-red and bleared by gazing into it\r\nfor so many years. \xe2\x80\x9cTake up the God-forgive-me, Jacob. See if \xe2\x80\x99tis\r\nwarm, Jacob.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJacob stooped to the God-forgive-me, which was a two-handled tall mug\r\nstanding in the ashes, cracked and charred with heat: it was rather\r\nfurred with extraneous matter about the outside, especially in the\r\ncrevices of the handles, the innermost curves of which may not have\r\nseen daylight for several years by reason of this encrustation\r\nthereon\xe2\x80\x94formed of ashes accidentally wetted with cider and baked hard;\r\nbut to the mind of any sensible drinker the cup was no worse for that,\r\nbeing incontestably clean on the inside and about the rim. It may be\r\nobserved that such a class of mug is called a God-forgive-me in\r\nWeatherbury and its vicinity for uncertain reasons; probably because\r\nits size makes any given toper feel ashamed of himself when he sees its\r\nbottom in drinking it empty.\r\n\r\nJacob, on receiving the order to see if the liquor was warm enough,\r\nplacidly dipped his forefinger into it by way of thermometer, and\r\nhaving pronounced it nearly of the proper degree, raised the cup and\r\nvery civilly attempted to dust some of the ashes from the bottom with\r\nthe skirt of his smock-frock, because Shepherd Oak was a stranger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA clane cup for the shepherd,\xe2\x80\x9d said the maltster commandingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94not at all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel, in a reproving tone of considerateness.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never fuss about dirt in its pure state, and when I know what sort\r\nit is.\xe2\x80\x9d Taking the mug he drank an inch or more from the depth of its\r\ncontents, and duly passed it to the next man. \xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t think of\r\ngiving such trouble to neighbours in washing up when there\xe2\x80\x99s so much\r\nwork to be done in the world already,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Oak in a moister tone,\r\nafter recovering from the stoppage of breath which is occasioned by\r\npulls at large mugs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA right sensible man,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jacob.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue, true; it can\xe2\x80\x99t be gainsaid!\xe2\x80\x9d observed a brisk young man\xe2\x80\x94Mark\r\nClark by name, a genial and pleasant gentleman, whom to meet anywhere\r\nin your travels was to know, to know was to drink with, and to drink\r\nwith was, unfortunately, to pay for.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd here\xe2\x80\x99s a mouthful of bread and bacon that mis\xe2\x80\x99ess have sent,\r\nshepherd. The cider will go down better with a bit of victuals. Don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nye chaw quite close, shepherd, for I let the bacon fall in the road\r\noutside as I was bringing it along, and may be \xe2\x80\x99tis rather gritty.\r\nThere, \xe2\x80\x99tis clane dirt; and we all know what that is, as you say, and\r\nyou bain\xe2\x80\x99t a particular man we see, shepherd.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue, true\xe2\x80\x94not at all,\xe2\x80\x9d said the friendly Oak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t let your teeth quite meet, and you won\xe2\x80\x99t feel the sandiness at\r\nall. believe that man would as soon\r\ntell a lie Sundays as working-days\xe2\x80\x94that I do so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood faith, you do talk!\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue enough,\xe2\x80\x9d said the man of bitter moods, looking round upon the\r\ncompany with the antithetic laughter that comes from a keener\r\nappreciation of the miseries of life than ordinary men are capable of.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, there\xe2\x80\x99s people of one sort, and people of another, but that\r\nman\xe2\x80\x94bless your souls!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGabriel thought fit to change the subject. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must be a very aged\r\nman, malter, to have sons growed mild and ancient,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFather\xe2\x80\x99s so old that \xe2\x80\x99a can\xe2\x80\x99t mind his age, can ye, father?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ninterposed Jacob. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd he\xe2\x80\x99s growed terrible crooked too, lately,\xe2\x80\x9d Jacob\r\ncontinued, surveying his father\xe2\x80\x99s figure, which was rather more bowed\r\nthan his own. \xe2\x80\x9cReally one may say that father there is three-double.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCrooked folk will last a long while,\xe2\x80\x9d said the maltster, grimly, and\r\nnot in the best humour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShepherd would like to hear the pedigree of yer life, father\xe2\x80\x94wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nye, shepherd?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, that I should,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel with the heartiness of a man who had\r\nlonged to hear it for several months. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat may your age be, malter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe maltster cleared his throat in an exaggerated form for emphasis,\r\nand elongating his gaze to the remotest point of the ashpit, said, in\r\nthe slow speech justifiable when the importance of a subject is so\r\ngenerally felt that any mannerism must be tolerated in getting at it,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind the year I were born in, but perhaps I can reckon\r\nup the places I\xe2\x80\x99ve lived at, and so get it that way. I bode at Upper\r\nLongpuddle across there\xe2\x80\x9d (nodding to the north) \xe2\x80\x9ctill I were eleven. I\r\nbode seven at Kingsbere\xe2\x80\x9d (nodding to the east) \xe2\x80\x9cwhere I took to\r\nmalting. I went therefrom to Norcombe, and malted there two-and-twenty\r\nyears, and-two-and-twenty years I was there turnip-hoeing and\r\nharvesting. Ah, I knowed that old place, Norcombe, years afore you were\r\nthought of, Master Oak\xe2\x80\x9d (Oak smiled sincere belief in the fact). \xe2\x80\x9cThen\r\nI malted at Durnover four year, and four year turnip-hoeing; and I was\r\nfourteen times eleven months at Millpond St. Jude\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x9d (nodding\r\nnorth-west-by-north). \xe2\x80\x9cOld Twills wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t hire me for more than eleven\r\nmonths at a time, to keep me from being chargeable to the parish if so\r\nbe I was disabled. Then I was three year at Mellstock, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve been\r\nhere one-and-thirty year come Candlemas. How much is that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHundred and seventeen,\xe2\x80\x9d chuckled another old gentleman, given to\r\nmental arithmetic and little conversation, who had hitherto sat\r\nunobserved in a corner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, that\xe2\x80\x99s my age,\xe2\x80\x9d said the maltster, emphatically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO no, father!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jacob. \xe2\x80\x9cYour turnip-hoeing were in the summer and\r\nyour malting in the winter of the same years, and ye don\xe2\x80\x99t ought to\r\ncount-both halves, father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChok\xe2\x80\x99 it all! I lived through the summers, didn\xe2\x80\x99t I? That\xe2\x80\x99s my\r\nquestion. I suppose ye\xe2\x80\x99ll say next I be no age at all to speak of?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure we shan\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel, soothingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe be a very old aged person, malter,\xe2\x80\x9d attested Jan Coggan, also\r\nsoothingly. \xe2\x80\x9cWe all know that, and ye must have a wonderful talented\r\nconstitution to be able to live so long, mustn\xe2\x80\x99t he, neighbours?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue, true; ye must, malter, wonderful,\xe2\x80\x9d said the meeting unanimously.\r\n\r\nThe maltster, being now pacified, was even generous enough to\r\nvoluntarily disparage in a slight degree the virtue of having lived a\r\ngreat many years, by mentioning that the cup they were drinking out of\r\nwas three years older than he.\r\n\r\nWhile the cup was being examined, the end of Gabriel Oak\xe2\x80\x99s flute became\r\nvisible over his smock-frock pocket, and Henery Fray exclaimed,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely, shepherd, I seed you blowing into a great flute by now at\r\nCasterbridge?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou did,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel, blushing faintly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve been in great trouble,\r\nneighbours, and was driven to it. I used not to be so poor as I be\r\nnow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind, heart!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mark Clark. \xe2\x80\x9cYou should take it\r\ncareless-like, shepherd, and your time will come. But we could thank ye\r\nfor a tune, if ye bain\xe2\x80\x99t too tired?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeither drum nor trumpet have I heard since Christmas,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jan\r\nCoggan. \xe2\x80\x9cCome, raise a tune, Master Oak!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, that I will,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel, pulling out his flute and putting it\r\ntogether. \xe2\x80\x9cA poor tool, neighbours; but such as I can do ye shall have\r\nand welcome.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOak then struck up \xe2\x80\x9cJockey to the Fair,\xe2\x80\x9d and played that sparkling\r\nmelody three times through, accenting the notes in the third round in a\r\nmost artistic and lively manner by bending his body in small jerks and\r\ntapping with his foot to beat time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe can blow the flute very well\xe2\x80\x94that \xe2\x80\x99a can,\xe2\x80\x9d said a young married\r\nman, who having no individuality worth mentioning was known as \xe2\x80\x9cSusan\r\nTall\xe2\x80\x99s husband.\xe2\x80\x9d He continued, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d as lief as not be able to blow into\r\na flute as well as that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a clever man, and \xe2\x80\x99tis a true comfort for us to have such a\r\nshepherd,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Joseph Poorgrass, in a soft cadence. \xe2\x80\x9cWe ought to\r\nfeel full o\xe2\x80\x99 thanksgiving that he\xe2\x80\x99s not a player of ba\xe2\x80\x99dy songs instead\r\nof these merry tunes; for \xe2\x80\x99twould have been just as easy for God to\r\nhave made the shepherd a loose low man\xe2\x80\x94a man of iniquity, so to speak\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94as what he is. Yes, for our wives\xe2\x80\x99 and daughters\xe2\x80\x99 sakes we should\r\nfeel real thanksgiving.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue, true,\xe2\x80\x94real thanksgiving!\xe2\x80\x9d dashed in Mark Clark conclusively, not\r\nfeeling it to be of any consequence to his opinion that he had only\r\nheard about a word and three-quarters of what Joseph had said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d added Joseph, beginning to feel like a man in the Bible; \xe2\x80\x9cfor\r\nevil do thrive so in these times that ye may be as much deceived in the\r\ncleanest shaved and whitest shirted man as in the raggedest tramp upon\r\nthe turnpike, if I may term it so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, I can mind yer face now, shepherd,\xe2\x80\x9d said Henery Fray, criticising\r\nGabriel with misty eyes as he entered upon his second tune. \xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94now I\r\nsee \xe2\x80\x99ee blowing into the flute I know \xe2\x80\x99ee to be the same man I see play\r\nat Casterbridge, for yer mouth were scrimped up and yer eyes a-staring\r\nout like a strangled man\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94just as they be now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis a pity that playing the flute should make a man look such a\r\nscarecrow,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Mr. Mark Clark, with additional criticism of\r\nGabriel\xe2\x80\x99s countenance, the latter person jerking out, with the ghastly\r\ngrimace required by the instrument, the chorus of \xe2\x80\x9cDame Durden:\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x99Twas Moll\xe2\x80\x99 and Bet\xe2\x80\x99, and Doll\xe2\x80\x99 and Kate\xe2\x80\x99,\r\nAnd Dor\xe2\x80\x99-othy Drag\xe2\x80\x99-gle Tail\xe2\x80\x99.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope you don\xe2\x80\x99t mind that young man\xe2\x80\x99s bad manners in naming your\r\nfeatures?\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Joseph to Gabriel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Oak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor by nature ye be a very handsome man, shepherd,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Joseph\r\nPoorgrass, with winning sauvity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, that ye be, shepard,\xe2\x80\x9d said the company.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you very much,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak, in the modest tone good manners\r\ndemanded, thinking, however, that he would never let Bathsheba see him\r\nplaying the flute; in this resolve showing a discretion equal to that\r\nrelated to its sagacious inventress, the divine Minerva herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, when I and my wife were married at Norcombe Church,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old\r\nmaltster, not pleased at finding himself left out of the subject, \xe2\x80\x9cwe\r\nwere called the handsomest couple in the neighbourhood\xe2\x80\x94everybody said\r\nso.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDanged if ye bain\xe2\x80\x99t altered now, malter,\xe2\x80\x9d said a voice with the vigour\r\nnatural to the enunciation of a remarkably evident truism. It came from\r\nthe old man in the background, whose offensiveness and spiteful ways\r\nwere barely atoned for by the occasional chuckle he contributed to\r\ngeneral laughs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO no, no,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t ye play no more shepherd\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan Tall\xe2\x80\x99s husband, the young\r\nmarried man who had spoken once before. \xe2\x80\x9cI must be moving and when\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s tunes going on I seem as if hung in wires. If I thought after\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99d left that music was still playing, and I not there, I should be\r\nquite melancholy-like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s yer hurry then, Laban?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Coggan. \xe2\x80\x9cYou used to bide as\r\nlate as the latest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, ye see, neighbours, I was lately married to a woman, and she\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmy vocation now, and so ye see\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d The young man halted lamely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNew Lords new laws, as the saying is, I suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Coggan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, \xe2\x80\x99a b\xe2\x80\x99lieve\xe2\x80\x94ha, ha!\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan Tall\xe2\x80\x99s husband, in a tone intended\r\nto imply his habitual reception of jokes without minding them at all.\r\nThe young man then wished them good-night and withdrew.\r\n\r\nHenery Fray was the first to follow. Then Gabriel arose and went off\r\nwith Jan Coggan, who had offered him a lodging. A few minutes later,\r\nwhen the remaining ones were on their legs and about to depart, Fray\r\ncame back again in a hurry. Flourishing his finger ominously he threw a\r\ngaze teeming with tidings just where his eye alighted by accident,\r\nwhich happened to be in Joseph Poorgrass\xe2\x80\x99s face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO\xe2\x80\x94what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter, what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter, Henery?\xe2\x80\x9d said Joseph, starting\r\nback.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s a-brewing, Henery?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Jacob and Mark Clark.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBaily Pennyways\xe2\x80\x94Baily Pennyways\xe2\x80\x94I said so; yes, I said so!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, found out stealing anything?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStealing it is. The news is, that after Miss Everdene got home she\r\nwent out again to see all was safe, as she usually do, and coming in\r\nfound Baily Pennyways creeping down the granary steps with half a\r\nbushel of barley. She fleed at him like a cat\xe2\x80\x94never such a tomboy as\r\nshe is\xe2\x80\x94of course I speak with closed doors?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou do\xe2\x80\x94you do, Henery.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe fleed at him, and, to cut a long story short, he owned to having\r\ncarried off five sack altogether, upon her promising not to persecute\r\nhim. Well, he\xe2\x80\x99s turned out neck and crop, and my question is, who\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngoing to be baily now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe question was such a profound one that Henery was obliged to drink\r\nthere and then from the large cup till the bottom was distinctly\r\nvisible inside. Before he had replaced it on the table, in came the\r\nyoung man, Susan Tall\xe2\x80\x99s husband, in a still greater hurry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave ye heard the news that\xe2\x80\x99s all over parish?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout Baily Pennyways?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut besides that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94not a morsel of it!\xe2\x80\x9d they replied, looking into the very midst of\r\nLaban Tall as if to meet his words half-way down his throat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a night of horrors!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Joseph Poorgrass, waving his hands\r\nspasmodically. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve had the news-bell ringing in my left ear quite bad\r\nenough for a murder, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen a magpie all alone!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFanny Robin\xe2\x80\x94Miss Everdene\xe2\x80\x99s youngest servant\xe2\x80\x94can\xe2\x80\x99t be found. They\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nbeen wanting to lock up the door these two hours, but she isn\xe2\x80\x99t come\r\nin. And they don\xe2\x80\x99t know what to do about going to bed for fear of\r\nlocking her out. They wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be so concerned if she hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been\r\nnoticed in such low spirits these last few days, and Maryann d\xe2\x80\x99 think\r\nthe beginning of a crowner\xe2\x80\x99s inquest has happened to the poor girl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99tis burned\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99tis burned!\xe2\x80\x9d came from Joseph Poorgrass\xe2\x80\x99s dry lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99tis drowned!\xe2\x80\x9d said Tall.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr \xe2\x80\x99tis her father\xe2\x80\x99s razor!\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Billy Smallbury, with a vivid\r\nsense of detail.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94Miss Everdene wants to speak to one or two of us before we go to\r\nbed. What with this trouble about the baily, and now about the girl,\r\nmis\xe2\x80\x99ess is almost wild.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey all hastened up the lane to the farmhouse, excepting the old\r\nmaltster, whom neither news, fire, rain, nor thunder could draw from\r\nhis hole. There, as the others\xe2\x80\x99 footsteps died away he sat down again\r\nand continued gazing as usual into the furnace with his red, bleared\r\neyes.\r\n\r\nFrom the bedroom window above their heads Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s head and\r\nshoulders, robed in mystic white, were dimly seen extended into the\r\nair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre any of my men among you?\xe2\x80\x9d she said anxiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, several,\xe2\x80\x9d said Susan Tall\xe2\x80\x99s husband.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo-morrow morning I wish two or three of you to make inquiries in the\r\nvillages round if they have seen such a person as Fanny Robin. Do it\r\nquietly; there is no reason for alarm as yet. She must have left whilst\r\nwe were all at the fire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg yer pardon, but had she any young man courting her in the\r\nparish, ma\xe2\x80\x99am?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Jacob Smallbury.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve never heard of any such thing, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x9d said two or three.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is hardly likely, either,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Bathsheba. \xe2\x80\x9cFor any lover of\r\nhers might have come to the house if he had been a respectable lad. The\r\nmost mysterious matter connected with her absence\xe2\x80\x94indeed, the only\r\nthing which gives me serious alarm\xe2\x80\x94is that she was seen to go out of\r\nthe house by Maryann with only her indoor working gown on\xe2\x80\x94not even a\r\nbonnet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you mean, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, excusing my words, that a young woman would\r\nhardly go to see her young man without dressing up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jacob,\r\nturning his mental vision upon past experiences. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s true\xe2\x80\x94she would\r\nnot, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe had, I think, a bundle, though I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see very well,\xe2\x80\x9d said a\r\nfemale voice from another window, which seemed that of Maryann. \xe2\x80\x9cBut\r\nshe had no young man about here. Hers lives in Casterbridge, and I\r\nbelieve he\xe2\x80\x99s a soldier.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know his name?\xe2\x80\x9d Bathsheba said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, mistress; she was very close about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps I might be able to find out if I went to Casterbridge\r\nbarracks,\xe2\x80\x9d said William Smallbury.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well; if she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t return to-morrow, mind you go there and try\r\nto discover which man it is, and see him. I feel more responsible than\r\nI should if she had had any friends or relations alive. I do hope she\r\nhas come to no harm through a man of that kind.... And then there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthis disgraceful affair of the bailiff\xe2\x80\x94but I can\xe2\x80\x99t speak of him now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba had so many reasons for uneasiness that it seemed she did not\r\nthink it worth while to dwell upon any particular one. \xe2\x80\x9cDo as I told\r\nyou, then,\xe2\x80\x9d she said in conclusion, closing the casement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, ay, mistress; we will,\xe2\x80\x9d they replied, and moved away.\r\n\r\nThat night at Coggan\xe2\x80\x99s, Gabriel Oak, beneath the screen of closed\r\neyelids, was busy with fancies, and full of movement, like a river\r\nflowing rapidly under its ice. Night had always been the time at which\r\nhe saw Bathsheba most vividly, and through the slow hours of shadow he\r\ntenderly regarded her image now. It is rarely that the pleasures of the\r\nimagination will compensate for the pain of sleeplessness, but they\r\npossibly did with Oak to-night, for the delight of merely seeing her\r\neffaced for the time his perception of the great difference between\r\nseeing and possessing.\r\n\r\nHe also thought of plans for fetching his few utensils and books from\r\nNorcombe. _The Young Man\xe2\x80\x99s Best Companion_, _The Farrier\xe2\x80\x99s Sure Guide_,\r\n_The Veterinary Surgeon_, _Paradise Lost_, _The Pilgrim\xe2\x80\x99s Progress_,\r\n_Robinson Crusoe_, Ash\xe2\x80\x99s _Dictionary_, and Walkingame\xe2\x80\x99s _Arithmetic_,\r\nconstituted his library; and though a limited series, it was one from\r\nwhich he had acquired more sound information by diligent perusal than\r\nmany a man of opportunities has done from a furlong of laden shelves.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX\r\nTHE HOMESTEAD\xe2\x80\x94A VISITOR\xe2\x80\x94HALF-CONFIDENCES\r\n\r\n\r\nBy daylight, the bower of Oak\xe2\x80\x99s new-found mistress, Bathsheba Everdene,\r\npresented itself as a hoary building, of the early stage of Classic\r\nRenaissance as regards its architecture, and of a proportion which told\r\nat a glance that, as is so frequently the case, it had once been the\r\nmemorial hall upon a small estate around it, now altogether effaced as\r\na distinct property, and merged in the vast tract of a non-resident\r\nlandlord, which comprised several such modest demesnes.\r\n\r\nFluted pilasters, worked from the solid stone, decorated its front, and\r\nabove the roof the chimneys were panelled or columnar, some coped\r\ngables with finials and like features still retaining traces of their\r\nGothic extraction. Soft brown mosses, like faded velveteen, formed\r\ncushions upon the stone tiling, and tufts of the houseleek or sengreen\r\nsprouted from the eaves of the low surrounding buildings. A gravel walk\r\nleading from the door to the road in front was encrusted at the sides\r\nwith more moss\xe2\x80\x94here it was a silver-green variety, the nut-brown of the\r\ngravel being visible to the width of only a foot or two in the centre.\r\nThis circumstance, and the generally sleepy air of the whole prospect\r\nhere, together with the animated and contrasting state of the reverse\r\nfa\xc3\xa7ade, suggested to the imagination that on the adaptation of the\r\nbuilding for farming purposes the vital principle of the house had\r\nturned round inside its body to face the other way. Reversals of this\r\nkind, strange deformities, tremendous paralyses, are often seen to be\r\ninflicted by trade upon edifices\xe2\x80\x94either individual or in the aggregate\r\nas streets and towns\xe2\x80\x94which were originally planned for pleasure alone.\r\n\r\nLively voices were heard this morning in the upper rooms, the main\r\nstaircase to which was of hard oak, the balusters, heavy as bed-posts,\r\nbeing turned and moulded in the quaint fashion of their century, the\r\nhandrail as stout as a parapet-top, and the stairs themselves\r\ncontinually twisting round like a person trying to look over his\r\nshoulder. Going up, the floors above were found to have a very\r\nirregular surface, rising to ridges, sinking into valleys; and being\r\njust then uncarpeted, the face of the boards was seen to be eaten into\r\ninnumerable vermiculations. Every window replied by a clang to the\r\nopening and shutting of every door, a tremble followed every bustling\r\nmovement, and a creak accompanied a walker about the house, like a\r\nspirit, wherever he went.\r\n\r\nIn the room from which the conversation proceeded Bathsheba and her\r\nservant-companion, Liddy Smallbury, were to be discovered sitting upon\r\nthe floor, and sorting a complication of papers, books, bottles, and\r\nrubbish spread out thereon\xe2\x80\x94remnants from the household stores of the\r\nlate occupier. Liddy, the maltster\xe2\x80\x99s great-granddaughter, was about\r\nBathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s equal in age, and her face was a prominent advertisement of\r\nthe light-hearted English country girl. The beauty her features might\r\nhave lacked in form was amply made up for by perfection of hue, which\r\nat this winter-time was the softened ruddiness on a surface of high\r\nrotundity that we meet with in a Terburg or a Gerard Douw; and, like\r\nthe presentations of those great colourists, it was a face which kept\r\nwell back from the boundary between comeliness and the ideal. Though\r\nelastic in nature she was less daring than Bathsheba, and occasionally\r\nshowed some earnestness, which consisted half of genuine feeling, and\r\nhalf of mannerliness superadded by way of duty.\r\n\r\nThrough a partly-opened door the noise of a scrubbing-brush led up to\r\nthe charwoman, Maryann Money, a person who for a face had a circular\r\ndisc, furrowed less by age than by long gazes of perplexity at distant\r\nobjects. To think of her was to get good-humoured; to speak of her was\r\nto raise the image of a dried Normandy pippin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop your scrubbing a moment,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba through the door to her.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hear something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMaryann suspended the brush.\r\n\r\nThe tramp of a horse was apparent, approaching the front of the\r\nbuilding. The paces slackened, turned in at the wicket, and, what was\r\nmost unusual, came up the mossy path close to the door. The door was\r\ntapped with the end of a crop or stick.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat impertinence!\xe2\x80\x9d said Liddy, in a low voice. \xe2\x80\x9cTo ride up the\r\nfootpath like that! Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t he stop at the gate? Lord! \xe2\x80\x99Tis a\r\ngentleman! I see the top of his hat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe quiet!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba.\r\n\r\nThe further expression of Liddy\xe2\x80\x99s concern was continued by aspect\r\ninstead of narrative.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy doesn\xe2\x80\x99t Mrs. Coggan go to the door?\xe2\x80\x9d Bathsheba continued.\r\n\r\nRat-tat-tat-tat resounded more decisively from Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s oak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaryann, you go!\xe2\x80\x9d said she, fluttering under the onset of a crowd of\r\nromantic possibilities.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh ma\xe2\x80\x99am\xe2\x80\x94see, here\xe2\x80\x99s a mess!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe argument was unanswerable after a glance at Maryann.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLiddy\xe2\x80\x94you must,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba.\r\n\r\nLiddy held up her hands and arms, coated with dust from the rubbish\r\nthey were sorting, and looked imploringly at her mistress.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x94Mrs. Coggan is going!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, exhaling her relief in\r\nthe form of a long breath which had lain in her bosom a minute or more.\r\n\r\nThe door opened, and a deep voice said\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs Miss Everdene at home?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll see, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Coggan, and in a minute appeared in the\r\nroom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear, what a thirtover place this world is!\xe2\x80\x9d continued Mrs. Coggan (a\r\nwholesome-looking lady who had a voice for each class of remark\r\naccording to the emotion involved; who could toss a pancake or twirl a\r\nmop with the accuracy of pure mathematics, and who at this moment\r\nshowed hands shaggy with fragments of dough and arms encrusted with\r\nflour). \xe2\x80\x9cI am never up to my elbows, Miss, in making a pudding but one\r\nof two things do happen\xe2\x80\x94either my nose must needs begin tickling, and I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t live without scratching it, or somebody knocks at the door.\r\nHere\xe2\x80\x99s Mr. Boldwood wanting to see you, Miss Everdene.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA woman\xe2\x80\x99s dress being a part of her countenance, and any disorder in\r\nthe one being of the same nature with a malformation or wound in the\r\nother, Bathsheba said at once\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t see him in this state. Whatever shall I do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNot-at-homes were hardly naturalized in Weatherbury farmhouses, so\r\nLiddy suggested\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cSay you\xe2\x80\x99re a fright with dust, and can\xe2\x80\x99t come down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94that sounds very well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Coggan, critically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay I can\xe2\x80\x99t see him\xe2\x80\x94that will do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Coggan went downstairs, and returned the answer as requested,\r\nadding, however, on her own responsibility, \xe2\x80\x9cMiss is dusting bottles,\r\nsir, and is quite a object\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s why \xe2\x80\x99tis.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, very well,\xe2\x80\x9d said the deep voice indifferently. \xe2\x80\x9cAll I wanted to\r\nask was, if anything had been heard of Fanny Robin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing, sir\xe2\x80\x94but we may know to-night. William Smallbury is gone to\r\nCasterbridge, where her young man lives, as is supposed, and the other\r\nmen be inquiring about everywhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe horse\xe2\x80\x99s tramp then recommenced and retreated, and the door closed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is Mr. Boldwood?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA gentleman-farmer at Little Weatherbury.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarried?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, miss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow old is he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cForty, I should say\xe2\x80\x94very handsome\xe2\x80\x94rather stern-looking\xe2\x80\x94and rich.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a bother this dusting is! I am always in some unfortunate plight\r\nor other,\xe2\x80\x9d Bathsheba said, complainingly. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy should he inquire about\r\nFanny?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, because, as she had no friends in her childhood, he took her and\r\nput her to school, and got her her place here under your uncle. He\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nvery kind man that way, but Lord\xe2\x80\x94there!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever was such a hopeless man for a woman! He\xe2\x80\x99s been courted by sixes\r\nand sevens\xe2\x80\x94all the girls, gentle and simple, for miles round, have\r\ntried him. Jane Perkins worked at him for two months like a slave, and\r\nthe two Miss Taylors spent a year upon him, and he cost Farmer Ives\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndaughter nights of tears and twenty pounds\xe2\x80\x99 worth of new clothes; but\r\nLord\xe2\x80\x94the money might as well have been thrown out of the window.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA little boy came up at this moment and looked in upon them. This child\r\nwas one of the Coggans, who, with the Smallburys, were as common among\r\nthe families of this district as the Avons and Derwents among our\r\nrivers. He always had a loosened tooth or a cut finger to show to\r\nparticular friends, which he did with an air of being thereby elevated\r\nabove the common herd of afflictionless humanity\xe2\x80\x94to which exhibition\r\npeople were expected to say \xe2\x80\x9cPoor child!\xe2\x80\x9d with a dash of congratulation\r\nas well as pity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got a pen-nee!\xe2\x80\x9d said Master Coggan in a scanning measure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94who gave it you, Teddy?\xe2\x80\x9d said Liddy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMis-terr Bold-wood! He gave it to me for opening the gate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did he say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe said, \xe2\x80\x98Where are you going, my little man?\xe2\x80\x99 and I said, \xe2\x80\x98To Miss\r\nEverdene\xe2\x80\x99s please,\xe2\x80\x99 and he said, \xe2\x80\x98She is a staid woman, isn\xe2\x80\x99t she, my\r\nlittle man?\xe2\x80\x99 and I said, \xe2\x80\x98Yes.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou naughty child! What did you say that for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Cause he gave me the penny!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a pucker everything is in!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, discontentedly when\r\nthe child had gone. \xe2\x80\x9cGet away, Maryann, or go on with your scrubbing,\r\nor do something! You ought to be married by this time, and not here\r\ntroubling me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, mistress\xe2\x80\x94so I did.
