But what between the poor men I won\xe2\x80\x99t have, and\r\nthe rich men who won\xe2\x80\x99t have me, I stand as a pelican in the\r\nwilderness!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid anybody ever want to marry you miss?\xe2\x80\x9d Liddy ventured to ask when\r\nthey were again alone. \xe2\x80\x9cLots of \xe2\x80\x99em, I daresay?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba paused, as if about to refuse a reply, but the temptation to\r\nsay yes, since it was really in her power was irresistible by aspiring\r\nvirginity, in spite of her spleen at having been published as old.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA man wanted to once,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, in a highly experienced tone, and the\r\nimage of Gabriel Oak, as the farmer, rose before her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow nice it must seem!\xe2\x80\x9d said Liddy, with the fixed features of mental\r\nrealization. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe wasn\xe2\x80\x99t quite good enough for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow sweet to be able to disdain, when most of us are glad to say,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thank you!\xe2\x80\x99 I seem I hear it. \xe2\x80\x98No, sir\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m your better.\xe2\x80\x99 or \xe2\x80\x98Kiss my\r\nfoot, sir; my face is for mouths of consequence.\xe2\x80\x99 And did you love him,\r\nmiss?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no. But I rather liked him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course not\xe2\x80\x94what footsteps are those I hear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLiddy looked from a back window into the courtyard behind, which was\r\nnow getting low-toned and dim with the earliest films of night. A\r\ncrooked file of men was approaching the back door. The whole string of\r\ntrailing individuals advanced in the completest balance of intention,\r\nlike the remarkable creatures known as Chain Salp\xc3\xa6, which, distinctly\r\norganized in other respects, have one will common to a whole family.\r\nSome were, as usual, in snow-white smock-frocks of Russia duck, and\r\nsome in whitey-brown ones of drabbet\xe2\x80\x94marked on the wrists, breasts,\r\nbacks, and sleeves with honeycomb-work. Two or three women in pattens\r\nbrought up the rear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Philistines be upon us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Liddy, making her nose white against\r\nthe glass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, very well. Maryann, go down and keep them in the kitchen till I am\r\ndressed, and then show them in to me in the hall.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X\r\nMISTRESS AND MEN\r\n\r\n\r\nHalf-an-hour later Bathsheba, in finished dress, and followed by Liddy,\r\nentered the upper end of the old hall to find that her men had all\r\ndeposited themselves on a long form and a settle at the lower\r\nextremity. She sat down at a table and opened the time-book, pen in her\r\nhand, with a canvas money-bag beside her. From this she poured a small\r\nheap of coin. Liddy chose a position at her elbow and began to sew,\r\nsometimes pausing and looking round, or, with the air of a privileged\r\nperson, taking up one of the half-sovereigns lying before her and\r\nsurveying it merely as a work of art, while strictly preventing her\r\ncountenance from expressing any wish to possess it as money.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow before I begin, men,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, \xe2\x80\x9cI have two matters to speak\r\nof. The first is that the bailiff is dismissed for thieving, and that I\r\nhave formed a resolution to have no bailiff at all, but to manage\r\neverything with my own head and hands.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe men breathed an audible breath of amazement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe next matter is, have you heard anything of Fanny?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you done anything?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI met Farmer Boldwood,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jacob Smallbury, \xe2\x80\x9cand I went with him and\r\ntwo of his men, and dragged Newmill Pond, but we found nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the new shepherd have been to Buck\xe2\x80\x99s Head, by Yalbury, thinking\r\nshe had gone there, but nobody had seed her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Laban Tall.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHasn\xe2\x80\x99t William Smallbury been to Casterbridge?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, but he\xe2\x80\x99s not yet come home. He promised to be back by\r\nsix.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt wants a quarter to six at present,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, looking at her\r\nwatch. \xe2\x80\x9cI daresay he\xe2\x80\x99ll be in directly. Well, now then\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94she looked into\r\nthe book\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cJoseph Poorgrass, are you there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir\xe2\x80\x94ma\xe2\x80\x99am I mane,\xe2\x80\x9d said the person addressed. \xe2\x80\x9cI be the personal\r\nname of Poorgrass.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what are you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing in my own eye. In the eye of other people\xe2\x80\x94well, I don\xe2\x80\x99t say\r\nit; though public thought will out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you do on the farm?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do do carting things all the year, and in seed time I shoots the\r\nrooks and sparrows, and helps at pig-killing, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease nine and ninepence and a good halfpenny where \xe2\x80\x99twas a bad one,\r\nsir\xe2\x80\x94ma\xe2\x80\x99am I mane.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite correct. Now here are ten shillings in addition as a small\r\npresent, as I am a new comer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba blushed slightly at the sense of being generous in public,\r\nand Henery Fray, who had drawn up towards her chair, lifted his\r\neyebrows and fingers to express amazement on a small scale.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much do I owe you\xe2\x80\x94that man in the corner\xe2\x80\x94what\xe2\x80\x99s your name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ncontinued Bathsheba.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMatthew Moon, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x9d said a singular framework of clothes with\r\nnothing of any consequence inside them, which advanced with the toes in\r\nno definite direction forwards, but turned in or out as they chanced to\r\nswing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMatthew Mark, did you say?\xe2\x80\x94speak out\xe2\x80\x94I shall not hurt you,\xe2\x80\x9d inquired\r\nthe young farmer, kindly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMatthew Moon, mem,\xe2\x80\x9d said Henery Fray, correctingly, from behind her\r\nchair, to which point he had edged himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMatthew Moon,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Bathsheba, turning her bright eyes to the\r\nbook. \xe2\x80\x9cTen and twopence halfpenny is the sum put down to you, I see?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, mis\xe2\x80\x99ess,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew, as the rustle of wind among dead leaves.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere it is, and ten shillings. Now the next\xe2\x80\x94Andrew Randle, you are a\r\nnew man, I hear. How come you to leave your last farm?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cP-p-p-p-p-pl-pl-pl-pl-l-l-l-l-ease, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, p-p-p-p-pl-pl-\r\npl-pl-please, ma\xe2\x80\x99am-please\xe2\x80\x99m-please\xe2\x80\x99m\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99A\xe2\x80\x99s a stammering man, mem,\xe2\x80\x9d said Henery Fray in an undertone, \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\nthey turned him away because the only time he ever did speak plain he\r\nsaid his soul was his own, and other iniquities, to the squire. \xe2\x80\x99A can\r\ncuss, mem, as well as you or I, but \xe2\x80\x99a can\xe2\x80\x99t speak a common speech to\r\nsave his life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAndrew Randle, here\xe2\x80\x99s yours\xe2\x80\x94finish thanking me in a day or two.\r\nTemperance Miller\xe2\x80\x94oh, here\xe2\x80\x99s another, Soberness\xe2\x80\x94both women I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x99m. Here we be, \xe2\x80\x99a b\xe2\x80\x99lieve,\xe2\x80\x9d was echoed in shrill unison.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat have you been doing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTending thrashing-machine and wimbling haybonds, and saying \xe2\x80\x98Hoosh!\xe2\x80\x99\r\nto the cocks and hens when they go upon your seeds, and planting Early\r\nFlourballs and Thompson\xe2\x80\x99s Wonderfuls with a dibble.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94I see. Are they satisfactory women?\xe2\x80\x9d she inquired softly of Henery\r\nFray.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh mem\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t ask me! Yielding women\xe2\x80\x94as scarlet a pair as ever was!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ngroaned Henery under his breath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSit down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho, mem?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSit down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJoseph Poorgrass, in the background twitched, and his lips became dry\r\nwith fear of some terrible consequences, as he saw Bathsheba summarily\r\nspeaking, and Henery slinking off to a corner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow the next. Laban Tall, you\xe2\x80\x99ll stay on working for me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor you or anybody that pays me well, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the young\r\nmarried man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue\xe2\x80\x94the man must live!\xe2\x80\x9d said a woman in the back quarter, who had\r\njust entered with clicking pattens.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat woman is that?\xe2\x80\x9d Bathsheba asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI be his lawful wife!\xe2\x80\x9d continued the voice with greater prominence of\r\nmanner and tone. This lady called herself five-and-twenty, looked\r\nthirty, passed as thirty-five, and was forty. She was a woman who\r\nnever, like some newly married, showed conjugal tenderness in public,\r\nperhaps because she had none to show.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you are,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, Laban, will you stay on?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, he\xe2\x80\x99ll stay, ma\xe2\x80\x99am!\xe2\x80\x9d said again the shrill tongue of Laban\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlawful wife.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, he can speak for himself, I suppose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh Lord, not he, ma\xe2\x80\x99am! A simple tool. Well enough, but a poor\r\ngawkhammer mortal,\xe2\x80\x9d the wife replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeh-heh-heh!\xe2\x80\x9d laughed the married man with a hideous effort of\r\nappreciation, for he was as irrepressibly good-humoured under ghastly\r\nsnubs as a parliamentary candidate on the hustings.\r\n\r\nThe names remaining were called in the same manner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I think I have done with you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, closing the book\r\nand shaking back a stray twine of hair. \xe2\x80\x9cHas William Smallbury\r\nreturned?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe new shepherd will want a man under him,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Henery Fray,\r\ntrying to make himself official again by a sideway approach towards her\r\nchair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94he will. Who can he have?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYoung Cain Ball is a very good lad,\xe2\x80\x9d Henery said, \xe2\x80\x9cand Shepherd Oak\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t mind his youth?\xe2\x80\x9d he added, turning with an apologetic smile to\r\nthe shepherd, who had just appeared on the scene, and was now leaning\r\nagainst the doorpost with his arms folded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did Cain come by such a name?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Bathsheba.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh you see, mem, his pore mother, not being a Scripture-read woman,\r\nmade a mistake at his christening, thinking \xe2\x80\x99twas Abel killed Cain, and\r\ncalled en Cain, meaning Abel all the time. The parson put it right, but\r\n\xe2\x80\x99twas too late, for the name could never be got rid of in the parish.\r\n\xe2\x80\x99Tis very unfortunate for the boy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is rather unfortunate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. However, we soften it down as much as we can, and call him Cainy.\r\nAh, pore widow-woman! she cried her heart out about it almost. She was\r\nbrought up by a very heathen father and mother, who never sent her to\r\nchurch or school, and it shows how the sins of the parents are visited\r\nupon the children, mem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Fray here drew up his features to the mild degree of melancholy\r\nrequired when the persons involved in the given misfortune do not\r\nbelong to your own family.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well then, Cainey Ball to be under-shepherd. And you quite\r\nunderstand your duties?\xe2\x80\x94you I mean, Gabriel Oak?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite well, I thank you, Miss Everdene,\xe2\x80\x9d said Shepherd Oak from the\r\ndoorpost. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I don\xe2\x80\x99t, I\xe2\x80\x99ll inquire.\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel was rather staggered by\r\nthe remarkable coolness of her manner. Certainly nobody without\r\nprevious information would have dreamt that Oak and the handsome woman\r\nbefore whom he stood had ever been other than strangers. But perhaps\r\nher air was the inevitable result of the social rise which had advanced\r\nher from a cottage to a large house and fields. The case is not\r\nunexampled in high places. When, in the writings of the later poets,\r\nJove and his family are found to have moved from their cramped quarters\r\non the peak of Olympus into the wide sky above it, their words show a\r\nproportionate increase of arrogance and reserve.\r\n\r\nFootsteps were heard in the passage, combining in their character the\r\nqualities both of weight and measure, rather at the expense of\r\nvelocity.\r\n\r\n(All.) \xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s Billy Smallbury come from Casterbridge.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what\xe2\x80\x99s the news?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, as William, after marching to\r\nthe middle of the hall, took a handkerchief from his hat and wiped his\r\nforehead from its centre to its remoter boundaries.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should have been sooner, miss,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cif it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been for the\r\nweather.\xe2\x80\x9d He then stamped with each foot severely, and on looking down\r\nhis boots were perceived to be clogged with snow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome at last, is it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Henery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what about Fanny?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, in round numbers, she\xe2\x80\x99s run away with the soldiers,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nWilliam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; not a steady girl like Fanny!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell ye all particulars. When I got to Casterbridge Barracks,\r\nthey said, \xe2\x80\x98The Eleventh Dragoon-Guards be gone away, and new troops\r\nhave come.\xe2\x80\x99 The Eleventh left last week for Melchester and onwards. The\r\nRoute came from Government like a thief in the night, as is his nature\r\nto, and afore the Eleventh knew it almost, they were on the march. They\r\npassed near here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGabriel had listened with interest. \xe2\x80\x9cI saw them go,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d continued William, \xe2\x80\x9cthey pranced down the street playing \xe2\x80\x98The\r\nGirl I Left Behind Me,\xe2\x80\x99 so \xe2\x80\x99tis said, in glorious notes of triumph.\r\nEvery looker-on\xe2\x80\x99s inside shook with the blows of the great drum to his\r\ndeepest vitals, and there was not a dry eye throughout the town among\r\nthe public-house people and the nameless women!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut they\xe2\x80\x99re not gone to any war?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, ma\xe2\x80\x99am; but they be gone to take the places of them who may, which\r\nis very close connected. And so I said to myself, Fanny\xe2\x80\x99s young man was\r\none of the regiment, and she\xe2\x80\x99s gone after him. There, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, that\xe2\x80\x99s it\r\nin black and white.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you find out his name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; nobody knew it. I believe he was higher in rank than a private.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGabriel remained musing and said nothing, for he was in doubt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we are not likely to know more to-night, at any rate,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nBathsheba. \xe2\x80\x9cBut one of you had better run across to Farmer Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nand tell him that much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe then rose; but before retiring, addressed a few words to them with\r\na pretty dignity, to which her mourning dress added a soberness that\r\nwas hardly to be found in the words themselves.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow mind, you have a mistress instead of a master. I don\xe2\x80\x99t yet know my\r\npowers or my talents in farming; but I shall do my best, and if you\r\nserve me well, so shall I serve you. Don\xe2\x80\x99t any unfair ones among you\r\n(if there are any such, but I hope not) suppose that because I\xe2\x80\x99m a\r\nwoman I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand the difference between bad goings-on and\r\ngood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n(All.) \xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x99m!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n(Liddy.) \xe2\x80\x9cExcellent well said.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be up before you are awake; I shall be afield before you are\r\nup; and I shall have breakfasted before you are afield. In short, I\r\nshall astonish you all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n(All.) \xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x99m!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so good-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n(All.) \xe2\x80\x9cGood-night, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen this small thesmothete stepped from the table, and surged out of\r\nthe hall, her black silk dress licking up a few straws and dragging\r\nthem along with a scratching noise upon the floor. Liddy, elevating her\r\nfeelings to the occasion from a sense of grandeur, floated off behind\r\nBathsheba with a milder dignity not entirely free from travesty, and\r\nthe door was closed.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI\r\nOUTSIDE THE BARRACKS\xe2\x80\x94SNOW\xe2\x80\x94A MEETING\r\n\r\n\r\nFor dreariness nothing could surpass a prospect in the outskirts of a\r\ncertain town and military station, many miles north of Weatherbury, at\r\na later hour on this same snowy evening\xe2\x80\x94if that may be called a\r\nprospect of which the chief constituent was darkness.\r\n\r\nIt was a night when sorrow may come to the brightest without causing\r\nany great sense of incongruity: when, with impressible persons, love\r\nbecomes solicitousness, hope sinks to misgiving, and faith to hope:\r\nwhen the exercise of memory does not stir feelings of regret at\r\nopportunities for ambition that have been passed by, and anticipation\r\ndoes not prompt to enterprise.\r\n\r\nThe scene was a public path, bordered on the left hand by a river,\r\nbehind which rose a high wall. On the right was a tract of land, partly\r\nmeadow and partly moor, reaching, at its remote verge, to a wide\r\nundulating upland.\r\n\r\nThe changes of the seasons are less obtrusive on spots of this kind\r\nthan amid woodland scenery. Still, to a close observer, they are just\r\nas perceptible; the difference is that their media of manifestation are\r\nless trite and familiar than such well-known ones as the bursting of\r\nthe buds or the fall of the leaf. Many are not so stealthy and gradual\r\nas we may be apt to imagine in considering the general torpidity of a\r\nmoor or waste. Winter, in coming to the country hereabout, advanced in\r\nwell-marked stages, wherein might have been successively observed the\r\nretreat of the snakes, the transformation of the ferns, the filling of\r\nthe pools, a rising of fogs, the embrowning by frost, the collapse of\r\nthe fungi, and an obliteration by snow.\r\n\r\nThis climax of the series had been reached to-night on the aforesaid\r\nmoor, and for the first time in the season its irregularities were\r\nforms without features; suggestive of anything, proclaiming nothing,\r\nand without more character than that of being the limit of something\r\nelse\xe2\x80\x94the lowest layer of a firmament of snow. From this chaotic skyful\r\nof crowding flakes the mead and moor momentarily received additional\r\nclothing, only to appear momentarily more naked thereby. The vast arch\r\nof cloud above was strangely low, and formed as it were the roof of a\r\nlarge dark cavern, gradually sinking in upon its floor; for the\r\ninstinctive thought was that the snow lining the heavens and that\r\nencrusting the earth would soon unite into one mass without any\r\nintervening stratum of air at all.\r\n\r\nWe turn our attention to the left-hand characteristics; which were\r\nflatness in respect of the river, verticality in respect of the wall\r\nbehind it, and darkness as to both. These features made up the mass. If\r\nanything could be darker than the sky, it was the wall, and if any\r\nthing could be gloomier than the wall it was the river beneath. The\r\nindistinct summit of the fa\xc3\xa7ade was notched and pronged by chimneys\r\nhere and there, and upon its face were faintly signified the oblong\r\nshapes of windows, though only in the upper part. Below, down to the\r\nwater\xe2\x80\x99s edge, the flat was unbroken by hole or projection.\r\n\r\nAn indescribable succession of dull blows, perplexing in their\r\nregularity, sent their sound with difficulty through the fluffy\r\natmosphere. It was a neighbouring clock striking ten. The bell was in\r\nthe open air, and being overlaid with several inches of muffling snow,\r\nhad lost its voice for the time.\r\n\r\nAbout this hour the snow abated: ten flakes fell where twenty had\r\nfallen, then one had the room of ten. Not long after a form moved by\r\nthe brink of the river.\r\n\r\nBy its outline upon the colourless background, a close observer might\r\nhave seen that it was small. This was all that was positively\r\ndiscoverable, though it seemed human.\r\n\r\nThe shape went slowly along, but without much exertion, for the snow,\r\nthough sudden, was not as yet more than two inches deep. At this time\r\nsome words were spoken aloud:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne. Two. Three. Four. Five.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBetween each utterance the little shape advanced about half a dozen\r\nyards. It was evident now that the windows high in the wall were being\r\ncounted. The word \xe2\x80\x9cFive\xe2\x80\x9d represented the fifth window from the end of\r\nthe wall.\r\n\r\nHere the spot stopped, and dwindled smaller. The figure was stooping.\r\nThen a morsel of snow flew across the river towards the fifth window.\r\nIt smacked against the wall at a point several yards from its mark. The\r\nthrow was the idea of a man conjoined with the execution of a woman. No\r\nman who had ever seen bird, rabbit, or squirrel in his childhood, could\r\npossibly have thrown with such utter imbecility as was shown here.\r\n\r\nAnother attempt, and another; till by degrees the wall must have become\r\npimpled with the adhering lumps of snow. At last one fragment struck\r\nthe fifth window.\r\n\r\nThe river would have been seen by day to be of that deep smooth sort\r\nwhich races middle and sides with the same gliding precision, any\r\nirregularities of speed being immediately corrected by a small\r\nwhirlpool. Nothing was heard in reply to the signal but the gurgle and\r\ncluck of one of these invisible wheels\xe2\x80\x94together with a few small sounds\r\nwhich a sad man would have called moans, and a happy man\r\nlaughter\xe2\x80\x94caused by the flapping of the waters against trifling objects\r\nin other parts of the stream.\r\n\r\nThe window was struck again in the same manner.\r\n\r\nThen a noise was heard, apparently produced by the opening of the\r\nwindow. This was followed by a voice from the same quarter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe tones were masculine, and not those of surprise. The high wall\r\nbeing that of a barrack, and marriage being looked upon with disfavour\r\nin the army, assignations and communications had probably been made\r\nacross the river before to-night.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it Sergeant Troy?\xe2\x80\x9d said the blurred spot in the snow, tremulously.\r\n\r\nThis person was so much like a mere shade upon the earth, and the other\r\nspeaker so much a part of the building, that one would have said the\r\nwall was holding a conversation with the snow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d came suspiciously from the shadow. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat girl are you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Frank\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t you know me?\xe2\x80\x9d said the spot. \xe2\x80\x9cYour wife, Fanny Robin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFanny!\xe2\x80\x9d said the wall, in utter astonishment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said the girl, with a half-suppressed gasp of emotion.\r\n\r\nThere was something in the woman\xe2\x80\x99s tone which is not that of the wife,\r\nand there was a manner in the man which is rarely a husband\xe2\x80\x99s. The\r\ndialogue went on:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did you come here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI asked which was your window. Forgive me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not expect you to-night. Indeed, I did not think you would come\r\nat all. It was a wonder you found me here. I am orderly to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou said I was to come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94I said that you might.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I mean that I might. You are glad to see me, Frank?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes\xe2\x80\x94of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you\xe2\x80\x94come to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Fan, no! The bugle has sounded, the barrack gates are closed,\r\nand I have no leave. We are all of us as good as in the county gaol\r\ntill to-morrow morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I shan\xe2\x80\x99t see you till then!\xe2\x80\x9d The words were in a faltering tone\r\nof disappointment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did you get here from Weatherbury?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI walked\xe2\x80\x94some part of the way\xe2\x80\x94the rest by the carriers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am surprised.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94so am I. And Frank, when will it be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat you promised.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t quite recollect.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO you do! Don\xe2\x80\x99t speak like that. It weighs me to the earth. It makes\r\nme say what ought to be said first by you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind\xe2\x80\x94say it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, must I?\xe2\x80\x94it is, when shall we be married, Frank?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I see. Well\xe2\x80\x94you have to get proper clothes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have money. Will it be by banns or license?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBanns, I should think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd we live in two parishes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo we? What then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy lodgings are in St. Mary\xe2\x80\x99s, and this is not. So they will have to\r\nbe published in both.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that the law?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. O Frank\xe2\x80\x94you think me forward, I am afraid! Don\xe2\x80\x99t, dear Frank\xe2\x80\x94will\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94for I love you so. And you said lots of times you would marry me,\r\nand\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t cry, now! It is foolish. If I said so, of course I will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd shall I put up the banns in my parish, and will you in yours?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo-morrow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot to-morrow. We\xe2\x80\x99ll settle in a few days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have the permission of the officers?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, not yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO\xe2\x80\x94how is it? You said you almost had before you left Casterbridge.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe fact is, I forgot to ask. Your coming like this is so sudden and\r\nunexpected.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94yes\xe2\x80\x94it is. It was wrong of me to worry you. I\xe2\x80\x99ll go away now. Will\r\nyou come and see me to-morrow, at Mrs. Twills\xe2\x80\x99s, in North Street? I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t like to come to the Barracks. There are bad women about, and they\r\nthink me one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite, so. I\xe2\x80\x99ll come to you, my dear. Good-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-night, Frank\xe2\x80\x94good-night!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd the noise was again heard of a window closing. The little spot\r\nmoved away. When she passed the corner a subdued exclamation was heard\r\ninside the wall.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHo\xe2\x80\x94ho\xe2\x80\x94Sergeant\xe2\x80\x94ho\xe2\x80\x94ho!\xe2\x80\x9d An expostulation followed, but it was\r\nindistinct; and it became lost amid a low peal of laughter, which was\r\nhardly distinguishable from the gurgle of the tiny whirlpools outside.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XII\r\nFARMERS\xe2\x80\x94A RULE\xe2\x80\x94AN EXCEPTION\r\n\r\n\r\nThe first public evidence of Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s decision to be a farmer in her\r\nown person and by proxy no more was her appearance the following\r\nmarket-day in the cornmarket at Casterbridge.\r\n\r\nThe low though extensive hall, supported by beams and pillars, and\r\nlatterly dignified by the name of Corn Exchange, was thronged with hot\r\nmen who talked among each other in twos and threes, the speaker of the\r\nminute looking sideways into his auditor\xe2\x80\x99s face and concentrating his\r\nargument by a contraction of one eyelid during delivery. The greater\r\nnumber carried in their hands ground-ash saplings, using them partly as\r\nwalking-sticks and partly for poking up pigs, sheep, neighbours with\r\ntheir backs turned, and restful things in general, which seemed to\r\nrequire such treatment in the course of their peregrinations. During\r\nconversations each subjected his sapling to great varieties of\r\nusage\xe2\x80\x94bending it round his back, forming an arch of it between his two\r\nhands, overweighting it on the ground till it reached nearly a\r\nsemicircle; or perhaps it was hastily tucked under the arm whilst the\r\nsample-bag was pulled forth and a handful of corn poured into the palm,\r\nwhich, after criticism, was flung upon the floor, an issue of events\r\nperfectly well known to half-a-dozen acute town-bred fowls which had as\r\nusual crept into the building unobserved, and waited the fulfilment of\r\ntheir anticipations with a high-stretched neck and oblique eye.\r\n\r\nAmong these heavy yeomen a feminine figure glided, the single one of\r\nher sex that the room contained. She was prettily and even daintily\r\ndressed. She moved between them as a chaise between carts, was heard\r\nafter them as a romance after sermons, was felt among them like a\r\nbreeze among furnaces. It had required a little determination\xe2\x80\x94far more\r\nthan she had at first imagined\xe2\x80\x94to take up a position here, for at her\r\nfirst entry the lumbering dialogues had ceased, nearly every face had\r\nbeen turned towards her, and those that were already turned rigidly\r\nfixed there.\r\n\r\nTwo or three only of the farmers were personally known to Bathsheba,\r\nand to these she had made her way. But if she was to be the practical\r\nwoman she had intended to show herself, business must be carried on,\r\nintroductions or none, and she ultimately acquired confidence enough to\r\nspeak and reply boldly to men merely known to her by hearsay. Bathsheba\r\ntoo had her sample-bags, and by degrees adopted the professional pour\r\ninto the hand\xe2\x80\x94holding up the grains in her narrow palm for inspection,\r\nin perfect Casterbridge manner.\r\n\r\nSomething in the exact arch of her upper unbroken row of teeth, and in\r\nthe keenly pointed corners of her red mouth when, with parted lips, she\r\nsomewhat defiantly turned up her face to argue a point with a tall man,\r\nsuggested that there was potentiality enough in that lithe slip of\r\nhumanity for alarming exploits of sex, and daring enough to carry them\r\nout. But her eyes had a softness\xe2\x80\x94invariably a softness\xe2\x80\x94which, had they\r\nnot been dark, would have seemed mistiness; as they were, it lowered an\r\nexpression that might have been piercing to simple clearness.\r\n\r\nStrange to say of a woman in full bloom and vigor, she always allowed\r\nher interlocutors to finish their statements before rejoining with\r\nhers. In arguing on prices, she held to her own firmly, as was natural\r\nin a dealer, and reduced theirs persistently, as was inevitable in a\r\nwoman. But there was an elasticity in her firmness which removed it\r\nfrom obstinacy, as there was a _na\xc3\xafvet\xc3\xa9_ in her cheapening which saved\r\nit from meanness.\r\n\r\nThose of the farmers with whom she had no dealings (by far the greater\r\npart) were continually asking each other, \xe2\x80\x9cWho is she?\xe2\x80\x9d The reply would\r\nbe\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFarmer Everdene\xe2\x80\x99s niece; took on Weatherbury Upper Farm; turned away\r\nthe baily, and swears she\xe2\x80\x99ll do everything herself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe other man would then shake his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, \xe2\x80\x99tis a pity she\xe2\x80\x99s so headstrong,\xe2\x80\x9d the first would say. \xe2\x80\x9cBut we\r\nought to be proud of her here\xe2\x80\x94she lightens up the old place. \xe2\x80\x99Tis such\r\na shapely maid, however, that she\xe2\x80\x99ll soon get picked up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt would be ungallant to suggest that the novelty of her engagement in\r\nsuch an occupation had almost as much to do with the magnetism as had\r\nthe beauty of her face and movements. However, the interest was\r\ngeneral, and this Saturday\xe2\x80\x99s _d\xc3\xa9but_ in the forum, whatever it may have\r\nbeen to Bathsheba as the buying and selling farmer, was unquestionably\r\na triumph to her as the maiden. Indeed, the sensation was so pronounced\r\nthat her instinct on two or three occasions was merely to walk as a\r\nqueen among these gods of the fallow, like a little sister of a little\r\nJove, and to neglect closing prices altogether.\r\n\r\nThe numerous evidences of her power to attract were only thrown into\r\ngreater relief by a marked exception. Women seem to have eyes in their\r\nribbons for such matters as these. Bathsheba, without looking within a\r\nright angle of him, was conscious of a black sheep among the flock.\r\n\r\nIt perplexed her first. If there had been a respectable minority on\r\neither side, the case would have been most natural. If nobody had\r\nregarded her, she would have taken the matter indifferently\xe2\x80\x94such cases\r\nhad occurred. If everybody, this man included, she would have taken it\r\nas a matter of course\xe2\x80\x94people had done so before. But the smallness of\r\nthe exception made the mystery.\r\n\r\nShe soon knew thus much of the recusant\xe2\x80\x99s appearance. He was a\r\ngentlemanly man, with full and distinctly outlined Roman features, the\r\nprominences of which glowed in the sun with a bronze-like richness of\r\ntone. He was erect in attitude, and quiet in demeanour. One\r\ncharacteristic pre-eminently marked him\xe2\x80\x94dignity.\r\n\r\nApparently he had some time ago reached that entrance to middle age at\r\nwhich a man\xe2\x80\x99s aspect naturally ceases to alter for the term of a dozen\r\nyears or so; and, artificially, a woman\xe2\x80\x99s does likewise. Thirty-five\r\nand fifty were his limits of variation\xe2\x80\x94he might have been either, or\r\nanywhere between the two.\r\n\r\nIt may be said that married men of forty are usually ready and generous\r\nenough to fling passing glances at any specimen of moderate beauty they\r\nmay discern by the way. Probably, as with persons playing whist for\r\nlove, the consciousness of a certain immunity under any circumstances\r\nfrom that worst possible ultimate, the having to pay, makes them unduly\r\nspeculative. Bathsheba was convinced that this unmoved person was not a\r\nmarried man.\r\n\r\nWhen marketing was over, she rushed off to Liddy, who was waiting for\r\nher beside the yellow gig in which they had driven to town. The horse\r\nwas put in, and on they trotted\xe2\x80\x94Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s sugar, tea, and drapery\r\nparcels being packed behind, and expressing in some indescribable\r\nmanner, by their colour, shape, and general lineaments, that they were\r\nthat young lady-farmer\xe2\x80\x99s property, and the grocer\xe2\x80\x99s and draper\xe2\x80\x99s no\r\nmore.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve been through it, Liddy, and it is over. I shan\xe2\x80\x99t mind it again,\r\nfor they will all have grown accustomed to seeing me there; but this\r\nmorning it was as bad as being married\xe2\x80\x94eyes everywhere!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knowed it would be,\xe2\x80\x9d Liddy said. \xe2\x80\x9cMen be such a terrible class of\r\nsociety to look at a body.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut there was one man who had more sense than to waste his time upon\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d The information was put in this form that Liddy might not for a\r\nmoment suppose her mistress was at all piqued. \xe2\x80\x9cA very good-looking\r\nman,\xe2\x80\x9d she continued, \xe2\x80\x9cupright; about forty, I should think. Do you know\r\nat all who he could be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLiddy couldn\xe2\x80\x99t think.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t you guess at all?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba with some disappointment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t a notion; besides, \xe2\x80\x99tis no difference, since he took less\r\nnotice of you than any of the rest. Now, if he\xe2\x80\x99d taken more, it would\r\nhave mattered a great deal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba was suffering from the reverse feeling just then, and they\r\nbowled along in silence. A low carriage, bowling along still more\r\nrapidly behind a horse of unimpeachable breed, overtook and passed\r\nthem.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, there he is!\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nLiddy looked. \xe2\x80\x9cThat! That\xe2\x80\x99s Farmer Boldwood\xe2\x80\x94of course \xe2\x80\x99tis\xe2\x80\x94the man you\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t see the other day when he called.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Farmer Boldwood,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Bathsheba, and looked at him as he\r\noutstripped them. The farmer had never turned his head once, but with\r\neyes fixed on the most advanced point along the road, passed as\r\nunconsciously and abstractedly as if Bathsheba and her charms were thin\r\nair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s an interesting man\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t you think so?\xe2\x80\x9d she remarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO yes, very. Everybody owns it,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Liddy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder why he is so wrapt up and indifferent, and seemingly so far\r\naway from all he sees around him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is said\xe2\x80\x94but not known for certain\xe2\x80\x94that he met with some bitter\r\ndisappointment when he was a young man and merry. A woman jilted him,\r\nthey say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeople always say that\xe2\x80\x94and we know very well women scarcely ever jilt\r\nmen; \xe2\x80\x99tis the men who jilt us. I expect it is simply his nature to be\r\nso reserved.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSimply his nature\xe2\x80\x94I expect so, miss\xe2\x80\x94nothing else in the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill, \xe2\x80\x99tis more romantic to think he has been served cruelly, poor\r\nthing\xe2\x80\x99! Perhaps, after all, he has!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDepend upon it he has. Oh yes, miss, he has! I feel he must have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHowever, we are very apt to think extremes of people. I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwonder after all if it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t a little of both\xe2\x80\x94just between the\r\ntwo\xe2\x80\x94rather cruelly used and rather reserved.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh dear no, miss\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t think it between the two!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s most likely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, yes, so it is. I am convinced it is most likely. You may take my\r\nword, miss, that that\xe2\x80\x99s what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIII\r\nSORTES SANCTORUM\xe2\x80\x94THE VALENTINE\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was Sunday afternoon in the farmhouse, on the thirteenth of\r\nFebruary. Dinner being over, Bathsheba, for want of a better companion,\r\nhad asked Liddy to come and sit with her. The mouldy pile was dreary in\r\nwinter-time before the candles were lighted and the shutters closed;\r\nthe atmosphere of the place seemed as old as the walls; every nook\r\nbehind the furniture had a temperature of its own, for the fire was not\r\nkindled in this part of the house early in the day; and Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s new\r\npiano, which was an old one in other annals, looked particularly\r\nsloping and out of level on the warped floor before night threw a shade\r\nover its less prominent angles and hid the unpleasantness. Liddy, like\r\na little brook, though shallow, was always rippling; her presence had\r\nnot so much weight as to task thought, and yet enough to exercise it.\r\n\r\nOn the table lay an old quarto Bible, bound in leather. Liddy looking\r\nat it said,\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you ever find out, miss, who you are going to marry by means of\r\nthe Bible and key?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be so foolish, Liddy. As if such things could be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, there\xe2\x80\x99s a good deal in it, all the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense, child.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd it makes your heart beat fearful. Some believe in it; some don\xe2\x80\x99t;\r\nI do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, let\xe2\x80\x99s try it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, bounding from her seat with\r\nthat total disregard of consistency which can be indulged in towards a\r\ndependent, and entering into the spirit of divination at once. \xe2\x80\x9cGo and\r\nget the front door key.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLiddy fetched it. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t Sunday,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, on returning.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps \xe2\x80\x99tis wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s right week days is right Sundays,\xe2\x80\x9d replied her mistress in a\r\ntone which was a proof in itself.\r\n\r\nThe book was opened\xe2\x80\x94the leaves, drab with age, being quite worn away at\r\nmuch-read verses by the forefingers of unpractised readers in former\r\ndays, where they were moved along under the line as an aid to the\r\nvision. The special verse in the Book of Ruth was sought out by\r\nBathsheba, and the sublime words met her eye. They slightly thrilled\r\nand abashed her. It was Wisdom in the abstract facing Folly in the\r\nconcrete. Folly in the concrete blushed, persisted in her intention,\r\nand placed the key on the book. A rusty patch immediately upon the\r\nverse, caused by previous pressure of an iron substance thereon, told\r\nthat this was not the first time the old volume had been used for the\r\npurpose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow keep steady, and be silent,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba.\r\n\r\nThe verse was repeated; the book turned round; Bathsheba blushed\r\nguiltily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho did you try?\xe2\x80\x9d said Liddy curiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall not tell you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you notice Mr. Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s doings in church this morning, miss?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nLiddy continued, adumbrating by the remark the track her thoughts had\r\ntaken.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, with serene indifference.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis pew is exactly opposite yours, miss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you did not see his goings on!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly I did not, I tell you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLiddy assumed a smaller physiognomy, and shut her lips decisively.\r\n\r\nThis move was unexpected, and proportionately disconcerting. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat did\r\nhe do?\xe2\x80\x9d Bathsheba said perforce.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t turn his head to look at you once all the service.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should he?\xe2\x80\x9d again demanded her mistress, wearing a nettled look.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t ask him to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no. But everybody else was noticing you; and it was odd he didn\xe2\x80\x99t.\r\nThere, \xe2\x80\x99tis like him. Rich and gentlemanly, what does he care?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba dropped into a silence intended to express that she had\r\nopinions on the matter too abstruse for Liddy\xe2\x80\x99s comprehension, rather\r\nthan that she had nothing to say.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear me\xe2\x80\x94I had nearly forgotten the valentine I bought yesterday,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nexclaimed at length.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cValentine! who for, miss?\xe2\x80\x9d said Liddy. \xe2\x80\x9cFarmer Boldwood?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was the single name among all possible wrong ones that just at this\r\nmoment seemed to Bathsheba more pertinent than the right.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, no. It is only for little Teddy Coggan. I have promised him\r\nsomething, and this will be a pretty surprise for him. Liddy, you may\r\nas well bring me my desk and I\xe2\x80\x99ll direct it at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba took from her desk a gorgeously illuminated and embossed\r\ndesign in post-octavo, which had been bought on the previous market-day\r\nat the chief stationer\xe2\x80\x99s in Casterbridge. In the centre was a small\r\noval enclosure; this was left blank, that the sender might insert\r\ntender words more appropriate to the special occasion than any\r\ngeneralities by a printer could possibly be.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s a place for writing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat shall I put?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomething of this sort, I should think,\xe2\x80\x9d returned Liddy promptly:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe rose is red,\r\nThe violet blue,\r\nCarnation\xe2\x80\x99s sweet,\r\nAnd so are you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that shall be it. It just suits itself to a chubby-faced child\r\nlike him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba. She inserted the words in a small though\r\nlegible handwriting; enclosed the sheet in an envelope, and dipped her\r\npen for the direction.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat fun it would be to send it to the stupid old Boldwood, and how he\r\nwould wonder!\xe2\x80\x9d said the irrepressible Liddy, lifting her eyebrows, and\r\nindulging in an awful mirth on the verge of fear as she thought of the\r\nmoral and social magnitude of the man contemplated.\r\n\r\nBathsheba paused to regard the idea at full length. Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s had\r\nbegun to be a troublesome image\xe2\x80\x94a species of Daniel in her kingdom who\r\npersisted in kneeling eastward when reason and common sense said that\r\nhe might just as well follow suit with the rest, and afford her the\r\nofficial glance of admiration which cost nothing at all. She was far\r\nfrom being seriously concerned about his nonconformity. Still, it was\r\nfaintly depressing that the most dignified and valuable man in the\r\nparish should withhold his eyes, and that a girl like Liddy should talk\r\nabout it. So Liddy\xe2\x80\x99s idea was at first rather harassing than piquant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I won\xe2\x80\x99t do that. He wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t see any humour in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99d worry to death,\xe2\x80\x9d said the persistent Liddy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally, I don\xe2\x80\x99t care particularly to send it to Teddy,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked her\r\nmistress. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s rather a naughty child sometimes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94that he is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s toss as men do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, idly. \xe2\x80\x9cNow then, head,\r\nBoldwood; tail, Teddy. No, we won\xe2\x80\x99t toss money on a Sunday, that would\r\nbe tempting the devil indeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToss this hymn-book; there can\xe2\x80\x99t be no sinfulness in that, miss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well. Open, Boldwood\xe2\x80\x94shut, Teddy. No; it\xe2\x80\x99s more likely to fall\r\nopen. Open, Teddy\xe2\x80\x94shut, Boldwood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe book went fluttering in the air and came down shut.\r\n\r\nBathsheba, a small yawn upon her mouth, took the pen, and with off-hand\r\nserenity directed the missive to Boldwood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow light a candle, Liddy. Which seal shall we use? Here\xe2\x80\x99s a unicorn\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhead\xe2\x80\x94there\xe2\x80\x99s nothing in that. What\xe2\x80\x99s this?\xe2\x80\x94two doves\xe2\x80\x94no. It ought to be\r\nsomething extraordinary, ought it not, Liddy? Here\xe2\x80\x99s one with a motto\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nremember it is some funny one, but I can\xe2\x80\x99t read it. We\xe2\x80\x99ll try this, and\r\nif it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t do we\xe2\x80\x99ll have another.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA large red seal was duly affixed. Bathsheba looked closely at the hot\r\nwax to discover the words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCapital!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, throwing down the letter frolicsomely.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Twould upset the solemnity of a parson and clerke too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLiddy looked at the words of the seal, and read\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMARRY ME.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nThe same evening the letter was sent, and was duly sorted in\r\nCasterbridge post-office that night, to be returned to Weatherbury\r\nagain in the morning.\r\n\r\nSo very idly and unreflectingly was this deed done. Of love as a\r\nspectacle Bathsheba had a fair knowledge; but of love subjectively she\r\nknew nothing.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIV\r\nEFFECT OF THE LETTER\xe2\x80\x94SUNRISE\r\n\r\n\r\nAt dusk, on the evening of St. Valentine\xe2\x80\x99s Day, Boldwood sat down to\r\nsupper as usual, by a beaming fire of aged logs. Upon the mantel-shelf\r\nbefore him was a time-piece, surmounted by a spread eagle, and upon the\r\neagle\xe2\x80\x99s wings was the letter Bathsheba had sent. Here the bachelor\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngaze was continually fastening itself, till the large red seal became\r\nas a blot of blood on the retina of his eye; and as he ate and drank he\r\nstill read in fancy the words thereon, although they were too remote\r\nfor his sight\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMARRY ME.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nThe pert injunction was like those crystal substances which, colourless\r\nthemselves, assume the tone of objects about them. Here, in the quiet\r\nof Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s parlour, where everything that was not grave was\r\nextraneous, and where the atmosphere was that of a Puritan Sunday\r\nlasting all the week, the letter and its dictum changed their tenor\r\nfrom the thoughtlessness of their origin to a deep solemnity, imbibed\r\nfrom their accessories now.\r\n\r\nSince the receipt of the missive in the morning, Boldwood had felt the\r\nsymmetry of his existence to be slowly getting distorted in the\r\ndirection of an ideal passion. The disturbance was as the first\r\nfloating weed to Columbus\xe2\x80\x94the contemptibly little suggesting\r\npossibilities of the infinitely great.\r\n\r\nThe letter must have had an origin and a motive. That the latter was of\r\nthe smallest magnitude compatible with its existence at all, Boldwood,\r\nof course, did not know. And such an explanation did not strike him as\r\na possibility even. It is foreign to a mystified condition of mind to\r\nrealize of the mystifier that the processes of approving a course\r\nsuggested by circumstance, and of striking out a course from inner\r\nimpulse, would look the same in the result. The vast difference between\r\nstarting a train of events, and directing into a particular groove a\r\nseries already started, is rarely apparent to the person confounded by\r\nthe issue.\r\n\r\nWhen Boldwood went to bed he placed the valentine in the corner of the\r\nlooking-glass. He was conscious of its presence, even when his back was\r\nturned upon it. It was the first time in Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s life that such an\r\nevent had occurred. The same fascination that caused him to think it an\r\nact which had a deliberate motive prevented him from regarding it as an\r\nimpertinence. He looked again at the direction. The mysterious\r\ninfluences of night invested the writing with the presence of the\r\nunknown writer. Somebody\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94some _woman\xe2\x80\x99s_\xe2\x80\x94hand had travelled softly\r\nover the paper bearing his name; her unrevealed eyes had watched every\r\ncurve as she formed it; her brain had seen him in imagination the\r\nwhile. Why should she have imagined him? Her mouth\xe2\x80\x94were the lips red or\r\npale, plump or creased?\xe2\x80\x94had curved itself to a certain expression as\r\nthe pen went on\xe2\x80\x94the corners had moved with all their natural\r\ntremulousness: what had been the expression?\r\n\r\nThe vision of the woman writing, as a supplement to the words written,\r\nhad no individuality. She was a misty shape, and well she might be,\r\nconsidering that her original was at that moment sound asleep and\r\noblivious of all love and letter-writing under the sky. Whenever\r\nBoldwood dozed she took a form, and comparatively ceased to be a\r\nvision: when he awoke there was the letter justifying the dream.\r\n\r\nThe moon shone to-night, and its light was not of a customary kind. His\r\nwindow admitted only a reflection of its rays, and the pale sheen had\r\nthat reversed direction which snow gives, coming upward and lighting up\r\nhis ceiling in an unnatural way, casting shadows in strange places, and\r\nputting lights where shadows had used to be.\r\n\r\nThe substance of the epistle had occupied him but little in comparison\r\nwith the fact of its arrival. He suddenly wondered if anything more\r\nmight be found in the envelope than what he had withdrawn. He jumped\r\nout of bed in the weird light, took the letter, pulled out the flimsy\r\nsheet, shook the envelope\xe2\x80\x94searched it. Nothing more was there. Boldwood\r\nlooked, as he had a hundred times the preceding day, at the insistent\r\nred seal: \xe2\x80\x9cMarry me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said aloud.\r\n\r\nThe solemn and reserved yeoman again closed the letter, and stuck it in\r\nthe frame of the glass. In doing so he caught sight of his reflected\r\nfeatures, wan in expression, and insubstantial in form. He saw how\r\nclosely compressed was his mouth, and that his eyes were wide-spread\r\nand vacant. Feeling uneasy and dissatisfied with himself for this\r\nnervous excitability, he returned to bed.\r\n\r\nThen the dawn drew on. The full power of the clear heaven was not equal\r\nto that of a cloudy sky at noon, when Boldwood arose and dressed\r\nhimself. He descended the stairs and went out towards the gate of a\r\nfield to the east, leaning over which he paused and looked around.\r\n\r\nIt was one of the usual slow sunrises of this time of the year, and the\r\nsky, pure violet in the zenith, was leaden to the northward, and murky\r\nto the east, where, over the snowy down or ewe-lease on Weatherbury\r\nUpper Farm, and apparently resting upon the ridge, the only half of the\r\nsun yet visible burnt rayless, like a red and flameless fire shining\r\nover a white hearthstone. The whole effect resembled a sunset as\r\nchildhood resembles age.\r\n\r\nIn other directions, the fields and sky were so much of one colour by\r\nthe snow, that it was difficult in a hasty glance to tell whereabouts\r\nthe horizon occurred; and in general there was here, too, that\r\nbefore-mentioned preternatural inversion of light and shade which\r\nattends the prospect when the garish brightness commonly in the sky is\r\nfound on the earth, and the shades of earth are in the sky. Over the\r\nwest hung the wasting moon, now dull and greenish-yellow, like\r\ntarnished brass.\r\n\r\nBoldwood was listlessly noting how the frost had hardened and glazed\r\nthe surface of the snow, till it shone in the red eastern light with\r\nthe polish of marble; how, in some portions of the slope, withered\r\ngrass-bents, encased in icicles, bristled through the smooth wan\r\ncoverlet in the twisted and curved shapes of old Venetian glass; and\r\nhow the footprints of a few birds, which had hopped over the snow\r\nwhilst it lay in the state of a soft fleece, were now frozen to a short\r\npermanency. A half-muffled noise of light wheels interrupted him.\r\nBoldwood turned back into the road. It was the mail-cart\xe2\x80\x94a crazy,\r\ntwo-wheeled vehicle, hardly heavy enough to resist a puff of wind. The\r\ndriver held out a letter. Boldwood seized it and opened it, expecting\r\nanother anonymous one\xe2\x80\x94so greatly are people\xe2\x80\x99s ideas of probability a\r\nmere sense that precedent will repeat itself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think it is for you, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said the man, when he saw\r\nBoldwood\xe2\x80\x99s action. \xe2\x80\x9cThough there is no name, I think it is for your\r\nshepherd.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBoldwood looked then at the address\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nTo the New Shepherd,\r\nWeatherbury Farm,\r\nNear Casterbridge\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94what a mistake!\xe2\x80\x94it is not mine. Nor is it for my shepherd. It is\r\nfor Miss Everdene\xe2\x80\x99s. You had better take it on to him\xe2\x80\x94Gabriel Oak\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nsay I opened it in mistake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this moment, on the ridge, up against the blazing sky, a figure was\r\nvisible, like the black snuff in the midst of a candle-flame. Then it\r\nmoved and began to bustle about vigorously from place to place,\r\ncarrying square skeleton masses, which were riddled by the same rays. A\r\nsmall figure on all fours followed behind. The tall form was that of\r\nGabriel Oak; the small one that of George; the articles in course of\r\ntransit were hurdles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait,\xe2\x80\x9d said Boldwood. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the man on the hill. I\xe2\x80\x99ll take the\r\nletter to him myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo Boldwood it was now no longer merely a letter to another man. It was\r\nan opportunity. Exhibiting a face pregnant with intention, he entered\r\nthe snowy field.\r\n\r\nGabriel, at that minute, descended the hill towards the right. The glow\r\nstretched down in this direction now, and touched the distant roof of\r\nWarren\xe2\x80\x99s Malthouse\xe2\x80\x94whither the shepherd was apparently bent: Boldwood\r\nfollowed at a distance.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XV\r\nA MORNING MEETING\xe2\x80\x94THE LETTER AGAIN\r\n\r\n\r\nThe scarlet and orange light outside the malthouse did not penetrate to\r\nits interior, which was, as usual, lighted by a rival glow of similar\r\nhue, radiating from the hearth.\r\n\r\nThe maltster, after having lain down in his clothes for a few hours,\r\nwas now sitting beside a three-legged table, breakfasting off bread and\r\nbacon. This was eaten on the plateless system, which is performed by\r\nplacing a slice of bread upon the table, the meat flat upon the bread,\r\na mustard plaster upon the meat, and a pinch of salt upon the whole,\r\nthen cutting them vertically downwards with a large pocket-knife till\r\nwood is reached, when the severed lump is impaled on the knife,\r\nelevated, and sent the proper way of food.\r\n\r\nThe maltster\xe2\x80\x99s lack of teeth appeared not to sensibly diminish his\r\npowers as a mill. He had been without them for so many years that\r\ntoothlessness was felt less to be a defect than hard gums an\r\nacquisition. Indeed, he seemed to approach the grave as a hyperbolic\r\ncurve approaches a straight line\xe2\x80\x94less directly as he got nearer, till\r\nit was doubtful if he would ever reach it at all.\r\n\r\nIn the ashpit was a heap of potatoes roasting, and a boiling pipkin of\r\ncharred bread, called \xe2\x80\x9ccoffee\xe2\x80\x9d, for the benefit of whomsoever should\r\ncall, for Warren\xe2\x80\x99s was a sort of clubhouse, used as an alternative to\r\nthe inn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, says I, we get a fine day, and then down comes a snapper at\r\nnight,\xe2\x80\x9d was a remark now suddenly heard spreading into the malthouse\r\nfrom the door, which had been opened the previous moment. The form of\r\nHenery Fray advanced to the fire, stamping the snow from his boots when\r\nabout half-way there. The speech and entry had not seemed to be at all\r\nan abrupt beginning to the maltster, introductory matter being often\r\nomitted in this neighbourhood, both from word and deed, and the\r\nmaltster having the same latitude allowed him, did not hurry to reply.\r\nHe picked up a fragment of cheese, by pecking upon it with his knife,\r\nas a butcher picks up skewers.\r\n\r\nHenery appeared in a drab kerseymere great-coat, buttoned over his\r\nsmock-frock, the white skirts of the latter being visible to the\r\ndistance of about a foot below the coat-tails, which, when you got used\r\nto the style of dress, looked natural enough, and even ornamental\xe2\x80\x94it\r\ncertainly was comfortable.\r\n\r\nMatthew Moon, Joseph Poorgrass, and other carters and waggoners\r\nfollowed at his heels, with great lanterns dangling from their hands,\r\nwhich showed that they had just come from the cart-horse stables, where\r\nthey had been busily engaged since four o\xe2\x80\x99clock that morning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how is she getting on without a baily?\xe2\x80\x9d the maltster inquired.\r\nHenery shook his head, and smiled one of the bitter smiles, dragging\r\nall the flesh of his forehead into a corrugated heap in the centre.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99ll rue it\xe2\x80\x94surely, surely!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBenjy Pennyways were not a\r\ntrue man or an honest baily\xe2\x80\x94as big a betrayer as Judas Iscariot\r\nhimself. But to think she can carr\xe2\x80\x99 on alone!\xe2\x80\x9d He allowed his head to\r\nswing laterally three or four times in silence. \xe2\x80\x9cNever in all my\r\ncreeping up\xe2\x80\x94never!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was recognized by all as the conclusion of some gloomy speech\r\nwhich had been expressed in thought alone during the shake of the head;\r\nHenery meanwhile retained several marks of despair upon his face, to\r\nimply that they would be required for use again directly he should go\r\non speaking.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll will be ruined, and ourselves too, or there\xe2\x80\x99s no meat in\r\ngentlemen\xe2\x80\x99s houses!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mark Clark.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA headstrong maid, that\xe2\x80\x99s what she is\xe2\x80\x94and won\xe2\x80\x99t listen to no advice at\r\nall. Pride and vanity have ruined many a cobbler\xe2\x80\x99s dog. Dear, dear,\r\nwhen I think o\xe2\x80\x99 it, I sorrows like a man in travel!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue, Henery, you do, I\xe2\x80\x99ve heard ye,\xe2\x80\x9d said Joseph Poorgrass in a voice\r\nof thorough attestation, and with a wire-drawn smile of misery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Twould do a martel man no harm to have what\xe2\x80\x99s under her bonnet,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nBilly Smallbury, who had just entered, bearing his one tooth before\r\nhim. \xe2\x80\x9cShe can spaik real language, and must have some sense somewhere.\r\nDo ye foller me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do, I do; but no baily\xe2\x80\x94I deserved that place,\xe2\x80\x9d wailed Henery,\r\nsignifying wasted genius by gazing blankly at visions of a high destiny\r\napparently visible to him on Billy Smallbury\xe2\x80\x99s smock-frock. \xe2\x80\x9cThere,\r\n\xe2\x80\x99twas to be, I suppose. Your lot is your lot, and Scripture is nothing;\r\nfor if you do good you don\xe2\x80\x99t get rewarded according to your works, but\r\nbe cheated in some mean way out of your recompense.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no; I don\xe2\x80\x99t agree with\xe2\x80\x99ee there,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mark Clark. \xe2\x80\x9cGod\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nperfect gentleman in that respect.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood works good pay, so to speak it,\xe2\x80\x9d attested Joseph Poorgrass.\r\n\r\nA short pause ensued, and as a sort of _entr\xe2\x80\x99acte_ Henery turned and\r\nblew out the lanterns, which the increase of daylight rendered no\r\nlonger necessary even in the malthouse, with its one pane of glass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder what a farmer-woman can want with a harpsichord, dulcimer,\r\npianner, or whatever \xe2\x80\x99tis they d\xe2\x80\x99call it?\xe2\x80\x9d said the maltster. \xe2\x80\x9cLiddy\r\nsaith she\xe2\x80\x99ve a new one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGot a pianner?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy. Seems her old uncle\xe2\x80\x99s things were not good enough for her. She\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nbought all but everything new. There\xe2\x80\x99s heavy chairs for the stout, weak\r\nand wiry ones for the slender; great watches, getting on to the size of\r\nclocks, to stand upon the chimbley-piece.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPictures, for the most part wonderful frames.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd long horse-hair settles for the drunk, with horse-hair pillows at\r\neach end,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Clark. \xe2\x80\x9cLikewise looking-glasses for the pretty,\r\nand lying books for the wicked.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA firm loud tread was now heard stamping outside; the door was opened\r\nabout six inches, and somebody on the other side exclaimed\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeighbours, have ye got room for a few new-born lambs?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, sure, shepherd,\xe2\x80\x9d said the conclave.\r\n\r\nThe door was flung back till it kicked the wall and trembled from top\r\nto bottom with the blow. Mr. Oak appeared in the entry with a steaming\r\nface, hay-bands wound about his ankles to keep out the snow, a leather\r\nstrap round his waist outside the smock-frock, and looking altogether\r\nan epitome of the world\xe2\x80\x99s health and vigour. Four lambs hung in various\r\nembarrassing attitudes over his shoulders, and the dog George, whom\r\nGabriel had contrived to fetch from Norcombe, stalked solemnly behind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Shepherd Oak, and how\xe2\x80\x99s lambing this year, if I mid say it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ninquired Joseph Poorgrass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTerrible trying,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve been wet through twice a-day, either\r\nin snow or rain, this last fortnight. Cainy and I haven\xe2\x80\x99t tined our\r\neyes to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA good few twins, too, I hear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo many by half. Yes; \xe2\x80\x99tis a very queer lambing this year. We shan\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhave done by Lady Day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd last year \xe2\x80\x99twer all over by Sexajessamine Sunday,\xe2\x80\x9d Joseph\r\nremarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBring on the rest Cain,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel, \xe2\x80\x9cand then run back to the ewes.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll follow you soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCainy Ball\xe2\x80\x94a cheery-faced young lad, with a small circular orifice by\r\nway of mouth, advanced and deposited two others, and retired as he was\r\nbidden. Joseph Poorgrass used to prent to Farmer James Everdene\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwaggons before you came, and \xe2\x80\x99a could never mind which way to turn the\r\nJ\xe2\x80\x99s and E\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94could ye, Joseph?\xe2\x80\x9d Joseph shook his head to express how\r\nabsolute was the fact that he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd so you used to do \xe2\x80\x99em the\r\nwrong way, like this, didn\xe2\x80\x99t ye, Joseph?\xe2\x80\x9d Matthew marked on the dusty\r\nfloor with his whip-handle\r\n\r\n[Illustration: The word J A M E S appears here with the \xe2\x80\x9cJ\xe2\x80\x9d, \xe2\x80\x9cE\xe2\x80\x9d, and\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cS\xe2\x80\x9d printed backwards]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how Farmer James would cuss, and call thee a fool, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t he,\r\nJoseph, when \xe2\x80\x99a seed his name looking so inside-out-like?\xe2\x80\x9d continued\r\nMatthew Moon with feeling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99a would,\xe2\x80\x9d said Joseph, meekly. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, you see, I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t so much to\r\nblame, for them J\xe2\x80\x99s and E\xe2\x80\x99s be such trying sons o\xe2\x80\x99 witches for the\r\nmemory to mind whether they face backward or forward; and I always had\r\nsuch a forgetful memory, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis a very bad affliction for ye, being such a man of calamities in\r\nother ways.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, \xe2\x80\x99tis; but a happy Providence ordered that it should be no worse,\r\nand I feel my thanks. Their\r\nnoise increased to a chorus of baas, upon which Oak pulled the milk-can\r\nfrom before the fire, and taking a small tea-pot from the pocket of his\r\nsmock-frock, filled it with milk, and taught those of the helpless\r\ncreatures which were not to be restored to their dams how to drink from\r\nthe spout\xe2\x80\x94a trick they acquired with astonishing aptitude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd she don\xe2\x80\x99t even let ye have the skins of the dead lambs, I hear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nresumed Joseph Poorgrass, his eyes lingering on the operations of Oak\r\nwith the necessary melancholy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t have them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe be very badly used, shepherd,\xe2\x80\x9d hazarded Joseph again, in the hope\r\nof getting Oak as an ally in lamentation after all. \xe2\x80\x9cI think she\xe2\x80\x99s took\r\nagainst ye\xe2\x80\x94that I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no\xe2\x80\x94not at all,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Gabriel, hastily, and a sigh escaped him,\r\nwhich the deprivation of lamb skins could hardly have caused.\r\n\r\nBefore any further remark had been added a shade darkened the door, and\r\nBoldwood entered the malthouse, bestowing upon each a nod of a quality\r\nbetween friendliness and condescension.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! Oak, I thought you were here,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI met the mail-cart ten\r\nminutes ago, and a letter was put into my hand, which I opened without\r\nreading the address. I believe it is yours. You must excuse the\r\naccident please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes\xe2\x80\x94not a bit of difference, Mr. Boldwood\xe2\x80\x94not a bit,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel,\r\nreadily. He had not a correspondent on earth, nor was there a possible\r\nletter coming to him whose contents the whole parish would not have\r\nbeen welcome to peruse.\r\n\r\nOak stepped aside, and read the following in an unknown hand:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nDEAR FRIEND,\xe2\x80\x94I do not know your name, but I think these few lines will\r\nreach you, which I wrote to thank you for your kindness to me the night\r\nI left Weatherbury in a reckless way. I also return the money I owe\r\nyou, which you will excuse my not keeping as a gift. All has ended\r\nwell, and I am happy to say I am going to be married to the young man\r\nwho has courted me for some time\xe2\x80\x94Sergeant Troy, of the 11th Dragoon\r\nGuards, now quartered in this town. He would, I know, object to my\r\nhaving received anything except as a loan, being a man of great\r\nrespectability and high honour\xe2\x80\x94indeed, a nobleman by blood.\r\n    I should be much obliged to you if you would keep the contents of\r\n    this letter a secret for the present, dear friend. We mean to\r\n    surprise Weatherbury by coming there soon as husband and wife,\r\n    though I blush to state it to one nearly a stranger. The sergeant\r\n    grew up in Weatherbury. Thanking you again for your kindness,\r\n\r\n\r\nI am, your sincere well-wisher,\r\nFANNY ROBIN.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you read it, Mr. Boldwood?\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel; \xe2\x80\x9cif not, you had better\r\ndo so. I see she gives no address.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat sort of a man is this Sergeant Troy?\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cH\xe2\x80\x99m\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid not one to build much hope upon in such a case as\r\nthis,\xe2\x80\x9d the farmer murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cthough he\xe2\x80\x99s a clever fellow, and up to\r\neverything. A slight romance attaches to him, too. His mother was a\r\nFrench governess, and it seems that a secret attachment existed between\r\nher and the late Lord Severn. She was married to a poor medical man,\r\nand soon after an infant was born; and while money was forthcoming all\r\nwent on well. Unfortunately for her boy, his best friends died; and he\r\ngot then a situation as second clerk at a lawyer\xe2\x80\x99s in Casterbridge. He\r\nstayed there for some time, and might have worked himself into a\r\ndignified position of some sort had he not indulged in the wild freak\r\nof enlisting. I have much doubt if ever little Fanny will surprise us\r\nin the way she mentions\xe2\x80\x94very much doubt. A silly girl!\xe2\x80\x94silly girl!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe door was hurriedly burst open again, and in came running Cainy Ball\r\nout of breath, his mouth red and open, like the bell of a penny\r\ntrumpet, from which he coughed with noisy vigour and great distension\r\nof face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Cain Ball,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak, sternly, \xe2\x80\x9cwhy will you run so fast and lose\r\nyour breath so? Two more ewes have twinned\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter, Shepherd\r\nOak.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that\xe2\x80\x99s it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak, jumping up, and dimissing for the present\r\nhis thoughts on poor Fanny. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are a good boy to run and tell me,\r\nCain, and you shall smell a large plum pudding some day as a treat.\r\nBut, before we go, Cainy, bring the tarpot, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll mark this lot and\r\nhave done with \xe2\x80\x99em.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOak took from his illimitable pockets a marking iron, dipped it into\r\nthe pot, and imprinted on the buttocks of the infant sheep the initials\r\nof her he delighted to muse on\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cB. E.,\xe2\x80\x9d which signified to all the\r\nregion round that henceforth the lambs belonged to Farmer Bathsheba\r\nEverdene, and to no one else.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Cainy, shoulder your two, and off. Good morning, Mr. Boldwood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nThe shepherd lifted the sixteen large legs and four small bodies he had\r\nhimself brought, and vanished with them in the direction of the lambing\r\nfield hard by\xe2\x80\x94their frames being now in a sleek and hopeful state,\r\npleasantly contrasting with their death\xe2\x80\x99s-door plight of half an hour\r\nbefore.\r\n\r\nBoldwood followed him a little way up the field, hesitated, and turned\r\nback. He followed him again with a last resolve, annihilating return.\r\nOn approaching the nook in which the fold was constructed, the farmer\r\ndrew out his pocket-book, unfastened it, and allowed it to lie open on\r\nhis hand. A letter was revealed\xe2\x80\x94Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was going to ask you, Oak,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with unreal carelessness, \xe2\x80\x9cif\r\nyou know whose writing this is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOak glanced into the book, and replied instantly, with a flushed face,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Everdene\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOak had coloured simply at the consciousness of sounding her name. He\r\nnow felt a strangely distressing qualm from a new thought. The letter\r\ncould of course be no other than anonymous, or the inquiry would not\r\nhave been necessary.\r\n\r\nBoldwood mistook his confusion: sensitive persons are always ready with\r\ntheir \xe2\x80\x9cIs it I?\xe2\x80\x9d in preference to objective reasoning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe question was perfectly fair,\xe2\x80\x9d he returned\xe2\x80\x94and there was something\r\nincongruous in the serious earnestness with which he applied himself to\r\nan argument on a valentine. \xe2\x80\x9cYou know it is always expected that privy\r\ninquiries will be made: that\xe2\x80\x99s where the\xe2\x80\x94fun lies.\xe2\x80\x9d If the word \xe2\x80\x9cfun\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhad been \xe2\x80\x9ctorture,\xe2\x80\x9d it could not have been uttered with a more\r\nconstrained and restless countenance than was Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s then.\r\n\r\nSoon parting from Gabriel, the lonely and reserved man returned to his\r\nhouse to breakfast\xe2\x80\x94feeling twinges of shame and regret at having so far\r\nexposed his mood by those fevered questions to a stranger. He again\r\nplaced the letter on the mantelpiece, and sat down to think of the\r\ncircumstances attending it by the light of Gabriel\xe2\x80\x99s information.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVI\r\nALL SAINTS\xe2\x80\x99 AND ALL SOULS\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\nOn a week-day morning a small congregation, consisting mainly of women\r\nand girls, rose from its knees in the mouldy nave of a church called\r\nAll Saints\xe2\x80\x99, in the distant barrack-town before-mentioned, at the end\r\nof a service without a sermon. They were about to disperse, when a\r\nsmart footstep, entering the porch and coming up the central passage,\r\narrested their attention. The step echoed with a ring unusual in a\r\nchurch; it was the clink of spurs. Everybody looked. A young cavalry\r\nsoldier in a red uniform, with the three chevrons of a sergeant upon\r\nhis sleeve, strode up the aisle, with an embarrassment which was only\r\nthe more marked by the intense vigour of his step, and by the\r\ndetermination upon his face to show none. A slight flush had mounted\r\nhis cheek by the time he had run the gauntlet between these women; but,\r\npassing on through the chancel arch, he never paused till he came close\r\nto the altar railing. Here for a moment he stood alone.\r\n\r\nThe officiating curate, who had not yet doffed his surplice, perceived\r\nthe new-comer, and followed him to the communion-space. He whispered to\r\nthe soldier, and then beckoned to the clerk, who in his turn whispered\r\nto an elderly woman, apparently his wife, and they also went up the\r\nchancel steps.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis a wedding!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured some of the women, brightening. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwait!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe majority again sat down.\r\n\r\nThere was a creaking of machinery behind, and some of the young ones\r\nturned their heads. From the interior face of the west wall of the\r\ntower projected a little canopy with a quarter-jack and small bell\r\nbeneath it, the automaton being driven by the same clock machinery that\r\nstruck the large bell in the tower. Between the tower and the church\r\nwas a close screen, the door of which was kept shut during services,\r\nhiding this grotesque clockwork from sight. At present, however, the\r\ndoor was open, and the egress of the jack, the blows on the bell, and\r\nthe mannikin\xe2\x80\x99s retreat into the nook again, were visible to many, and\r\naudible throughout the church.\r\n\r\nThe jack had struck half-past eleven.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere\xe2\x80\x99s the woman?\xe2\x80\x9d whispered some of the spectators.\r\n\r\nThe young sergeant stood still with the abnormal rigidity of the old\r\npillars around. He faced the south-east, and was as silent as he was\r\nstill.\r\n\r\nThe silence grew to be a noticeable thing as the minutes went on, and\r\nnobody else appeared, and not a soul moved.
