And you should take their words\r\nfor it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey don\xe2\x80\x99t say so exactly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, they must!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I mean to my face, as you do,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on, allowing herself to\r\nbe further lured into a conversation that intention had rigorously\r\nforbidden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you know they think so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94that is\xe2\x80\x94I certainly have heard Liddy say they do, but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d She paused.\r\n\r\nCapitulation\xe2\x80\x94that was the purport of the simple reply, guarded as it\r\nwas\xe2\x80\x94capitulation, unknown to herself. Never did a fragile tailless\r\nsentence convey a more perfect meaning. The careless sergeant smiled\r\nwithin himself, and probably too the devil smiled from a loop-hole in\r\nTophet, for the moment was the turning-point of a career. Her tone and\r\nmien signified beyond mistake that the seed which was to lift the\r\nfoundation had taken root in the chink: the remainder was a mere\r\nquestion of time and natural changes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere the truth comes out!\xe2\x80\x9d said the soldier, in reply. \xe2\x80\x9cNever tell me\r\nthat a young lady can live in a buzz of admiration without knowing\r\nsomething about it. Ah, well, Miss Everdene, you are\xe2\x80\x94pardon my blunt\r\nway\xe2\x80\x94you are rather an injury to our race than otherwise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow\xe2\x80\x94indeed?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, opening her eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it is true enough. I may as well be hung for a sheep as a lamb (an\r\nold country saying, not of much account, but it will do for a rough\r\nsoldier), and so I will speak my mind, regardless of your pleasure, and\r\nwithout hoping or intending to get your pardon. Why, Miss Everdene, it\r\nis in this manner that your good looks may do more harm than good in\r\nthe world.\xe2\x80\x9d The sergeant looked down the mead in critical abstraction.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cProbably some one man on an average falls in love with each ordinary\r\nwoman. She can marry him: he is content, and leads a useful life. Such\r\nwomen as you a hundred men always covet\xe2\x80\x94your eyes will bewitch scores\r\non scores into an unavailing fancy for you\xe2\x80\x94you can only marry one of\r\nthat many. Out of these say twenty will endeavour to drown the\r\nbitterness of despised love in drink; twenty more will mope away their\r\nlives without a wish or attempt to make a mark in the world, because\r\nthey have no ambition apart from their attachment to you; twenty\r\nmore\xe2\x80\x94the susceptible person myself possibly among them\xe2\x80\x94will be always\r\ndraggling after you, getting where they may just see you, doing\r\ndesperate things. Men are such constant fools! The rest may try to get\r\nover their passion with more or less success. But all these men will be\r\nsaddened. And not only those ninety-nine men, but the ninety-nine women\r\nthey might have married are saddened with them. There\xe2\x80\x99s my tale. That\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhy I say that a woman so charming as yourself, Miss Everdene, is\r\nhardly a blessing to her race.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe handsome sergeant\xe2\x80\x99s features were during this speech as rigid and\r\nstern as John Knox\xe2\x80\x99s in addressing his gay young queen.\r\n\r\nSeeing she made no reply, he said, \xe2\x80\x9cDo you read French?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; I began, but when I got to the verbs, father died,\xe2\x80\x9d she said\r\nsimply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do\xe2\x80\x94when I have an opportunity, which latterly has not been often (my\r\nmother was a Parisienne)\xe2\x80\x94and there\xe2\x80\x99s a proverb they have, _Qui aime\r\nbien, ch\xc3\xa2tie bien_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98He chastens who loves well.\xe2\x80\x99 Do you understand me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d she replied, and there was even a little tremulousness in the\r\nusually cool girl\xe2\x80\x99s voice; \xe2\x80\x9cif you can only fight half as winningly as\r\nyou can talk, you are able to make a pleasure of a bayonet wound!\xe2\x80\x9d And\r\nthen poor Bathsheba instantly perceived her slip in making this\r\nadmission: in hastily trying to retrieve it, she went from bad to\r\nworse. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t, however, suppose that _I_ derive any pleasure from what\r\nyou tell me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know you do not\xe2\x80\x94I know it perfectly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy, with much hearty\r\nconviction on the exterior of his face: and altering the expression to\r\nmoodiness; \xe2\x80\x9cwhen a dozen men are ready to speak tenderly to you, and\r\ngive the admiration you deserve without adding the warning you need, it\r\nstands to reason that my poor rough-and-ready mixture of praise and\r\nblame cannot convey much pleasure. Fool as I may be, I am not so\r\nconceited as to suppose that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you\xe2\x80\x94are conceited, nevertheless,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, looking\r\naskance at a reed she was fitfully pulling with one hand, having lately\r\ngrown feverish under the soldier\xe2\x80\x99s system of procedure\xe2\x80\x94not because the\r\nnature of his cajolery was entirely unperceived, but because its vigour\r\nwas overwhelming.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would not own it to anybody else\xe2\x80\x94nor do I exactly to you. Still,\r\nthere might have been some self-conceit in my foolish supposition the\r\nother night. I knew that what I said in admiration might be an opinion\r\ntoo often forced upon you to give any pleasure, but I certainly did\r\nthink that the kindness of your nature might prevent you judging an\r\nuncontrolled tongue harshly\xe2\x80\x94which you have done\xe2\x80\x94and thinking badly of\r\nme and wounding me this morning, when I am working hard to save your\r\nhay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you need not think more of that: perhaps you did not mean to be\r\nrude to me by speaking out your mind: indeed, I believe you did not,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the shrewd woman, in painfully innocent earnest. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I thank you\r\nfor giving help here. But\xe2\x80\x94but mind you don\xe2\x80\x99t speak to me again in that\r\nway, or in any other, unless I speak to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Miss Bathsheba! That is too hard!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it isn\xe2\x80\x99t. Why is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will never speak to me; for I shall not be here long. I am soon\r\ngoing back again to the miserable monotony of drill\xe2\x80\x94and perhaps our\r\nregiment will be ordered out soon. And yet you take away the one little\r\newe-lamb of pleasure that I have in this dull life of mine. Well,\r\nperhaps generosity is not a woman\xe2\x80\x99s most marked characteristic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen are you going from here?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, with some interest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn a month.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how can it give you pleasure to speak to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you ask Miss Everdene\xe2\x80\x94knowing as you do\xe2\x80\x94what my offence is based\r\non?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you do care so much for a silly trifle of that kind, then, I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmind doing it,\xe2\x80\x9d she uncertainly and doubtingly answered. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nreally care for a word from me? you only say so\xe2\x80\x94I think you only say\r\nso.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s unjust\xe2\x80\x94but I won\xe2\x80\x99t repeat the remark. I am too gratified to get\r\nsuch a mark of your friendship at any price to cavil at the tone. I\r\n_do_, Miss Everdene, care for it. You may think a man foolish to want a\r\nmere word\xe2\x80\x94just a good morning. Perhaps he is\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t know. But you have\r\nnever been a man looking upon a woman, and that woman yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you know nothing of what such an experience is like\xe2\x80\x94and Heaven\r\nforbid that you ever should!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense, flatterer! What is it like? I am interested in knowing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPut shortly, it is not being able to think, hear, or look in any\r\ndirection except one without wretchedness, nor there without torture.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, sergeant, it won\xe2\x80\x99t do\xe2\x80\x94you are pretending!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, shaking her\r\nhead. \xe2\x80\x9cYour words are too dashing to be true.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not, upon the honour of a soldier.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut _why_ is it so?\xe2\x80\x94Of course I ask for mere pastime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause you are so distracting\xe2\x80\x94and I am so distracted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou look like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am indeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you only saw me the other night!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat makes no difference. The lightning works instantaneously. I loved\r\nyou then, at once\xe2\x80\x94as I do now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba surveyed him curiously, from the feet upward, as high as she\r\nliked to venture her glance, which was not quite so high as his eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou cannot and you don\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d she said demurely. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is no such sudden\r\nfeeling in people. I won\xe2\x80\x99t listen to you any longer. Hear me, I wish I\r\nknew what o\xe2\x80\x99clock it is\xe2\x80\x94I am going\xe2\x80\x94I have wasted too much time here\r\nalready!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe sergeant looked at his watch and told her. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat, haven\xe2\x80\x99t you a\r\nwatch, miss?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not just at present\xe2\x80\x94I am about to get a new one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. You shall be given one. Yes\xe2\x80\x94you shall. A gift, Miss Everdene\xe2\x80\x94a\r\ngift.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd before she knew what the young man was intending, a heavy gold\r\nwatch was in her hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is an unusually good one for a man like me to possess,\xe2\x80\x9d he quietly\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cThat watch has a history. Press the spring and open the back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe did so.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you see?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA crest and a motto.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA coronet with five points, and beneath, _Cedit amor rebus_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98Love\r\nyields to circumstance.\xe2\x80\x99 It\xe2\x80\x99s the motto of the Earls of Severn. That\r\nwatch belonged to the last lord, and was given to my mother\xe2\x80\x99s husband,\r\na medical man, for his use till I came of age, when it was to be given\r\nto me. It was all the fortune that ever I inherited. That watch has\r\nregulated imperial interests in its time\xe2\x80\x94the stately ceremonial, the\r\ncourtly assignation, pompous travels, and lordly sleeps. Now it is\r\nyours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, Sergeant Troy, I cannot take this\xe2\x80\x94I cannot!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, with\r\nround-eyed wonder. \xe2\x80\x9cA gold watch! What are you doing? Don\xe2\x80\x99t be such a\r\ndissembler!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe sergeant retreated to avoid receiving back his gift, which she held\r\nout persistently towards him. Bathsheba followed as he retired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKeep it\xe2\x80\x94do, Miss Everdene\xe2\x80\x94keep it!\xe2\x80\x9d said the erratic child of impulse.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe fact of your possessing it makes it worth ten times as much to me.\r\nA more plebeian one will answer my purpose just as well, and the\r\npleasure of knowing whose heart my old one beats against\xe2\x80\x94well, I won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nspeak of that. It is in far worthier hands than ever it has been in\r\nbefore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut indeed I can\xe2\x80\x99t have it!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, in a perfect simmer of\r\ndistress. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, how can you do such a thing; that is if you really mean\r\nit! Give me your dead father\xe2\x80\x99s watch, and such a valuable one! You\r\nshould not be so reckless, indeed, Sergeant Troy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI loved my father: good; but better, I love you more. That\xe2\x80\x99s how I can\r\ndo it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the sergeant, with an intonation of such exquisite\r\nfidelity to nature that it was evidently not all acted now. Her beauty,\r\nwhich, whilst it had been quiescent, he had praised in jest, had in its\r\nanimated phases moved him to earnest; and though his seriousness was\r\nless than she imagined, it was probably more than he imagined himself.\r\n\r\nBathsheba was brimming with agitated bewilderment, and she said, in\r\nhalf-suspicious accents of feeling, \xe2\x80\x9cCan it be! Oh, how can it be, that\r\nyou care for me, and so suddenly! You have seen so little of me: I may\r\nnot be really so\xe2\x80\x94so nice-looking as I seem to you. Please, do take it;\r\nOh, do! I cannot and will not have it. Believe me, your generosity is\r\ntoo great. I have never done you a single kindness, and why should you\r\nbe so kind to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA factitious reply had been again upon his lips, but it was again\r\nsuspended, and he looked at her with an arrested eye. The truth was,\r\nthat as she now stood\xe2\x80\x94excited, wild, and honest as the day\xe2\x80\x94her alluring\r\nbeauty bore out so fully the epithets he had bestowed upon it that he\r\nwas quite startled at his temerity in advancing them as false. He said\r\nmechanically, \xe2\x80\x9cAh, why?\xe2\x80\x9d and continued to look at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd my workfolk see me following you about the field, and are\r\nwondering. Oh, this is dreadful!\xe2\x80\x9d she went on, unconscious of the\r\ntransmutation she was effecting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not quite mean you to accept it at first, for it was my one poor\r\npatent of nobility,\xe2\x80\x9d he broke out, bluntly; \xe2\x80\x9cbut, upon my soul, I wish\r\nyou would now. Without any shamming, come! Don\xe2\x80\x99t deny me the happiness\r\nof wearing it for my sake? But you are too lovely even to care to be\r\nkind as others are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no; don\xe2\x80\x99t say so! I have reasons for reserve which I cannot\r\nexplain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet it be, then, let it be,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, receiving back the watch at\r\nlast; \xe2\x80\x9cI must be leaving you now. And will you speak to me for these\r\nfew weeks of my stay?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed I will. Yet, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know if I will! Oh, why did you come and\r\ndisturb me so!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps in setting a gin, I have caught myself. Such things have\r\nhappened. Well, will you let me work in your fields?\xe2\x80\x9d he coaxed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I suppose so; if it is any pleasure to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Everdene, I thank you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe sergeant brought his hand to the cap on the slope of his head,\r\nsaluted, and returned to the distant group of haymakers.\r\n\r\nBathsheba could not face the haymakers now. Her heart erratically\r\nflitting hither and thither from perplexed excitement, hot, and almost\r\ntearful, she retreated homeward, murmuring, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, what have I done! What\r\ndoes it mean! I wish I knew how much of it was true!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVII\r\nHIVING THE BEES\r\n\r\n\r\nThe Weatherbury bees were late in their swarming this year. It was in\r\nthe latter part of June, and the day after the interview with Troy in\r\nthe hayfield, that Bathsheba was standing in her garden, watching a\r\nswarm in the air and guessing their probable settling place. Not only\r\nwere they late this year, but unruly. Sometimes throughout a whole\r\nseason all the swarms would alight on the lowest attainable bough\xe2\x80\x94such\r\nas part of a currant-bush or espalier apple-tree; next year they would,\r\nwith just the same unanimity, make straight off to the uppermost member\r\nof some tall, gaunt costard, or quarrenden, and there defy all invaders\r\nwho did not come armed with ladders and staves to take them.\r\n\r\nThis was the case at present. Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, shaded by one hand,\r\nwere following the ascending multitude against the unexplorable stretch\r\nof blue till they ultimately halted by one of the unwieldy trees spoken\r\nof. A process somewhat analogous to that of alleged formations of the\r\nuniverse, time and times ago, was observable. The bustling swarm had\r\nswept the sky in a scattered and uniform haze, which now thickened to a\r\nnebulous centre: this glided on to a bough and grew still denser, till\r\nit formed a solid black spot upon the light.\r\n\r\nThe men and women being all busily engaged in saving the hay\xe2\x80\x94even Liddy\r\nhad left the house for the purpose of lending a hand\xe2\x80\x94Bathsheba resolved\r\nto hive the bees herself, if possible. She had dressed the hive with\r\nherbs and honey, fetched a ladder, brush, and crook, made herself\r\nimpregnable with armour of leather gloves, straw hat, and large gauze\r\nveil\xe2\x80\x94once green but now faded to snuff colour\xe2\x80\x94and ascended a dozen\r\nrungs of the ladder. At once she heard, not ten yards off, a voice that\r\nwas beginning to have a strange power in agitating her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Everdene, let me assist you; you should not attempt such a thing\r\nalone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTroy was just opening the garden gate.\r\n\r\nBathsheba flung down the brush, crook, and empty hive, pulled the skirt\r\nof her dress tightly round her ankles in a tremendous flurry, and as\r\nwell as she could slid down the ladder. By the time she reached the\r\nbottom Troy was there also, and he stooped to pick up the hive.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow fortunate I am to have dropped in at this moment!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the\r\nsergeant.\r\n\r\nShe found her voice in a minute. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat! and will you shake them in for\r\nme?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, in what, for a defiant girl, was a faltering way;\r\nthough, for a timid girl, it would have seemed a brave way enough.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill I!\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, of course I will. How blooming you are\r\nto-day!\xe2\x80\x9d Troy flung down his cane and put his foot on the ladder to\r\nascend.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you must have on the veil and gloves, or you\xe2\x80\x99ll be stung\r\nfearfully!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, yes. I must put on the veil and gloves. Will you kindly show me\r\nhow to fix them properly?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you must have the broad-brimmed hat, too, for your cap has no brim\r\nto keep the veil off, and they\xe2\x80\x99d reach your face.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe broad-brimmed hat, too, by all means.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo a whimsical fate ordered that her hat should be taken off\xe2\x80\x94veil and\r\nall attached\xe2\x80\x94and placed upon his head, Troy tossing his own into a\r\ngooseberry bush. Then the veil had to be tied at its lower edge round\r\nhis collar and the gloves put on him.\r\n\r\nHe looked such an extraordinary object in this guise that, flurried as\r\nshe was, she could not avoid laughing outright. It was the removal of\r\nyet another stake from the palisade of cold manners which had kept him\r\noff.\r\n\r\nBathsheba looked on from the ground whilst he was busy sweeping and\r\nshaking the bees from the tree, holding up the hive with the other hand\r\nfor them to fall into. She made use of an unobserved minute whilst his\r\nattention was absorbed in the operation to arrange her plumes a little.\r\nHe came down holding the hive at arm\xe2\x80\x99s length, behind which trailed a\r\ncloud of bees.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon my life,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy, through the veil, \xe2\x80\x9cholding up this hive\r\nmakes one\xe2\x80\x99s arm ache worse than a week of sword-exercise.\xe2\x80\x9d When the\r\nman\xc5\x93uvre was complete he approached her. \xe2\x80\x9cWould you be good enough to\r\nuntie me and let me out? I am nearly stifled inside this silk cage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo hide her embarrassment during the unwonted process of untying the\r\nstring about his neck, she said:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have never seen that you spoke of.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe sword-exercise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! would you like to?\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy.\r\n\r\nBathsheba hesitated. She had heard wondrous reports from time to time\r\nby dwellers in Weatherbury, who had by chance sojourned awhile in\r\nCasterbridge, near the barracks, of this strange and glorious\r\nperformance, the sword-exercise. Men and boys who had peeped through\r\nchinks or over walls into the barrack-yard returned with accounts of\r\nits being the most flashing affair conceivable; accoutrements and\r\nweapons glistening like stars\xe2\x80\x94here, there, around\xe2\x80\x94yet all by rule and\r\ncompass. So she said mildly what she felt strongly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; I should like to see it very much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so you shall; you shall see me go through it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo! How?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me consider.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot with a walking-stick\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t care to see that. It must be a real\r\nsword.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know; and I have no sword here; but I think I could get one by\r\nthe evening. Now, will you do this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTroy bent over her and murmured some suggestion in a low voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, blushing. \xe2\x80\x9cThank you very much, but I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t on any account.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely you might? Nobody would know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe shook her head, but with a weakened negation. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I were to,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cI must bring Liddy too. Might I not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTroy looked far away. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see why you want to bring her,\xe2\x80\x9d he said\r\ncoldly.\r\n\r\nAn unconscious look of assent in Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s eyes betrayed that\r\nsomething more than his coldness had made her also feel that Liddy\r\nwould be superfluous in the suggested scene. She had felt it, even\r\nwhilst making the proposal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I won\xe2\x80\x99t bring Liddy\xe2\x80\x94and I\xe2\x80\x99ll come. But only for a very short\r\ntime,\xe2\x80\x9d she added; \xe2\x80\x9ca very short time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will not take five minutes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVIII\r\nTHE HOLLOW AMID THE FERNS\r\n\r\n\r\nThe hill opposite Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s dwelling extended, a mile off, into an\r\nuncultivated tract of land, dotted at this season with tall thickets of\r\nbrake fern, plump and diaphanous from recent rapid growth, and radiant\r\nin hues of clear and untainted green.\r\n\r\nAt eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock this midsummer evening, whilst the bristling ball of\r\ngold in the west still swept the tips of the ferns with its long,\r\nluxuriant rays, a soft brushing-by of garments might have been heard\r\namong them, and Bathsheba appeared in their midst, their soft, feathery\r\narms caressing her up to her shoulders. She paused, turned, went back\r\nover the hill and half-way to her own door, whence she cast a farewell\r\nglance upon the spot she had just left, having resolved not to remain\r\nnear the place after all.\r\n\r\nShe saw a dim spot of artificial red moving round the shoulder of the\r\nrise. It disappeared on the other side.\r\n\r\nShe waited one minute\xe2\x80\x94two minutes\xe2\x80\x94thought of Troy\xe2\x80\x99s disappointment at\r\nher non-fulfilment of a promised engagement, till she again ran along\r\nthe field, clambered over the bank, and followed the original\r\ndirection. She was now literally trembling and panting at this her\r\ntemerity in such an errant undertaking; her breath came and went\r\nquickly, and her eyes shone with an infrequent light. Yet go she must.\r\nShe reached the verge of a pit in the middle of the ferns. Troy stood\r\nin the bottom, looking up towards her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI heard you rustling through the fern before I saw you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\ncoming up and giving her his hand to help her down the slope.\r\n\r\nThe pit was a saucer-shaped concave, naturally formed, with a top\r\ndiameter of about thirty feet, and shallow enough to allow the sunshine\r\nto reach their heads. Standing in the centre, the sky overhead was met\r\nby a circular horizon of fern: this grew nearly to the bottom of the\r\nslope and then abruptly ceased. The middle within the belt of verdure\r\nwas floored with a thick flossy carpet of moss and grass intermingled,\r\nso yielding that the foot was half-buried within it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy, producing the sword, which, as he raised it into the\r\nsunlight, gleamed a sort of greeting, like a living thing, \xe2\x80\x9cfirst, we\r\nhave four right and four left cuts; four right and four left thrusts.\r\nInfantry cuts and guards are more interesting than ours, to my mind;\r\nbut they are not so swashing. They have seven cuts and three thrusts.\r\nSo much as a preliminary. Well, next, our cut one is as if you were\r\nsowing your corn\xe2\x80\x94so.\xe2\x80\x9d Bathsheba saw a sort of rainbow, upside down in\r\nthe air, and Troy\xe2\x80\x99s arm was still again. \xe2\x80\x9cCut two, as if you were\r\nhedging\xe2\x80\x94so. Three, as if you were reaping\xe2\x80\x94so. Four, as if you were\r\nthreshing\xe2\x80\x94in that way. Then the same on the left. The thrusts are\r\nthese: one, two, three, four, right; one, two, three, four, left.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nrepeated them. \xe2\x80\x9cHave \xe2\x80\x99em again?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cOne, two\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe hurriedly interrupted: \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d rather not; though I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind your\r\ntwos and fours; but your ones and threes are terrible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well. I\xe2\x80\x99ll let you off the ones and threes. Next, cuts, points\r\nand guards altogether.\xe2\x80\x9d Troy duly exhibited them. \xe2\x80\x9cThen there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npursuing practice, in this way.\xe2\x80\x9d He gave the movements as before.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, those are the stereotyped forms. The infantry have two most\r\ndiabolical upward cuts, which we are too humane to use. Like\r\nthis\xe2\x80\x94three, four.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow murderous and bloodthirsty!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are rather deathly. Now I\xe2\x80\x99ll be more interesting, and let you see\r\nsome loose play\xe2\x80\x94giving all the cuts and points, infantry and cavalry,\r\nquicker than lightning, and as promiscuously\xe2\x80\x94with just enough rule to\r\nregulate instinct and yet not to fetter it. You are my antagonist, with\r\nthis difference from real warfare, that I shall miss you every time by\r\none hair\xe2\x80\x99s breadth, or perhaps two. Mind you don\xe2\x80\x99t flinch, whatever you\r\ndo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll be sure not to!\xe2\x80\x9d she said invincibly.\r\n\r\nHe pointed to about a yard in front of him.\r\n\r\nBathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s adventurous spirit was beginning to find some grains of\r\nrelish in these highly novel proceedings. She took up her position as\r\ndirected, facing Troy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow just to learn whether you have pluck enough to let me do what I\r\nwish, I\xe2\x80\x99ll give you a preliminary test.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe flourished the sword by way of introduction number two, and the next\r\nthing of which she was conscious was that the point and blade of the\r\nsword were darting with a gleam towards her left side, just above her\r\nhip; then of their reappearance on her right side, emerging as it were\r\nfrom between her ribs, having apparently passed through her body. The\r\nthird item of consciousness was that of seeing the same sword,\r\nperfectly clean and free from blood held vertically in Troy\xe2\x80\x99s hand (in\r\nthe position technically called \xe2\x80\x9crecover swords\xe2\x80\x9d). All was as quick as\r\nelectricity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried out in affright, pressing her hand to her side. \xe2\x80\x9cHave\r\nyou run me through?\xe2\x80\x94no, you have not! Whatever have you done!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not touched you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy, quietly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was mere sleight of\r\nhand. The sword passed behind you. Now you are not afraid, are you?\r\nBecause if you are I can\xe2\x80\x99t perform. I give my word that I will not only\r\nnot hurt you, but not once touch you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I am afraid. You are quite sure you will not hurt me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs the sword very sharp?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO no\xe2\x80\x94only stand as still as a statue. Now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn an instant the atmosphere was transformed to Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s eyes. Beams\r\nof light caught from the low sun\xe2\x80\x99s rays, above, around, in front of\r\nher, well-nigh shut out earth and heaven\xe2\x80\x94all emitted in the marvellous\r\nevolutions of Troy\xe2\x80\x99s reflecting blade, which seemed everywhere at once,\r\nand yet nowhere specially. These circling gleams were accompanied by a\r\nkeen rush that was almost a whistling\xe2\x80\x94also springing from all sides of\r\nher at once. In short, she was enclosed in a firmament of light, and of\r\nsharp hisses, resembling a sky-full of meteors close at hand.\r\n\r\nNever since the broadsword became the national weapon had there been\r\nmore dexterity shown in its management than by the hands of Sergeant\r\nTroy, and never had he been in such splendid temper for the performance\r\nas now in the evening sunshine among the ferns with Bathsheba. It may\r\nsafely be asserted with respect to the closeness of his cuts, that had\r\nit been possible for the edge of the sword to leave in the air a\r\npermanent substance wherever it flew past, the space left untouched\r\nwould have been almost a mould of Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s figure.\r\n\r\nBehind the luminous streams of this _aurora militaris_, she could see\r\nthe hue of Troy\xe2\x80\x99s sword arm, spread in a scarlet haze over the space\r\ncovered by its motions, like a twanged harpstring, and behind all Troy\r\nhimself, mostly facing her; sometimes, to show the rear cuts, half\r\nturned away, his eye nevertheless always keenly measuring her breadth\r\nand outline, and his lips tightly closed in sustained effort. Next, his\r\nmovements lapsed slower, and she could see them individually. The\r\nhissing of the sword had ceased, and he stopped entirely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat outer loose lock of hair wants tidying,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, before she had\r\nmoved or spoken. \xe2\x80\x9cWait: I\xe2\x80\x99ll do it for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAn arc of silver shone on her right side: the sword had descended. The\r\nlock dropped to the ground.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBravely borne!\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy. \xe2\x80\x9cYou didn\xe2\x80\x99t flinch a shade\xe2\x80\x99s thickness.\r\nWonderful in a woman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was because I didn\xe2\x80\x99t expect it. Oh, you have spoilt my hair!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly once more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94no! I am afraid of you\xe2\x80\x94indeed I am!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t touch you at all\xe2\x80\x94not even your hair. I am only going to kill\r\nthat caterpillar settling on you. Now: still!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt appeared that a caterpillar had come from the fern and chosen the\r\nfront of her bodice as his resting place. She saw the point glisten\r\ntowards her bosom, and seemingly enter it. Bathsheba closed her eyes in\r\nthe full persuasion that she was killed at last. However, feeling just\r\nas usual, she opened them again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere it is, look,\xe2\x80\x9d said the sergeant, holding his sword before her\r\neyes.\r\n\r\nThe caterpillar was spitted upon its point.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it is magic!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, amazed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no\xe2\x80\x94dexterity. I merely gave point to your bosom where the\r\ncaterpillar was, and instead of running you through checked the\r\nextension a thousandth of an inch short of your surface.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how could you chop off a curl of my hair with a sword that has no\r\nedge?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo edge! This sword will shave like a razor. Look here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe touched the palm of his hand with the blade, and then, lifting it,\r\nshowed her a thin shaving of scarf-skin dangling therefrom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you said before beginning that it was blunt and couldn\xe2\x80\x99t cut me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was to get you to stand still, and so make sure of your safety.\r\nThe risk of injuring you through your moving was too great not to force\r\nme to tell you a fib to escape it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe shuddered. \xe2\x80\x9cI have been within an inch of my life, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nit!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore precisely speaking, you have been within half an inch of being\r\npared alive two hundred and ninety-five times.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCruel, cruel, \xe2\x80\x99tis of you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have been perfectly safe, nevertheless. My sword never errs.\xe2\x80\x9d And\r\nTroy returned the weapon to the scabbard.\r\n\r\nBathsheba, overcome by a hundred tumultuous feelings resulting from the\r\nscene, abstractedly sat down on a tuft of heather.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must leave you now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy, softly. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x99ll venture to take\r\nand keep this in remembrance of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe saw him stoop to the grass, pick up the winding lock which he had\r\nsevered from her manifold tresses, twist it round his fingers, unfasten\r\na button in the breast of his coat, and carefully put it inside. She\r\nfelt powerless to withstand or deny him. He was altogether too much for\r\nher, and Bathsheba seemed as one who, facing a reviving wind, finds it\r\nblow so strongly that it stops the breath. He drew near and said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nmust be leaving you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe drew nearer still. A minute later and she saw his scarlet form\r\ndisappear amid the ferny thicket, almost in a flash, like a brand\r\nswiftly waved.\r\n\r\nThat minute\xe2\x80\x99s interval had brought the blood beating into her face, set\r\nher stinging as if aflame to the very hollows of her feet, and enlarged\r\nemotion to a compass which quite swamped thought. It had brought upon\r\nher a stroke resulting, as did that of Moses in Horeb, in a liquid\r\nstream\xe2\x80\x94here a stream of tears. She felt like one who has sinned a great\r\nsin.\r\n\r\nThe circumstance had been the gentle dip of Troy\xe2\x80\x99s mouth downwards upon\r\nher own. He had kissed her.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIX\r\nPARTICULARS OF A TWILIGHT WALK\r\n\r\n\r\nWe now see the element of folly distinctly mingling with the many\r\nvarying particulars which made up the character of Bathsheba Everdene.\r\nIt was almost foreign to her intrinsic nature. Introduced as lymph on\r\nthe dart of Eros, it eventually permeated and coloured her whole\r\nconstitution. Bathsheba, though she had too much understanding to be\r\nentirely governed by her womanliness, had too much womanliness to use\r\nher understanding to the best advantage. Perhaps in no minor point does\r\nwoman astonish her helpmate more than in the strange power she\r\npossesses of believing cajoleries that she knows to be false\xe2\x80\x94except,\r\nindeed, in that of being utterly sceptical on strictures that she knows\r\nto be true.\r\n\r\nBathsheba loved Troy in the way that only self-reliant women love when\r\nthey abandon their self-reliance. When a strong woman recklessly throws\r\naway her strength she is worse than a weak woman who has never had any\r\nstrength to throw away. One source of her inadequacy is the novelty of\r\nthe occasion. She has never had practice in making the best of such a\r\ncondition. Weakness is doubly weak by being new.\r\n\r\nBathsheba was not conscious of guile in this matter. Though in one\r\nsense a woman of the world, it was, after all, that world of daylight\r\ncoteries and green carpets wherein cattle form the passing crowd and\r\nwinds the busy hum; where a quiet family of rabbits or hares lives on\r\nthe other side of your party-wall, where your neighbour is everybody in\r\nthe tything, and where calculation is confined to market-days. Of the\r\nfabricated tastes of good fashionable society she knew but little, and\r\nof the formulated self-indulgence of bad, nothing at all. Had her\r\nutmost thoughts in this direction been distinctly worded (and by\r\nherself they never were), they would only have amounted to such a\r\nmatter as that she felt her impulses to be pleasanter guides than her\r\ndiscretion. Her love was entire as a child\xe2\x80\x99s, and though warm as summer\r\nit was fresh as spring. Her culpability lay in her making no attempt to\r\ncontrol feeling by subtle and careful inquiry into consequences. She\r\ncould show others the steep and thorny way, but \xe2\x80\x9creck\xe2\x80\x99d not her own\r\nrede.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Troy\xe2\x80\x99s deformities lay deep down from a woman\xe2\x80\x99s vision, whilst his\r\nembellishments were upon the very surface; thus contrasting with homely\r\nOak, whose defects were patent to the blindest, and whose virtues were\r\nas metals in a mine.\r\n\r\nThe difference between love and respect was markedly shown in her\r\nconduct. Bathsheba had spoken of her interest in Boldwood with the\r\ngreatest freedom to Liddy, but she had only communed with her own heart\r\nconcerning Troy.\r\n\r\nAll this infatuation Gabriel saw, and was troubled thereby from the\r\ntime of his daily journey a-field to the time of his return, and on to\r\nthe small hours of many a night. That he was not beloved had hitherto\r\nbeen his great sorrow; that Bathsheba was getting into the toils was\r\nnow a sorrow greater than the first, and one which nearly obscured it.\r\nIt was a result which paralleled the oft-quoted observation of\r\nHippocrates concerning physical pains.\r\n\r\nThat is a noble though perhaps an unpromising love which not even the\r\nfear of breeding aversion in the bosom of the one beloved can deter\r\nfrom combating his or her errors. Oak determined to speak to his\r\nmistress. He would base his appeal on what he considered her unfair\r\ntreatment of Farmer Boldwood, now absent from home.\r\n\r\nAn opportunity occurred one evening when she had gone for a short walk\r\nby a path through the neighbouring cornfields. It was dusk when Oak,\r\nwho had not been far a-field that day, took the same path and met her\r\nreturning, quite pensively, as he thought.\r\n\r\nThe wheat was now tall, and the path was narrow; thus the way was quite\r\na sunken groove between the embowing thicket on either side. Two\r\npersons could not walk abreast without damaging the crop, and Oak stood\r\naside to let her pass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, is it Gabriel?\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are taking a walk too. Good-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought I would come to meet you, as it is rather late,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak,\r\nturning and following at her heels when she had brushed somewhat\r\nquickly by him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, indeed, but I am not very fearful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no; but there are bad characters about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never meet them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow Oak, with marvellous ingenuity, had been going to introduce the\r\ngallant sergeant through the channel of \xe2\x80\x9cbad characters.\xe2\x80\x9d But all at\r\nonce the scheme broke down, it suddenly occurring to him that this was\r\nrather a clumsy way, and too barefaced to begin with. He tried another\r\npreamble.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd as the man who would naturally come to meet you is away from home,\r\ntoo\xe2\x80\x94I mean Farmer Boldwood\xe2\x80\x94why, thinks I, I\xe2\x80\x99ll go,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, yes.\xe2\x80\x9d She walked on without turning her head, and for many steps\r\nnothing further was heard from her quarter than the rustle of her dress\r\nagainst the heavy corn-ears. Then she resumed rather tartly\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t quite understand what you meant by saying that Mr. Boldwood\r\nwould naturally come to meet me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI meant on account of the wedding which they say is likely to take\r\nplace between you and him, miss. Forgive my speaking plainly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey say what is not true.\xe2\x80\x9d she returned quickly. \xe2\x80\x9cNo marriage is\r\nlikely to take place between us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGabriel now put forth his unobscured opinion, for the moment had come.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Miss Everdene,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cputting aside what people say, I never\r\nin my life saw any courting if his is not a courting of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba would probably have terminated the conversation there and\r\nthen by flatly forbidding the subject, had not her conscious weakness\r\nof position allured her to palter and argue in endeavours to better it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSince this subject has been mentioned,\xe2\x80\x9d she said very emphatically, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nam glad of the opportunity of clearing up a mistake which is very\r\ncommon and very provoking. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t definitely promise Mr. Boldwood\r\nanything. I have never cared for him. I respect him, and he has urged\r\nme to marry him. But I have given him no distinct answer. As soon as he\r\nreturns I shall do so; and the answer will be that I cannot think of\r\nmarrying him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeople are full of mistakes, seemingly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe other day they said you were trifling with him, and you almost\r\nproved that you were not; lately they have said that you be not, and\r\nyou straightway begin to show\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat I am, I suppose you mean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I hope they speak the truth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey do, but wrongly applied. I don\xe2\x80\x99t trifle with him; but then, I\r\nhave nothing to do with him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOak was unfortunately led on to speak of Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s rival in a wrong\r\ntone to her after all. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish you had never met that young Sergeant\r\nTroy, miss,\xe2\x80\x9d he sighed.\r\n\r\nBathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s steps became faintly spasmodic. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is not good enough for \xe2\x80\x99ee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid any one tell you to speak to me like this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNobody at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen it appears to me that Sergeant Troy does not concern us here,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe said, intractably. \xe2\x80\x9cYet I must say that Sergeant Troy is an\r\neducated man, and quite worthy of any woman. He is well born.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis being higher in learning and birth than the ruck o\xe2\x80\x99 soldiers is\r\nanything but a proof of his worth. It show\xe2\x80\x99s his course to be\r\ndown\xe2\x80\x99ard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot see what this has to do with our conversation. I don\xe2\x80\x99t say he\xe2\x80\x99s such a bad man as I have fancied\xe2\x80\x94I pray to\r\nGod he is not. Don\xe2\x80\x99t trust\r\nhim, mistress; I ask you not to trust him so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, pray?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like soldiers, but this one I do not like,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, sturdily. \xe2\x80\x9cHis\r\ncleverness in his calling may have tempted him astray, and what is\r\nmirth to the neighbours is ruin to the woman. When he tries to talk to\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ee again, why not turn away with a short \xe2\x80\x98Good day\xe2\x80\x99; and when you see\r\nhim coming one way, turn the other. When he says anything laughable,\r\nfail to see the point and don\xe2\x80\x99t smile, and speak of him before those\r\nwho will report your talk as \xe2\x80\x98that fantastical man,\xe2\x80\x99 or \xe2\x80\x98that Sergeant\r\nWhat\xe2\x80\x99s-his-name.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98That man of a family that has come to the dogs.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nDon\xe2\x80\x99t be unmannerly towards en, but harmless-uncivil, and so get rid of\r\nthe man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo Christmas robin detained by a window-pane ever pulsed as did\r\nBathsheba now.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say\xe2\x80\x94I say again\xe2\x80\x94that it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t become you to talk about him. Why he\r\nshould be mentioned passes me quite!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed desperately. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nknow this, th-th-that he is a thoroughly conscientious man\xe2\x80\x94blunt\r\nsometimes even to rudeness\xe2\x80\x94but always speaking his mind about you plain\r\nto your face!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is as good as anybody in this parish! He told me so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis supreme instance of Troy\xe2\x80\x99s goodness fell upon Gabriel ears like\r\nthe thirteenth stroke of crazy clock. It was not only received with\r\nutter incredulity as regarded itself, but threw a doubt on all the\r\nassurances that had preceded it.\r\n\r\nOak was grieved to find how entirely she trusted him. He brimmed with\r\ndeep feeling as he replied in a steady voice, the steadiness of which\r\nwas spoilt by the palpableness of his great effort to keep it so:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know, mistress, that I love you, and shall love you always. You shall go, sir\xe2\x80\x94your lecturing I will not hear! I am\r\nmistress here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo, indeed\xe2\x80\x94what folly will you say next? Treating me like Dick, Tom\r\nand Harry when you know that a short time ago my position was as good\r\nas yours! Upon my life, Bathsheba, it is too barefaced. You know, too,\r\nthat I can\xe2\x80\x99t go without putting things in such a strait as you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nget out of I can\xe2\x80\x99t tell when. I\xe2\x80\x99ll go at once\r\nif you\xe2\x80\x99ll promise that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall have no bailiff; I shall continue to be my own manager,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid decisively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then; you should be thankful to me for biding. How would\r\nthe farm go on with nobody to mind it but a woman? But mind this, I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t wish \xe2\x80\x99ee to feel you owe me anything. Not I. What I do, I do.\r\nSometimes I say I should be as glad as a bird to leave the place\xe2\x80\x94for\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t suppose I\xe2\x80\x99m content to be a nobody. But you\r\nknow well enough how it is, and who she is that I like too well, and\r\nfeel too much like a fool about to be civil to her!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt is more than probable that she privately and unconsciously respected\r\nhim a little for this grim fidelity, which had been shown in his tone\r\neven more than in his words. At any rate she murmured something to the\r\neffect that he might stay if he wished. She said more distinctly, \xe2\x80\x9cWill\r\nyou leave me alone now? I don\xe2\x80\x99t order it as a mistress\xe2\x80\x94I ask it as a\r\nwoman, and I expect you not to be so uncourteous as to refuse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly I will, Miss Everdene,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel, gently. He wondered\r\nthat the request should have come at this moment, for the strife was\r\nover, and they were on a most desolate hill, far from every human\r\nhabitation, and the hour was getting late. He stood still and allowed\r\nher to get far ahead of him till he could only see her form upon the\r\nsky.\r\n\r\nA distressing explanation of this anxiety to be rid of him at that\r\npoint now ensued. A figure apparently rose from the earth beside her.\r\nThe shape beyond all doubt was Troy\xe2\x80\x99s. Oak would not be even a possible\r\nlistener, and at once turned back till a good two hundred yards were\r\nbetween the lovers and himself.\r\n\r\nGabriel went home by way of the churchyard. In passing the tower he\r\nthought of what she had said about the sergeant\xe2\x80\x99s virtuous habit of\r\nentering the church unperceived at the beginning of service. Believing\r\nthat the little gallery door alluded to was quite disused, he ascended\r\nthe external flight of steps at the top of which it stood, and examined\r\nit. The pale lustre yet hanging in the north-western heaven was\r\nsufficient to show that a sprig of ivy had grown from the wall across\r\nthe door to a length of more than a foot, delicately tying the panel to\r\nthe stone jamb. It was a decisive proof that the door had not been\r\nopened at least since Troy came back to Weatherbury.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXX\r\nHOT CHEEKS AND TEARFUL EYES\r\n\r\n\r\nHalf an hour later Bathsheba entered her own house. There burnt upon\r\nher face when she met the light of the candles the flush and excitement\r\nwhich were little less than chronic with her now. The farewell words of\r\nTroy, who had accompanied her to the very door, still lingered in her\r\nears. He had bidden her adieu for two days, which were, so he stated,\r\nto be spent at Bath in visiting some friends. He had also kissed her a\r\nsecond time.\r\n\r\nIt is only fair to Bathsheba to explain here a little fact which did\r\nnot come to light till a long time afterwards: that Troy\xe2\x80\x99s presentation\r\nof himself so aptly at the roadside this evening was not by any\r\ndistinctly preconcerted arrangement. He had hinted\xe2\x80\x94she had forbidden;\r\nand it was only on the chance of his still coming that she had\r\ndismissed Oak, fearing a meeting between them just then.\r\n\r\nShe now sank down into a chair, wild and perturbed by all these new and\r\nfevering sequences. Then she jumped up with a manner of decision, and\r\nfetched her desk from a side table.\r\n\r\nIn three minutes, without pause or modification, she had written a\r\nletter to Boldwood, at his address beyond Casterbridge, saying mildly\r\nbut firmly that she had well considered the whole subject he had\r\nbrought before her and kindly given her time to decide upon; that her\r\nfinal decision was that she could not marry him. She had expressed to\r\nOak an intention to wait till Boldwood came home before communicating\r\nto him her conclusive reply. But Bathsheba found that she could not\r\nwait.\r\n\r\nIt was impossible to send this letter till the next day; yet to quell\r\nher uneasiness by getting it out of her hands, and so, as it were,\r\nsetting the act in motion at once, she arose to take it to any one of\r\nthe women who might be in the kitchen.\r\n\r\nShe paused in the passage. A dialogue was going on in the kitchen, and\r\nBathsheba and Troy were the subject of it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf he marry her, she\xe2\x80\x99ll gie up farming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Twill be a gallant life, but may bring some trouble between the\r\nmirth\xe2\x80\x94so say I.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I wish I had half such a husband.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba had too much sense to mind seriously what her servitors said\r\nabout her; but too much womanly redundance of speech to leave alone\r\nwhat was said till it died the natural death of unminded things. She\r\nburst in upon them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho are you speaking of?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\nThere was a pause before anybody replied. At last Liddy said frankly,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was passing was a bit of a word about yourself, miss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought so! Maryann and Liddy and Temperance\xe2\x80\x94now I forbid you to\r\nsuppose such things. You know I don\xe2\x80\x99t care the least for Mr. Troy\xe2\x80\x94not\r\nI. I\xe2\x80\x99ll make answer hencefor\xe2\x80\x99ard, \xe2\x80\x98Of course a lady like Miss\r\nEverdene can\xe2\x80\x99t love him\xe2\x80\x99; I\xe2\x80\x99ll say it out in plain black and white.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba burst out: \xe2\x80\x9cO Liddy, are you such a simpleton?
