And do not let them know\r\nthat I have been crying about him, because it will be dreadful for me,\r\nand no good to him, poor thing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDeath\xe2\x80\x99s head himself shan\xe2\x80\x99t wring it from me, mistress, if I\xe2\x80\x99ve a mind\r\nto keep anything; and I\xe2\x80\x99ll always be your friend,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Liddy,\r\nemphatically, at the same time bringing a few more tears into her own\r\neyes, not from any particular necessity, but from an artistic sense of\r\nmaking herself in keeping with the remainder of the picture, which\r\nseems to influence women at such times. \xe2\x80\x9cI think God likes us to be\r\ngood friends, don\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd, dear miss, you won\xe2\x80\x99t harry me and storm at me, will you? because\r\nyou seem to swell so tall as a lion then, and it frightens me! Do you\r\nknow, I fancy you would be a match for any man when you are in one o\xe2\x80\x99\r\nyour takings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever! do you?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, slightly laughing, though somewhat\r\nseriously alarmed by this Amazonian picture of herself. \xe2\x80\x9cI hope I am\r\nnot a bold sort of maid\xe2\x80\x94mannish?\xe2\x80\x9d she continued with some anxiety.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, not mannish; but so almighty womanish that \xe2\x80\x99tis getting on that\r\nway sometimes. Ah! miss,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, after having drawn her breath very\r\nsadly in and sent it very sadly out, \xe2\x80\x9cI wish I had half your failing\r\nthat way. \xe2\x80\x99Tis a great protection to a poor maid in these illegit\xe2\x80\x99mate\r\ndays!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXI\r\nBLAME\xe2\x80\x94FURY\r\n\r\n\r\nThe next evening Bathsheba, with the idea of getting out of the way of\r\nMr. Boldwood in the event of his returning to answer her note in\r\nperson, proceeded to fulfil an engagement made with Liddy some few\r\nhours earlier. Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s companion, as a gauge of their\r\nreconciliation, had been granted a week\xe2\x80\x99s holiday to visit her sister,\r\nwho was married to a thriving hurdler and cattle-crib-maker living in a\r\ndelightful labyrinth of hazel copse not far beyond Yalbury. The\r\narrangement was that Miss Everdene should honour them by coming there\r\nfor a day or two to inspect some ingenious contrivances which this man\r\nof the woods had introduced into his wares.\r\n\r\nLeaving her instructions with Gabriel and Maryann, that they were to\r\nsee everything carefully locked up for the night, she went out of the\r\nhouse just at the close of a timely thunder-shower, which had refined\r\nthe air, and daintily bathed the coat of the land, though all beneath\r\nwas dry as ever. Freshness was exhaled in an essence from the varied\r\ncontours of bank and hollow, as if the earth breathed maiden breath;\r\nand the pleased birds were hymning to the scene. Before her, among the\r\nclouds, there was a contrast in the shape of lairs of fierce light\r\nwhich showed themselves in the neighbourhood of a hidden sun, lingering\r\non to the farthest north-west corner of the heavens that this midsummer\r\nseason allowed.\r\n\r\nShe had walked nearly two miles of her journey, watching how the day\r\nwas retreating, and thinking how the time of deeds was quietly melting\r\ninto the time of thought, to give place in its turn to the time of\r\nprayer and sleep, when she beheld advancing over Yalbury hill the very\r\nman she sought so anxiously to elude. Boldwood was stepping on, not\r\nwith that quiet tread of reserved strength which was his customary\r\ngait, in which he always seemed to be balancing two thoughts. His\r\nmanner was stunned and sluggish now.\r\n\r\nBoldwood had for the first time been awakened to woman\xe2\x80\x99s privileges in\r\ntergiversation even when it involves another person\xe2\x80\x99s possible blight.\r\nThat Bathsheba was a firm and positive girl, far less inconsequent than\r\nher fellows, had been the very lung of his hope; for he had held that\r\nthese qualities would lead her to adhere to a straight course for\r\nconsistency\xe2\x80\x99s sake, and accept him, though her fancy might not flood\r\nhim with the iridescent hues of uncritical love. But the argument now\r\ncame back as sorry gleams from a broken mirror. The discovery was no\r\nless a scourge than a surprise.\r\n\r\nHe came on looking upon the ground, and did not see Bathsheba till they\r\nwere less than a stone\xe2\x80\x99s throw apart. He looked up at the sound of her\r\npit-pat, and his changed appearance sufficiently denoted to her the\r\ndepth and strength of the feelings paralyzed by her letter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh; is it you, Mr. Boldwood?\xe2\x80\x9d she faltered, a guilty warmth pulsing in\r\nher face.\r\n\r\nThose who have the power of reproaching in silence may find it a means\r\nmore effective than words. There are accents in the eye which are not\r\non the tongue, and more tales come from pale lips than can enter an\r\near. It is both the grandeur and the pain of the remoter moods that\r\nthey avoid the pathway of sound. Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s look was unanswerable.\r\n\r\nSeeing she turned a little aside, he said, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat, are you afraid of\r\nme?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should you say that?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fancied you looked so,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd it is most strange, because of\r\nits contrast with my feeling for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe regained self-possession, fixed her eyes calmly, and waited.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know what that feeling is,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Boldwood, deliberately. \xe2\x80\x9cA\r\nthing strong as death. No dismissal by a hasty letter affects that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish you did not feel so strongly about me,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is\r\ngenerous of you, and more than I deserve, but I must not hear it now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear it? What do you think I have to say, then? I am not to marry you,\r\nand that\xe2\x80\x99s enough. Your letter was excellently plain. I want you to\r\nhear nothing\xe2\x80\x94not I.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba was unable to direct her will into any definite groove for\r\nfreeing herself from this fearfully awkward position. She confusedly\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cGood evening,\xe2\x80\x9d and was moving on. Boldwood walked up to her\r\nheavily and dully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBathsheba\xe2\x80\x94darling\xe2\x80\x94is it final indeed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x94have pity upon me!\xe2\x80\x9d Boldwood burst out. \xe2\x80\x9cGod\xe2\x80\x99s sake,\r\nyes\xe2\x80\x94I am come to that low, lowest stage\xe2\x80\x94to ask a woman for pity! Still,\r\nshe is you\xe2\x80\x94she is you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba commanded herself well. But she could hardly get a clear\r\nvoice for what came instinctively to her lips: \xe2\x80\x9cThere is little honour\r\nto the woman in that speech.\xe2\x80\x9d It was only whispered, for something\r\nunutterably mournful no less than distressing in this spectacle of a\r\nman showing himself to be so entirely the vane of a passion enervated\r\nthe feminine instinct for punctilios.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am beyond myself about this, and am mad,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI am no stoic at\r\nall to be supplicating here; but I do supplicate to you. I wish you\r\nknew what is in me of devotion to you; but it is impossible, that. In\r\nbare human mercy to a lonely man, don\xe2\x80\x99t throw me off now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t throw you off\xe2\x80\x94indeed, how can I? I never had you.\xe2\x80\x9d In her\r\nnoon-clear sense that she had never loved him she forgot for a moment\r\nher thoughtless angle on that day in February.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut there was a time when you turned to me, before I thought of you! I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t reproach you, for even now I feel that the ignorant and cold\r\ndarkness that I should have lived in if you had not attracted me by\r\nthat letter\xe2\x80\x94valentine you call it\xe2\x80\x94would have been worse than my\r\nknowledge of you, though it has brought this misery. But, I say, there\r\nwas a time when I knew nothing of you, and cared nothing for you, and\r\nyet you drew me on. And if you say you gave me no encouragement, I\r\ncannot but contradict you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat you call encouragement was the childish game of an idle minute. I\r\nhave bitterly repented of it\xe2\x80\x94ay, bitterly, and in tears. Can you still\r\ngo on reminding me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t accuse you of it\xe2\x80\x94I deplore it. I took for earnest what you\r\ninsist was jest, and now this that I pray to be jest you say is awful,\r\nwretched earnest. Our moods meet at wrong places. I wish your feeling\r\nwas more like mine, or my feeling more like yours! Oh, could I but have\r\nforeseen the torture that trifling trick was going to lead me into, how\r\nI should have cursed you; but only having been able to see it since, I\r\ncannot do that, for I love you too well! But it is weak, idle\r\ndrivelling to go on like this.... Bathsheba, you are the first woman of\r\nany shade or nature that I have ever looked at to love, and it is the\r\nhaving been so near claiming you for my own that makes this denial so\r\nhard to bear. How nearly you promised me! But I don\xe2\x80\x99t speak now to move\r\nyour heart, and make you grieve because of my pain; it is no use, that.\r\nI must bear it; my pain would get no less by paining you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I do pity you\xe2\x80\x94deeply\xe2\x80\x94O, so deeply!\xe2\x80\x9d she earnestly said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo no such thing\xe2\x80\x94do no such thing. Your dear love, Bathsheba, is such\r\na vast thing beside your pity, that the loss of your pity as well as\r\nyour love is no great addition to my sorrow, nor does the gain of your\r\npity make it sensibly less. O sweet\xe2\x80\x94how dearly you spoke to me behind\r\nthe spear-bed at the washing-pool, and in the barn at the shearing, and\r\nthat dearest last time in the evening at your home! Where are your\r\npleasant words all gone\xe2\x80\x94your earnest hope to be able to love me? Where\r\nis your firm conviction that you would get to care for me very much?\r\nReally forgotten?\xe2\x80\x94really?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe checked emotion, looked him quietly and clearly in the face, and\r\nsaid in her low, firm voice, \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Boldwood, I promised you nothing.\r\nWould you have had me a woman of clay when you paid me that furthest,\r\nhighest compliment a man can pay a woman\xe2\x80\x94telling her he loves her? I\r\nwas bound to show some feeling, if I would not be a graceless shrew.\r\nYet each of those pleasures was just for the day\xe2\x80\x94the day just for the\r\npleasure. How was I to know that what is a pastime to all other men was\r\ndeath to you? Have reason, do, and think more kindly of me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, never mind arguing\xe2\x80\x94never mind. One thing is sure: you were all\r\nbut mine, and now you are not nearly mine. Everything is changed, and\r\nthat by you alone, remember. You were nothing to me once, and I was\r\ncontented; you are now nothing to me again, and how different the\r\nsecond nothing is from the first! Would to God you had never taken me\r\nup, since it was only to throw me down!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba, in spite of her mettle, began to feel unmistakable signs\r\nthat she was inherently the weaker vessel. She strove miserably against\r\nthis femininity which would insist upon supplying unbidden emotions in\r\nstronger and stronger current. She had tried to elude agitation by\r\nfixing her mind on the trees, sky, any trivial object before her eyes,\r\nwhilst his reproaches fell, but ingenuity could not save her now.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not take you up\xe2\x80\x94surely I did not!\xe2\x80\x9d she answered as heroically as\r\nshe could. \xe2\x80\x9cBut don\xe2\x80\x99t be in this mood with me. I can endure being told\r\nI am in the wrong, if you will only tell it me gently! O sir, will you\r\nnot kindly forgive me, and look at it cheerfully?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCheerfully! Can a man fooled to utter heart-burning find a reason for\r\nbeing merry? If I have lost, how can I be as if I had won? Heavens you\r\nmust be heartless quite! Had I known what a fearfully bitter sweet this\r\nwas to be, how I would have avoided you, and never seen you, and been\r\ndeaf of you. I tell you all this, but what do you care! You don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncare.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe returned silent and weak denials to his charges, and swayed her\r\nhead desperately, as if to thrust away the words as they came showering\r\nabout her ears from the lips of the trembling man in the climax of\r\nlife, with his bronzed Roman face and fine frame.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDearest, dearest, I am wavering even now between the two opposites of\r\nrecklessly renouncing you, and labouring humbly for you again. Forget\r\nthat you have said No, and let it be as it was! Say, Bathsheba, that\r\nyou only wrote that refusal to me in fun\xe2\x80\x94come, say it to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be untrue, and painful to both of us. You overrate my\r\ncapacity for love. I don\xe2\x80\x99t possess half the warmth of nature you\r\nbelieve me to have. An unprotected childhood in a cold world has beaten\r\ngentleness out of me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe immediately said with more resentment: \xe2\x80\x9cThat may be true, somewhat;\r\nbut ah, Miss Everdene, it won\xe2\x80\x99t do as a reason! You are not the cold\r\nwoman you would have me believe. No, no! It isn\xe2\x80\x99t because you have no\r\nfeeling in you that you don\xe2\x80\x99t love me. You naturally would have me\r\nthink so\xe2\x80\x94you would hide from me that you have a burning heart like\r\nmine. You have love enough, but it is turned into a new channel. I know\r\nwhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe swift music of her heart became hubbub now, and she throbbed to\r\nextremity. He was coming to Troy. He did then know what had occurred!\r\nAnd the name fell from his lips the next moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did Troy not leave my treasure alone?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, fiercely. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen I\r\nhad no thought of injuring him, why did he force himself upon your\r\nnotice! Before he worried you your inclination was to have me; when\r\nnext I should have come to you your answer would have been Yes. Can you\r\ndeny it\xe2\x80\x94I ask, can you deny it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe delayed the reply, but was too honest to withhold it. \xe2\x80\x9cI cannot,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe whispered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know you cannot. But he stole in in my absence and robbed me. Why\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t he win you away before, when nobody would have been\r\ngrieved?\xe2\x80\x94when nobody would have been set tale-bearing. Now the people\r\nsneer at me\xe2\x80\x94the very hills and sky seem to laugh at me till I blush\r\nshamefully for my folly. I have lost my respect, my good name, my\r\nstanding\xe2\x80\x94lost it, never to get it again. Go and marry your man\xe2\x80\x94go on!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh sir\xe2\x80\x94Mr. Boldwood!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may as well. I have no further claim upon you. As for me, I had\r\nbetter go somewhere alone, and hide\xe2\x80\x94and pray. I loved a woman once. I\r\nam now ashamed. When I am dead they\xe2\x80\x99ll say, miserable love-sick man\r\nthat he was. Heaven\xe2\x80\x94heaven\xe2\x80\x94if I had got jilted secretly, and the\r\ndishonour not known, and my position kept! But no matter, it is gone,\r\nand the woman not gained. Shame upon him\xe2\x80\x94shame!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis unreasonable anger terrified her, and she glided from him, without\r\nobviously moving, as she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI am only a girl\xe2\x80\x94do not speak to me\r\nso!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll the time you knew\xe2\x80\x94how very well you knew\xe2\x80\x94that your new freak was\r\nmy misery. Dazzled by brass and scarlet\xe2\x80\x94Oh, Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x94this is woman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfolly indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe fired up at once. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are taking too much upon yourself!\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid, vehemently. \xe2\x80\x9cEverybody is upon me\xe2\x80\x94everybody. It is unmanly to\r\nattack a woman so! I have nobody in the world to fight my battles for\r\nme; but no mercy is shown. Yet if a thousand of you sneer and say\r\nthings against me, I _will not_ be put down!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll chatter with him doubtless about me. Say to him, \xe2\x80\x98Boldwood\r\nwould have died for me.\xe2\x80\x99 Yes, and you have given way to him, knowing\r\nhim to be not the man for you. He has kissed you\xe2\x80\x94claimed you as his. Do\r\nyou hear\xe2\x80\x94he has kissed you. Deny it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe most tragic woman is cowed by a tragic man, and although Boldwood\r\nwas, in vehemence and glow, nearly her own self rendered into another\r\nsex, Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s cheek quivered. She gasped, \xe2\x80\x9cLeave me, sir\xe2\x80\x94leave me! I\r\nam nothing to you. Let me go on!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDeny that he has kissed you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHa\xe2\x80\x94then he has!\xe2\x80\x9d came hoarsely from the farmer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, slowly, and, in spite of her fear, defiantly. \xe2\x80\x9cI am\r\nnot ashamed to speak the truth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen curse him; and curse him!\xe2\x80\x9d said Boldwood, breaking into a\r\nwhispered fury. \xe2\x80\x9cWhilst I would have given worlds to touch your hand,\r\nyou have let a rake come in without right or ceremony and\xe2\x80\x94kiss you!\r\nHeaven\xe2\x80\x99s mercy\xe2\x80\x94kiss you!... Ah, a time of his life shall come when he\r\nwill have to repent, and think wretchedly of the pain he has caused\r\nanother man; and then may he ache, and wish, and curse, and yearn\xe2\x80\x94as I\r\ndo now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t, don\xe2\x80\x99t, oh, don\xe2\x80\x99t pray down evil upon him!\xe2\x80\x9d she implored in a\r\nmiserable cry. \xe2\x80\x9cAnything but that\xe2\x80\x94anything. Oh, be kind to him, sir,\r\nfor I love him true!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBoldwood\xe2\x80\x99s ideas had reached that point of fusion at which outline and\r\nconsistency entirely disappear. The impending night appeared to\r\nconcentrate in his eye. He did not hear her at all now.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll punish him\xe2\x80\x94by my soul, that will I! I\xe2\x80\x99ll meet him, soldier or no,\r\nand I\xe2\x80\x99ll horsewhip the untimely stripling for this reckless theft of my\r\none delight. If he were a hundred men I\xe2\x80\x99d horsewhip him\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d He dropped\r\nhis voice suddenly and unnaturally. \xe2\x80\x9cBathsheba, sweet, lost coquette,\r\npardon me! I\xe2\x80\x99ve been blaming you, threatening you, behaving like a\r\nchurl to you, when he\xe2\x80\x99s the greatest sinner. He stole your dear heart\r\naway with his unfathomable lies!... It is a fortunate thing for him\r\nthat he\xe2\x80\x99s gone back to his regiment\xe2\x80\x94that he\xe2\x80\x99s away up the country, and\r\nnot here! I hope he may not return here just yet. I pray God he may not\r\ncome into my sight, for I may be tempted beyond myself. Oh, Bathsheba,\r\nkeep him away\xe2\x80\x94yes, keep him away from me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a moment Boldwood stood so inertly after this that his soul seemed\r\nto have been entirely exhaled with the breath of his passionate words.\r\nHe turned his face away, and withdrew, and his form was soon covered\r\nover by the twilight as his footsteps mixed in with the low hiss of the\r\nleafy trees.\r\n\r\nBathsheba, who had been standing motionless as a model all this latter\r\ntime, flung her hands to her face, and wildly attempted to ponder on\r\nthe exhibition which had just passed away. Such astounding wells of\r\nfevered feeling in a still man like Mr. Boldwood were incomprehensible,\r\ndreadful. Instead of being a man trained to repression he was\xe2\x80\x94what she\r\nhad seen him.\r\n\r\nThe force of the farmer\xe2\x80\x99s threats lay in their relation to a\r\ncircumstance known at present only to herself: her lover was coming\r\nback to Weatherbury in the course of the very next day or two. Troy had\r\nnot returned to his distant barracks as Boldwood and others supposed,\r\nbut had merely gone to visit some acquaintance in Bath, and had yet a\r\nweek or more remaining to his furlough.\r\n\r\nShe felt wretchedly certain that if he revisited her just at this nick\r\nof time, and came into contact with Boldwood, a fierce quarrel would be\r\nthe consequence. She panted with solicitude when she thought of\r\npossible injury to Troy. The least spark would kindle the farmer\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nswift feelings of rage and jealousy; he would lose his self-mastery as\r\nhe had this evening; Troy\xe2\x80\x99s blitheness might become aggressive; it\r\nmight take the direction of derision, and Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s anger might then\r\ntake the direction of revenge.\r\n\r\nWith almost a morbid dread of being thought a gushing girl, this\r\nguileless woman too well concealed from the world under a manner of\r\ncarelessness the warm depths of her strong emotions. But now there was\r\nno reserve. In her distraction, instead of advancing further she walked\r\nup and down, beating the air with her fingers, pressing on her brow,\r\nand sobbing brokenly to herself. Then she sat down on a heap of stones\r\nby the wayside to think. There she remained long. Above the dark margin\r\nof the earth appeared foreshores and promontories of coppery cloud,\r\nbounding a green and pellucid expanse in the western sky. Amaranthine\r\nglosses came over them then, and the unresting world wheeled her round\r\nto a contrasting prospect eastward, in the shape of indecisive and\r\npalpitating stars. She gazed upon their silent throes amid the shades\r\nof space, but realised none at all. Her troubled spirit was far away\r\nwith Troy.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXII\r\nNIGHT\xe2\x80\x94HORSES TRAMPING\r\n\r\n\r\nThe village of Weatherbury was quiet as the graveyard in its midst, and\r\nthe living were lying well-nigh as still as the dead. The church clock\r\nstruck eleven. The air was so empty of other sounds that the whirr of\r\nthe clock-work immediately before the strokes was distinct, and so was\r\nalso the click of the same at their close. The notes flew forth with\r\nthe usual blind obtuseness of inanimate things\xe2\x80\x94flapping and rebounding\r\namong walls, undulating against the scattered clouds, spreading through\r\ntheir interstices into unexplored miles of space.\r\n\r\nBathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s crannied and mouldy halls were to-night occupied only by\r\nMaryann, Liddy being, as was stated, with her sister, whom Bathsheba\r\nhad set out to visit. A few minutes after eleven had struck, Maryann\r\nturned in her bed with a sense of being disturbed. She was totally\r\nunconscious of the nature of the interruption to her sleep. It led to a\r\ndream, and the dream to an awakening, with an uneasy sensation that\r\nsomething had happened. She left her bed and looked out of the window.\r\nThe paddock abutted on this end of the building, and in the paddock she\r\ncould just discern by the uncertain gray a moving figure approaching\r\nthe horse that was feeding there. The figure seized the horse by the\r\nforelock, and led it to the corner of the field. Here she could see\r\nsome object which circumstances proved to be a vehicle, for after a few\r\nminutes spent apparently in harnessing, she heard the trot of the horse\r\ndown the road, mingled with the sound of light wheels.\r\n\r\nTwo varieties only of humanity could have entered the paddock with the\r\nghostlike glide of that mysterious figure. They were a woman and a\r\ngipsy man. A woman was out of the question in such an occupation at\r\nthis hour, and the comer could be no less than a thief, who might\r\nprobably have known the weakness of the household on this particular\r\nnight, and have chosen it on that account for his daring attempt.\r\nMoreover, to raise suspicion to conviction itself, there were gipsies\r\nin Weatherbury Bottom.\r\n\r\nMaryann, who had been afraid to shout in the robber\xe2\x80\x99s presence, having\r\nseen him depart had no fear. She hastily slipped on her clothes,\r\nstumped down the disjointed staircase with its hundred creaks, ran to\r\nCoggan\xe2\x80\x99s, the nearest house, and raised an alarm. Coggan called\r\nGabriel, who now again lodged in his house as at first, and together\r\nthey went to the paddock. Beyond all doubt the horse was gone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHark!\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel.\r\n\r\nThey listened. Distinct upon the stagnant air came the sounds of a\r\ntrotting horse passing up Longpuddle Lane\xe2\x80\x94just beyond the gipsies\xe2\x80\x99\r\nencampment in Weatherbury Bottom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s our Dainty\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll swear to her step,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMighty me! Won\xe2\x80\x99t mis\xe2\x80\x99ess storm and call us stupids when she comes\r\nback!\xe2\x80\x9d moaned Maryann. \xe2\x80\x9cHow I wish it had happened when she was at\r\nhome, and none of us had been answerable!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must ride after,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel, decisively. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll be responsible to\r\nMiss Everdene for what we do. Yes, we\xe2\x80\x99ll follow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith, I don\xe2\x80\x99t see how,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coggan. \xe2\x80\x9cAll our horses are too heavy\r\nfor that trick except little Poppet, and what\xe2\x80\x99s she between two of\r\nus?\xe2\x80\x94If we only had that pair over the hedge we might do something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich pair?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s Tidy and Moll.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen wait here till I come hither again,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel. He ran down\r\nthe hill towards Farmer Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFarmer Boldwood is not at home,\xe2\x80\x9d said Maryann.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll the better,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coggan. \xe2\x80\x9cI know what he\xe2\x80\x99s gone for.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLess than five minutes brought up Oak again, running at the same pace,\r\nwith two halters dangling from his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere did you find \xe2\x80\x99em?\xe2\x80\x9d said Coggan, turning round and leaping upon\r\nthe hedge without waiting for an answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnder the eaves. I knew where they were kept,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel, following\r\nhim. \xe2\x80\x9cCoggan, you can ride bare-backed? there\xe2\x80\x99s no time to look for\r\nsaddles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLike a hero!\xe2\x80\x9d said Jan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaryann, you go to bed,\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel shouted to her from the top of the\r\nhedge.\r\n\r\nSpringing down into Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s pastures, each pocketed his halter to\r\nhide it from the horses, who, seeing the men empty-handed, docilely\r\nallowed themselves to be seized by the mane, when the halters were\r\ndexterously slipped on. Having neither bit nor bridle, Oak and Coggan\r\nextemporized the former by passing the rope in each case through the\r\nanimal\xe2\x80\x99s mouth and looping it on the other side. Oak vaulted astride,\r\nand Coggan clambered up by aid of the bank, when they ascended to the\r\ngate and galloped off in the direction taken by Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s horse and\r\nthe robber. Whose vehicle the horse had been harnessed to was a matter\r\nof some uncertainty.\r\n\r\nWeatherbury Bottom was reached in three or four minutes. They scanned\r\nthe shady green patch by the roadside. The gipsies were gone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe villains!\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel. \xe2\x80\x9cWhich way have they gone, I wonder?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStraight on, as sure as God made little apples,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well; we are better mounted, and must overtake \xe2\x80\x99em\xe2\x80\x9d, said Oak.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow on at full speed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo sound of the rider in their van could now be discovered. The\r\nroad-metal grew softer and more clayey as Weatherbury was left behind,\r\nand the late rain had wetted its surface to a somewhat plastic, but not\r\nmuddy state. They came to cross-roads. Coggan suddenly pulled up Moll\r\nand slipped off.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must try to track \xe2\x80\x99em, since we can\xe2\x80\x99t hear \xe2\x80\x99em,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jan, fumbling\r\nin his pockets. He struck a light, and held the match to the ground.\r\nThe rain had been heavier here, and all foot and horse tracks made\r\nprevious to the storm had been abraded and blurred by the drops, and\r\nthey were now so many little scoops of water, which reflected the flame\r\nof the match like eyes. One set of tracks was fresh and had no water in\r\nthem; one pair of ruts was also empty, and not small canals, like the\r\nothers. The footprints forming this recent impression were full of\r\ninformation as to pace; they were in equidistant pairs, three or four\r\nfeet apart, the right and left foot of each pair being exactly opposite\r\none another.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStraight on!\xe2\x80\x9d Jan exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cTracks like that mean a stiff gallop. No\r\nwonder we don\xe2\x80\x99t hear him. And the horse is harnessed\xe2\x80\x94look at the ruts.\r\nAy, that\xe2\x80\x99s our mare sure enough!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOld Jimmy Harris only shoed her last week, and I\xe2\x80\x99d swear to his make\r\namong ten thousand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe rest of the gipsies must ha\xe2\x80\x99 gone on earlier, or some other way,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Oak. \xe2\x80\x9cYou saw there were no other tracks?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue.\xe2\x80\x9d They rode along silently for a long weary time. Coggan carried\r\nan old pinchbeck repeater which he had inherited from some genius in\r\nhis family; and it now struck one. He lighted another match, and\r\nexamined the ground again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis a canter now,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, throwing away the light. \xe2\x80\x9cA twisty,\r\nrickety pace for a gig. The fact is, they over-drove her at starting;\r\nwe shall catch \xe2\x80\x99em yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain they hastened on, and entered Blackmore Vale. Coggan\xe2\x80\x99s watch\r\nstruck one. When they looked again the hoof-marks were so spaced as to\r\nform a sort of zigzag if united, like the lamps along a street.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a trot, I know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly a trot now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coggan, cheerfully. \xe2\x80\x9cWe shall overtake him in\r\ntime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey pushed rapidly on for yet two or three miles. \xe2\x80\x9cAh! a moment,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nJan. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s see how she was driven up this hill. \xe2\x80\x99Twill help us.\xe2\x80\x9d A\r\nlight was promptly struck upon his gaiters as before, and the\r\nexamination made.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHurrah!\xe2\x80\x9d said Coggan. \xe2\x80\x9cShe walked up here\xe2\x80\x94and well she might. We shall\r\nget them in two miles, for a crown.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey rode three, and listened. No sound was to be heard save a millpond\r\ntrickling hoarsely through a hatch, and suggesting gloomy possibilities\r\nof drowning by jumping in. Gabriel dismounted when they came to a\r\nturning. The tracks were absolutely the only guide as to the direction\r\nthat they now had, and great caution was necessary to avoid confusing\r\nthem with some others which had made their appearance lately.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does this mean?\xe2\x80\x94though I guess,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel, looking up at\r\nCoggan as he moved the match over the ground about the turning. Coggan,\r\nwho, no less than the panting horses, had latterly shown signs of\r\nweariness, again scrutinized the mystic characters. This time only\r\nthree were of the regular horseshoe shape. Every fourth was a dot.\r\n\r\nHe screwed up his face and emitted a long \xe2\x80\x9cWhew-w-w!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLame,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Dainty is lamed; the near-foot-afore,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coggan slowly,\r\nstaring still at the footprints.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll push on,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel, remounting his humid steed.\r\n\r\nAlthough the road along its greater part had been as good as any\r\nturnpike-road in the country, it was nominally only a byway. The last\r\nturning had brought them into the high road leading to Bath. Coggan\r\nrecollected himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall have him now!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSherton Turnpike. The keeper of that gate is the sleepiest man between\r\nhere and London\xe2\x80\x94Dan Randall, that\xe2\x80\x99s his name\xe2\x80\x94knowed en for years, when\r\nhe was at Casterbridge gate. Between the lameness and the gate \xe2\x80\x99tis a\r\ndone job.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey now advanced with extreme caution. Nothing was said until, against\r\na shady background of foliage, five white bars were visible, crossing\r\ntheir route a little way ahead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush\xe2\x80\x94we are almost close!\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAmble on upon the grass,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coggan.\r\n\r\nThe white bars were blotted out in the midst by a dark shape in front\r\nof them. The silence of this lonely time was pierced by an exclamation\r\nfrom that quarter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHoy-a-hoy! Gate!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt appeared that there had been a previous call which they had not\r\nnoticed, for on their close approach the door of the turnpike-house\r\nopened, and the keeper came out half-dressed, with a candle in his\r\nhand. The rays illumined the whole group.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKeep the gate close!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Gabriel. \xe2\x80\x9cHe has stolen the horse!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho?\xe2\x80\x9d said the turnpike-man.\r\n\r\nGabriel looked at the driver of the gig, and saw a woman\xe2\x80\x94Bathsheba, his\r\nmistress.\r\n\r\nOn hearing his voice she had turned her face away from the light.\r\nCoggan had, however, caught sight of her in the meanwhile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, \xe2\x80\x99tis mistress\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll take my oath!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, amazed.\r\n\r\nBathsheba it certainly was, and she had by this time done the trick she\r\ncould do so well in crises not of love, namely, mask a surprise by\r\ncoolness of manner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Gabriel,\xe2\x80\x9d she inquired quietly, \xe2\x80\x9cwhere are you going?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe thought\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d began Gabriel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am driving to Bath,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, taking for her own use the assurance\r\nthat Gabriel lacked. \xe2\x80\x9cAn important matter made it necessary for me to\r\ngive up my visit to Liddy, and go off at once. What, then, were you\r\nfollowing me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe thought the horse was stole.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94what a thing! How very foolish of you not to know that I had\r\ntaken the trap and horse. I could neither wake Maryann nor get into the\r\nhouse, though I hammered for ten minutes against her window-sill.\r\nFortunately, I could get the key of the coach-house, so I troubled no\r\none further. Didn\xe2\x80\x99t you think it might be me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should we, miss?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps not. Why, those are never Farmer Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s horses! Goodness\r\nmercy! what have you been doing\xe2\x80\x94bringing trouble upon me in this way?\r\nWhat! mustn\xe2\x80\x99t a lady move an inch from her door without being dogged\r\nlike a thief?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how was we to know, if you left no account of your doings?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nexpostulated Coggan, \xe2\x80\x9cand ladies don\xe2\x80\x99t drive at these hours, miss, as a\r\njineral rule of society.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did leave an account\xe2\x80\x94and you would have seen it in the morning. I\r\nwrote in chalk on the coach-house doors that I had come back for the\r\nhorse and gig, and driven off; that I could arouse nobody, and should\r\nreturn soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you\xe2\x80\x99ll consider, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, that we couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see that till it got\r\ndaylight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, and though vexed at first she had too much sense to\r\nblame them long or seriously for a devotion to her that was as valuable\r\nas it was rare. She added with a very pretty grace, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I really\r\nthank you heartily for taking all this trouble; but I wish you had\r\nborrowed anybody\xe2\x80\x99s horses but Mr. Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDainty is lame, miss,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coggan. \xe2\x80\x9cCan ye go on?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was only a stone in her shoe. I got down and pulled it out a\r\nhundred yards back. I can manage very well, thank you. I shall be in\r\nBath by daylight. Will you now return, please?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe turned her head\xe2\x80\x94the gateman\xe2\x80\x99s candle shimmering upon her quick,\r\nclear eyes as she did so\xe2\x80\x94passed through the gate, and was soon wrapped\r\nin the embowering shades of mysterious summer boughs. Coggan and\r\nGabriel put about their horses, and, fanned by the velvety air of this\r\nJuly night, retraced the road by which they had come.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA strange vagary, this of hers, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it, Oak?\xe2\x80\x9d said Coggan,\r\ncuriously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel, shortly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe won\xe2\x80\x99t be in Bath by no daylight!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCoggan, suppose we keep this night\xe2\x80\x99s work as quiet as we can?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am of one and the same mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well. We shall be home by three o\xe2\x80\x99clock or so, and can creep into\r\nthe parish like lambs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s perturbed meditations by the roadside had ultimately\r\nevolved a conclusion that there were only two remedies for the present\r\ndesperate state of affairs. The first was merely to keep Troy away from\r\nWeatherbury till Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s indignation had cooled; the second to\r\nlisten to Oak\xe2\x80\x99s entreaties, and Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s denunciations, and give up\r\nTroy altogether.\r\n\r\nAlas! Could she give up this new love\xe2\x80\x94induce him to renounce her by\r\nsaying she did not like him\xe2\x80\x94could no more speak to him, and beg him,\r\nfor her good, to end his furlough in Bath, and see her and Weatherbury\r\nno more?\r\n\r\nIt was a picture full of misery, but for a while she contemplated it\r\nfirmly, allowing herself, nevertheless, as girls will, to dwell upon\r\nthe happy life she would have enjoyed had Troy been Boldwood, and the\r\npath of love the path of duty\xe2\x80\x94inflicting upon herself gratuitous\r\ntortures by imagining him the lover of another woman after forgetting\r\nher; for she had penetrated Troy\xe2\x80\x99s nature so far as to estimate his\r\ntendencies pretty accurately, but unfortunately loved him no less in\r\nthinking that he might soon cease to love her\xe2\x80\x94indeed, considerably\r\nmore.\r\n\r\nShe jumped to her feet. She would see him at once. Yes, she would\r\nimplore him by word of mouth to assist her in this dilemma. A letter to\r\nkeep him away could not reach him in time, even if he should be\r\ndisposed to listen to it.\r\n\r\nWas Bathsheba altogether blind to the obvious fact that the support of\r\na lover\xe2\x80\x99s arms is not of a kind best calculated to assist a resolve to\r\nrenounce him? Or was she sophistically sensible, with a thrill of\r\npleasure, that by adopting this course for getting rid of him she was\r\nensuring a meeting with him, at any rate, once more?\r\n\r\nIt was now dark, and the hour must have been nearly ten. The only way\r\nto accomplish her purpose was to give up her idea of visiting Liddy at\r\nYalbury, return to Weatherbury Farm, put the horse into the gig, and\r\ndrive at once to Bath. The scheme seemed at first impossible: the\r\njourney was a fearfully heavy one, even for a strong horse, at her own\r\nestimate; and she much underrated the distance. It was most venturesome\r\nfor a woman, at night, and alone.\r\n\r\nBut could she go on to Liddy\xe2\x80\x99s and leave things to take their course?\r\nNo, no; anything but that. Bathsheba was full of a stimulating\r\nturbulence, beside which caution vainly prayed for a hearing. She\r\nturned back towards the village.\r\n\r\nHer walk was slow, for she wished not to enter Weatherbury till the\r\ncottagers were in bed, and, particularly, till Boldwood was secure. Her\r\nplan was now to drive to Bath during the night, see Sergeant Troy in\r\nthe morning before he set out to come to her, bid him farewell, and\r\ndismiss him: then to rest the horse thoroughly (herself to weep the\r\nwhile, she thought), starting early the next morning on her return\r\njourney. By this arrangement she could trot Dainty gently all the day,\r\nreach Liddy at Yalbury in the evening, and come home to Weatherbury\r\nwith her whenever they chose\xe2\x80\x94so nobody would know she had been to Bath\r\nat all. Such was Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s scheme. But in her topographical ignorance\r\nas a late comer to the place, she misreckoned the distance of her\r\njourney as not much more than half what it really was.\r\n\r\nThis idea she proceeded to carry out, with what initial success we have\r\nalready seen.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXIII\r\nIN THE SUN\xe2\x80\x94A HARBINGER\r\n\r\n\r\nA week passed, and there were no tidings of Bathsheba; nor was there\r\nany explanation of her Gilpin\xe2\x80\x99s rig.\r\n\r\nThen a note came for Maryann, stating that the business which had\r\ncalled her mistress to Bath still detained her there; but that she\r\nhoped to return in the course of another week.\r\n\r\nAnother week passed. The oat-harvest began, and all the men were\r\na-field under a monochromatic Lammas sky, amid the trembling air and\r\nshort shadows of noon. Indoors nothing was to be heard save the droning\r\nof blue-bottle flies; out-of-doors the whetting of scythes and the hiss\r\nof tressy oat-ears rubbing together as their perpendicular stalks of\r\namber-yellow fell heavily to each swath. Every drop of moisture not in\r\nthe men\xe2\x80\x99s bottles and flagons in the form of cider was raining as\r\nperspiration from their foreheads and cheeks. Drought was everywhere\r\nelse.\r\n\r\nThey were about to withdraw for a while into the charitable shade of a\r\ntree in the fence, when Coggan saw a figure in a blue coat and brass\r\nbuttons running to them across the field.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder who that is?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope nothing is wrong about mistress,\xe2\x80\x9d said Maryann, who with some\r\nother women was tying the bundles (oats being always sheafed on this\r\nfarm), \xe2\x80\x9cbut an unlucky token came to me indoors this morning. I went to\r\nunlock the door and dropped the key, and it fell upon the stone floor\r\nand broke into two pieces. Breaking a key is a dreadful bodement. I\r\nwish mis\xe2\x80\x99ess was home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis Cain Ball,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel, pausing from whetting his reaphook.\r\n\r\nOak was not bound by his agreement to assist in the corn-field; but the\r\nharvest month is an anxious time for a farmer, and the corn was\r\nBathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s, so he lent a hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s dressed up in his best clothes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew Moon. \xe2\x80\x9cHe hev been\r\naway from home for a few days, since he\xe2\x80\x99s had that felon upon his\r\nfinger; for \xe2\x80\x99a said, since I can\xe2\x80\x99t work I\xe2\x80\x99ll have a hollerday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA good time for one\xe2\x80\x94a\xe2\x80\x99 excellent time,\xe2\x80\x9d said Joseph Poorgrass,\r\nstraightening his back; for he, like some of the others, had a way of\r\nresting a while from his labour on such hot days for reasons\r\npreternaturally small; of which Cain Ball\xe2\x80\x99s advent on a week-day in his\r\nSunday-clothes was one of the first magnitude. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Twas a bad leg allowed\r\nme to read the _Pilgrim\xe2\x80\x99s Progress_, and Mark Clark learnt All-Fours in\r\na whitlow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, and my father put his arm out of joint to have time to go\r\ncourting,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jan Coggan, in an eclipsing tone, wiping his face with\r\nhis shirt-sleeve and thrusting back his hat upon the nape of his neck.\r\n\r\nBy this time Cainy was nearing the group of harvesters, and was\r\nperceived to be carrying a large slice of bread and ham in one hand,\r\nfrom which he took mouthfuls as he ran, the other being wrapped in a\r\nbandage. When he came close, his mouth assumed the bell shape, and he\r\nbegan to cough violently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Cainy!\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel, sternly. \xe2\x80\x9cHow many more times must I tell\r\nyou to keep from running so fast when you be eating? And then\xe2\x80\x94I didn\xe2\x80\x99t see no more of Miss Everdene at\r\nall.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t you say so afore, then?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Oak, with much\r\ndisappointment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Matthew Moon, \xe2\x80\x9cshe\xe2\x80\x99ll wish her cake dough if so be she\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nover intimate with that man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s not over intimate with him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel, indignantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe would know better,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coggan. \xe2\x80\x9cOur mis\xe2\x80\x99ess has too much sense\r\nunder they knots of black hair to do such a mad thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see, he\xe2\x80\x99s not a coarse, ignorant man, for he was well brought up,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Matthew, dubiously. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Twas only wildness that made him a soldier,\r\nand maids rather like your man of sin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Cain Ball,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel restlessly, \xe2\x80\x9ccan you swear in the most\r\nawful form that the woman you saw was Miss Everdene?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCain Ball, you be no longer a babe and suckling,\xe2\x80\x9d said Joseph in the\r\nsepulchral tone the circumstances demanded, \xe2\x80\x9cand you know what taking\r\nan oath is. \xe2\x80\x99Tis a horrible testament mind ye, which you say and seal\r\nwith your blood-stone, and the prophet Matthew tells us that on\r\nwhomsoever it shall fall it will grind him to powder. Now, before all\r\nthe work-folk here assembled, can you swear to your words as the\r\nshepherd asks ye?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease no, Mister Oak!\xe2\x80\x9d said Cainy, looking from one to the other with\r\ngreat uneasiness at the spiritual magnitude of the position. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmind saying \xe2\x80\x99tis true, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t like to say \xe2\x80\x99tis damn true, if\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s what you mane.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCain, Cain, how can you!\xe2\x80\x9d asked Joseph sternly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou be asked to swear\r\nin a holy manner, and you swear like wicked Shimei, the son of Gera,\r\nwho cursed as he came. Young man, fie!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I don\xe2\x80\x99t! \xe2\x80\x99Tis you want to squander a pore boy\xe2\x80\x99s soul, Joseph\r\nPoorgrass\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what \xe2\x80\x99tis!\xe2\x80\x9d said Cain, beginning to cry. \xe2\x80\x9cAll I mane\r\nis that in common truth \xe2\x80\x99twas Miss Everdene and Sergeant Troy, but in\r\nthe horrible so-help-me truth that ye want to make of it perhaps \xe2\x80\x99twas\r\nsomebody else!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s no getting at the rights of it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel, turning to his\r\nwork.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCain Ball, you\xe2\x80\x99ll come to a bit of bread!\xe2\x80\x9d groaned Joseph Poorgrass.\r\n\r\nThen the reapers\xe2\x80\x99 hooks were flourished again, and the old sounds went\r\non. Gabriel, without making any pretence of being lively, did nothing\r\nto show that he was particularly dull. However, Coggan knew pretty\r\nnearly how the land lay, and when they were in a nook together he said\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t take on about her, Gabriel. What difference does it make whose\r\nsweetheart she is, since she can\xe2\x80\x99t be yours?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the very thing I say to myself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXIV\r\nHOME AGAIN\xe2\x80\x94A TRICKSTER\r\n\r\n\r\nThat same evening at dusk Gabriel was leaning over Coggan\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngarden-gate, taking an up-and-down survey before retiring to rest.\r\n\r\nA vehicle of some kind was softly creeping along the grassy margin of\r\nthe lane. From it spread the tones of two women talking. The tones were\r\nnatural and not at all suppressed. Oak instantly knew the voices to be\r\nthose of Bathsheba and Liddy.\r\n\r\nThe carriage came opposite and passed by. It was Miss Everdene\xe2\x80\x99s gig,\r\nand Liddy and her mistress were the only occupants of the seat. Liddy\r\nwas asking questions about the city of Bath, and her companion was\r\nanswering them listlessly and unconcernedly. Both Bathsheba and the\r\nhorse seemed weary.\r\n\r\nThe exquisite relief of finding that she was here again, safe and\r\nsound, overpowered all reflection, and Oak could only luxuriate in the\r\nsense of it. All grave reports were forgotten.\r\n\r\nHe lingered and lingered on, till there was no difference between the\r\neastern and western expanses of sky, and the timid hares began to limp\r\ncourageously round the dim hillocks. Gabriel might have been there an\r\nadditional half-hour when a dark form walked slowly by. \xe2\x80\x9cGood-night,\r\nGabriel,\xe2\x80\x9d the passer said.\r\n\r\nIt was Boldwood. \xe2\x80\x9cGood-night, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel.\r\n\r\nBoldwood likewise vanished up the road, and Oak shortly afterwards\r\nturned indoors to bed.\r\n\r\nFarmer Boldwood went on towards Miss Everdene\xe2\x80\x99s house. He reached the\r\nfront, and approaching the entrance, saw a light in the parlour. The\r\nblind was not drawn down, and inside the room was Bathsheba, looking\r\nover some papers or letters. Her back was towards Boldwood. He went to\r\nthe door, knocked, and waited with tense muscles and an aching brow.\r\n\r\nBoldwood had not been outside his garden since his meeting with\r\nBathsheba in the road to Yalbury. Silent and alone, he had remained in\r\nmoody meditation on woman\xe2\x80\x99s ways, deeming as essentials of the whole\r\nsex the accidents of the single one of their number he had ever closely\r\nbeheld. By degrees a more charitable temper had pervaded him, and this\r\nwas the reason of his sally to-night. He had come to apologize and beg\r\nforgiveness of Bathsheba with something like a sense of shame at his\r\nviolence, having but just now learnt that she had returned\xe2\x80\x94only from a\r\nvisit to Liddy, as he supposed, the Bath escapade being quite unknown\r\nto him.\r\n\r\nHe inquired for Miss Everdene. Liddy\xe2\x80\x99s manner was odd, but he did not\r\nnotice it. She went in, leaving him standing there, and in her absence\r\nthe blind of the room containing Bathsheba was pulled down. Boldwood\r\naugured ill from that sign. Liddy came out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy mistress cannot see you, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nThe farmer instantly went out by the gate. He was unforgiven\xe2\x80\x94that was\r\nthe issue of it all. He had seen her who was to him simultaneously a\r\ndelight and a torture, sitting in the room he had shared with her as a\r\npeculiarly privileged guest only a little earlier in the summer, and\r\nshe had denied him an entrance there now.\r\n\r\nBoldwood did not hurry homeward. It was ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock at least, when,\r\nwalking deliberately through the lower part of Weatherbury, he heard\r\nthe carrier\xe2\x80\x99s spring van entering the village. The van ran to and from\r\na town in a northern direction, and it was owned and driven by a\r\nWeatherbury man, at the door of whose house it now pulled up. The lamp\r\nfixed to the head of the hood illuminated a scarlet and gilded form,\r\nwho was the first to alight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Boldwood to himself, \xe2\x80\x9ccome to see her again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTroy entered the carrier\xe2\x80\x99s house, which had been the place of his\r\nlodging on his last visit to his native place. Boldwood was moved by a\r\nsudden determination. He hastened home. In ten minutes he was back\r\nagain, and made as if he were going to call upon Troy at the carrier\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\nBut as he approached, some one opened the door and came out. He heard\r\nthis person say \xe2\x80\x9cGood-night\xe2\x80\x9d to the inmates, and the voice was Troy\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\nThis was strange, coming so immediately after his arrival. Boldwood,\r\nhowever, hastened up to him. Troy had what appeared to be a carpet-bag\r\nin his hand\xe2\x80\x94the same that he had brought with him. It seemed as if he\r\nwere going to leave again this very night.\r\n\r\nTroy turned up the hill and quickened his pace. Boldwood stepped\r\nforward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSergeant Troy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m Sergeant Troy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust arrived from up the country, I think?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust arrived from Bath.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am William Boldwood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe tone in which this word was uttered was all that had been wanted to\r\nbring Boldwood to the point.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish to speak a word with you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat about?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout her who lives just ahead there\xe2\x80\x94and about a woman you have\r\nwronged.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder at your impertinence,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy, moving on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow look here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Boldwood, standing in front of him, \xe2\x80\x9cwonder or\r\nnot, you are going to hold a conversation with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTroy heard the dull determination in Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s voice, looked at his\r\nstalwart frame, then at the thick cudgel he carried in his hand. He\r\nremembered it was past ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock. It seemed worth while to be civil\r\nto Boldwood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, I\xe2\x80\x99ll listen with pleasure,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy, placing his bag on\r\nthe ground, \xe2\x80\x9conly speak low, for somebody or other may overhear us in\r\nthe farmhouse there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then\xe2\x80\x94I know a good deal concerning your Fanny Robin\xe2\x80\x99s attachment\r\nto you. I may say, too, that I believe I am the only person in the\r\nvillage, excepting Gabriel Oak, who does know it. You ought to marry\r\nher.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose I ought. Indeed, I wish to, but I cannot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTroy was about to utter something hastily; he then checked himself and\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cI am too poor.\xe2\x80\x9d His voice was changed. Previously it had had a\r\ndevil-may-care tone. It was the voice of a trickster now.\r\n\r\nBoldwood\xe2\x80\x99s present mood was not critical enough to notice tones. He\r\ncontinued, \xe2\x80\x9cI may as well speak plainly; and understand, I don\xe2\x80\x99t wish\r\nto enter into the questions of right or wrong, woman\xe2\x80\x99s honour and\r\nshame, or to express any opinion on your conduct. I intend a business\r\ntransaction with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy. \xe2\x80\x9cSuppose we sit down here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAn old tree trunk lay under the hedge immediately opposite, and they\r\nsat down.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was engaged to be married to Miss Everdene,\xe2\x80\x9d said Boldwood, \xe2\x80\x9cbut you\r\ncame and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot engaged,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs good as engaged.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I had not turned up she might have become engaged to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHang might!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you had not come I should certainly\xe2\x80\x94yes, _certainly_\xe2\x80\x94have been\r\naccepted by this time. If you had not seen her you might have been\r\nmarried to Fanny. Well, there\xe2\x80\x99s too much difference between Miss\r\nEverdene\xe2\x80\x99s station and your own for this flirtation with her ever to\r\nbenefit you by ending in marriage. So all I ask is, don\xe2\x80\x99t molest her\r\nany more. Marry Fanny. I\xe2\x80\x99ll make it worth your while.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow will you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll pay you well now, I\xe2\x80\x99ll settle a sum of money upon her, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nsee that you don\xe2\x80\x99t suffer from poverty in the future. I\xe2\x80\x99ll put it\r\nclearly. Bathsheba is only playing with you: you are too poor for her\r\nas I said; so give up wasting your time about a great match you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nnever make for a moderate and rightful match you may make to-morrow;\r\ntake up your carpet-bag, turn about, leave Weatherbury now, this night,\r\nand you shall take fifty pounds with you. Fanny shall have fifty to\r\nenable her to prepare for the wedding, when you have told me where she\r\nis living, and she shall have five hundred paid down on her\r\nwedding-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn making this statement Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s voice revealed only too clearly a\r\nconsciousness of the weakness of his position, his aims, and his\r\nmethod. His manner had lapsed quite from that of the firm and dignified\r\nBoldwood of former times; and such a scheme as he had now engaged in he\r\nwould have condemned as childishly imbecile only a few months ago. We\r\ndiscern a grand force in the lover which he lacks whilst a free man;\r\nbut there is a breadth of vision in the free man which in the lover we\r\nvainly seek. Where there is much bias there must be some narrowness,\r\nand love, though added emotion, is subtracted capacity. Boldwood\r\nexemplified this to an abnormal degree: he knew nothing of Fanny\r\nRobin\xe2\x80\x99s circumstances or whereabouts, he knew nothing of Troy\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npossibilities, yet that was what he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like Fanny best,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy; \xe2\x80\x9cand if, as you say, Miss Everdene is\r\nout of my reach, why I have all to gain by accepting your money, and\r\nmarrying Fan. But she\xe2\x80\x99s only a servant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind\xe2\x80\x94do you agree to my arrangement?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Boldwood, in a more elastic voice. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Troy, if you like\r\nher best, why then did you step in here and injure my happiness?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI love Fanny best now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy. \xe2\x80\x9cBut Bathsh\xe2\x80\x94Miss Everdene inflamed\r\nme, and displaced Fanny for a time. It is over now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should it be over so soon? And why then did you come here again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are weighty reasons. Fifty pounds at once, you said!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did,\xe2\x80\x9d said Boldwood, \xe2\x80\x9cand here they are\xe2\x80\x94fifty sovereigns.\xe2\x80\x9d He handed\r\nTroy a small packet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have everything ready\xe2\x80\x94it seems that you calculated on my accepting\r\nthem,\xe2\x80\x9d said the sergeant, taking the packet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you might accept them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Boldwood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve only my word that the programme shall be adhered to, whilst I\r\nat any rate have fifty pounds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had thought of that, and I have considered that if I can\xe2\x80\x99t appeal to\r\nyour honour I can trust to your\xe2\x80\x94well, shrewdness we\xe2\x80\x99ll call it\xe2\x80\x94not to\r\nlose five hundred pounds in prospect, and also make a bitter enemy of a\r\nman who is willing to be an extremely useful friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop, listen!\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy in a whisper.\r\n\r\nA light pit-pat was audible upon the road just above them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy George\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99tis she,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued. \xe2\x80\x9cI must go on and meet her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x94who?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBathsheba.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBathsheba\xe2\x80\x94out alone at this time o\xe2\x80\x99 night!\xe2\x80\x9d said Boldwood in\r\namazement, and starting up. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy must you meet her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was expecting me to-night\xe2\x80\x94and I must now speak to her, and wish\r\nher good-bye, according to your wish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see the necessity of speaking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt can do no harm\xe2\x80\x94and she\xe2\x80\x99ll be wandering about looking for me if I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t. You shall hear all I say to her. It will help you in your\r\nlove-making when I am gone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour tone is mocking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no. And remember this, if she does not know what has become of me,\r\nshe will think more about me than if I tell her flatly I have come to\r\ngive her up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you confine your words to that one point?\xe2\x80\x94Shall I hear every word\r\nyou say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEvery word. Now sit still there, and hold my carpet bag for me, and\r\nmark what you hear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe light footstep came closer, halting occasionally, as if the walker\r\nlistened for a sound. Troy whistled a double note in a soft, fluty\r\ntone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome to that, is it!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Boldwood, uneasily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou promised silence,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI promise again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTroy stepped forward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrank, dearest, is that you?\xe2\x80\x9d The tones were Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO God!\xe2\x80\x9d said Boldwood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy to her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow late you are,\xe2\x80\x9d she continued, tenderly. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you come by the\r\ncarrier? I listened and heard his wheels entering the village, but it\r\nwas some time ago, and I had almost given you up, Frank.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was sure to come,\xe2\x80\x9d said Frank. \xe2\x80\x9cYou knew I should, did you not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I thought you would,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, playfully; \xe2\x80\x9cand, Frank, it is so\r\nlucky! There\xe2\x80\x99s not a soul in my house but me to-night. I\xe2\x80\x99ve packed them\r\nall off so nobody on earth will know of your visit to your lady\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbower. Liddy wanted to go to her grandfather\xe2\x80\x99s to tell him about her\r\nholiday, and I said she might stay with them till to-morrow\xe2\x80\x94when you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nbe gone again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCapital,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, dear me, I had better go back for my bag,\r\nbecause my slippers and brush and comb are in it; you run home whilst I\r\nfetch it, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll promise to be in your parlour in ten minutes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d She turned and tripped up the hill again.\r\n\r\nDuring the progress of this dialogue there was a nervous twitching of\r\nBoldwood\xe2\x80\x99s tightly closed lips, and his face became bathed in a clammy\r\ndew. He now started forward towards Troy. Troy turned to him and took\r\nup the bag.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I tell her I have come to give her up and cannot marry her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the soldier, mockingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no; wait a minute. I want to say more to you\xe2\x80\x94more to you!\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nBoldwood, in a hoarse whisper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy, \xe2\x80\x9cyou see my dilemma. Perhaps I am a bad man\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nvictim of my impulses\xe2\x80\x94led away to do what I ought to leave undone. I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t, however, marry them both. And I have two reasons for choosing\r\nFanny. First, I like her best upon the whole, and second, you make it\r\nworth my while.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt the same instant Boldwood sprang upon him, and held him by the neck.\r\nTroy felt Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s grasp slowly tightening. The move was absolutely\r\nunexpected.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA moment,\xe2\x80\x9d he gasped. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are injuring her you love!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d said the farmer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive me breath,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy.\r\n\r\nBoldwood loosened his hand, saying, \xe2\x80\x9cBy Heaven, I\xe2\x80\x99ve a mind to kill\r\nyou!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd ruin her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSave her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, how can she be saved now, unless I marry her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBoldwood groaned. He reluctantly released the soldier, and flung him\r\nback against the hedge. \xe2\x80\x9cDevil, you torture me!\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\nTroy rebounded like a ball, and was about to make a dash at the farmer;\r\nbut he checked himself, saying lightly\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not worth while to measure my strength with you. Indeed it is a\r\nbarbarous way of settling a quarrel. I shall shortly leave the army\r\nbecause of the same conviction. Now after that revelation of how the\r\nland lies with Bathsheba, \xe2\x80\x99twould be a mistake to kill me, would it\r\nnot?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Twould be a mistake to kill you,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Boldwood, mechanically,\r\nwith a bowed head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetter kill yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFar better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m glad you see it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTroy, make her your wife, and don\xe2\x80\x99t act upon what I arranged just now.\r\nThe alternative is dreadful, but take Bathsheba; I give her up! She\r\nmust love you indeed to sell soul and body to you so utterly as she has\r\ndone. Wretched woman\xe2\x80\x94deluded woman\xe2\x80\x94you are, Bathsheba!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut about Fanny?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBathsheba is a woman well to do,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Boldwood, in nervous\r\nanxiety, \xe2\x80\x9cand, Troy, she will make a good wife; and, indeed, she is\r\nworth your hastening on your marriage with her!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut she has a will\xe2\x80\x94not to say a temper, and I shall be a mere slave to\r\nher. I could do anything with poor Fanny Robin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTroy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Boldwood, imploringly, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll do anything for you, only\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t desert her; pray don\xe2\x80\x99t desert her, Troy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich, poor Fanny?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; Bathsheba Everdene. Love her best! Love her tenderly! How shall I\r\nget you to see how advantageous it will be to you to secure her at\r\nonce?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t wish to secure her in any new way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBoldwood\xe2\x80\x99s arm moved spasmodically towards Troy\xe2\x80\x99s person again. He\r\nrepressed the instinct, and his form drooped as with pain.\r\n\r\nTroy went on\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall soon purchase my discharge, and then\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I wish you to hasten on this marriage! It will be better for you\r\nboth. You love each other, and you must let me help you to do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, by settling the five hundred on Bathsheba instead of Fanny, to\r\nenable you to marry at once. No; she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have it of me. I\xe2\x80\x99ll pay\r\nit down to you on the wedding-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTroy paused in secret amazement at Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s wild infatuation. He\r\ncarelessly said, \xe2\x80\x9cAnd am I to have anything now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, if you wish to. But I have not much additional money with me. I\r\ndid not expect this; but all I have is yours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBoldwood, more like a somnambulist than a wakeful man, pulled out the\r\nlarge canvas bag he carried by way of a purse, and searched it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have twenty-one pounds more with me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cTwo notes and a\r\nsovereign. But before I leave you I must have a paper signed\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPay me the money, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll go straight to her parlour, and make any\r\narrangement you please to secure my compliance with your wishes. But\r\nshe must know nothing of this cash business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing, nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Boldwood, hastily. \xe2\x80\x9cHere is the sum, and if\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll come to my house we\xe2\x80\x99ll write out the agreement for the\r\nremainder, and the terms also.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFirst we\xe2\x80\x99ll call upon her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why? Come with me to-night, and go with me to-morrow to the\r\nsurrogate\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut she must be consulted; at any rate informed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well; go on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey went up the hill to Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s house. When they stood at the\r\nentrance, Troy said, \xe2\x80\x9cWait here a moment.\xe2\x80\x9d Opening the door, he glided\r\ninside, leaving the door ajar.\r\n\r\nBoldwood waited. In two minutes a light appeared in the passage.\r\nBoldwood then saw that the chain had been fastened across the door.\r\nTroy appeared inside, carrying a bedroom candlestick.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, did you think I should break in?\xe2\x80\x9d said Boldwood, contemptuously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no, it is merely my humour to secure things. Will you read this a\r\nmoment? I\xe2\x80\x99ll hold the light.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTroy handed a folded newspaper through the slit between door and\r\ndoorpost, and put the candle close. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the paragraph,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\nplacing his finger on a line.\r\n\r\nBoldwood looked and read\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nMARRIAGES.\r\nOn the 17th inst., at St. Ambrose\xe2\x80\x99s Church, Bath, by the Rev. G.\r\nMincing, B.A., Francis Troy, only son of the late Edward Troy, Esq.,\r\nM.D., of Weatherbury, and sergeant with Dragoon Guards, to Bathsheba,\r\nonly surviving daughter of the late Mr. John Everdene, of Casterbridge.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis may be called Fort meeting Feeble, hey, Boldwood?\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy. A\r\nlow gurgle of derisive laughter followed the words.\r\n\r\nThe paper fell from Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s hands. Troy continued\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFifty pounds to marry Fanny. Good. Twenty-one pounds not to marry\r\nFanny, but Bathsheba. Good. Finale: already Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s husband. Now,\r\nBoldwood, yours is the ridiculous fate which always attends\r\ninterference between a man and his wife. And another word. Bad as I am,\r\nI am not such a villain as to make the marriage or misery of any woman\r\na matter of huckster and sale. Fanny has long ago left me. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nwhere she is. I have searched everywhere. Another word yet. You say you\r\nlove Bathsheba; yet on the merest apparent evidence you instantly\r\nbelieve in her dishonour. A fig for such love! Now that I\xe2\x80\x99ve taught you\r\na lesson, take your money back again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will not; I will not!\xe2\x80\x9d said Boldwood, in a hiss.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnyhow I won\xe2\x80\x99t have it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy, contemptuously. He wrapped the\r\npacket of gold in the notes, and threw the whole into the road.\r\n\r\nBoldwood shook his clenched fist at him. \xe2\x80\x9cYou juggler of Satan! You\r\nblack hound! But I\xe2\x80\x99ll punish you yet; mark me, I\xe2\x80\x99ll punish you yet!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnother peal of laughter. Troy then closed the door, and locked himself\r\nin.\r\n\r\nThroughout the whole of that night Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s dark form might have been\r\nseen walking about the hills and downs of Weatherbury like an unhappy\r\nShade in the Mournful Fields by Acheron.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXV\r\nAT AN UPPER WINDOW\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was very early the next morning\xe2\x80\x94a time of sun and dew. The confused\r\nbeginnings of many birds\xe2\x80\x99 songs spread into the healthy air, and the\r\nwan blue of the heaven was here and there coated with thin webs of\r\nincorporeal cloud which were of no effect in obscuring day. All the\r\nlights in the scene were yellow as to colour, and all the shadows were\r\nattenuated as to form. The creeping plants about the old manor-house\r\nwere bowed with rows of heavy water drops, which had upon objects\r\nbehind them the effect of minute lenses of high magnifying power.\r\n\r\nJust before the clock struck five Gabriel Oak and Coggan passed the\r\nvillage cross, and went on together to the fields. They were yet barely\r\nin view of their mistress\xe2\x80\x99s house, when Oak fancied he saw the opening\r\nof a casement in one of the upper windows. The two men were at this\r\nmoment partially screened by an elder bush, now beginning to be\r\nenriched with black bunches of fruit, and they paused before emerging\r\nfrom its shade.\r\n\r\nA handsome man leaned idly from the lattice. He looked east and then\r\nwest, in the manner of one who makes a first morning survey. The man\r\nwas Sergeant Troy. His red jacket was loosely thrown on, but not\r\nbuttoned, and he had altogether the relaxed bearing of a soldier taking\r\nhis ease.\r\n\r\nCoggan spoke first, looking quietly at the window.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has married him!\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nGabriel had previously beheld the sight, and he now stood with his back\r\nturned, making no reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fancied we should know something to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Coggan. \xe2\x80\x9cI heard\r\nwheels pass my door just after dark\xe2\x80\x94you were out somewhere.\xe2\x80\x9d He glanced\r\nround upon Gabriel. \xe2\x80\x9cGood heavens above us, Oak, how white your face\r\nis; you look like a corpse!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo I?\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak, with a faint smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLean on the gate: I\xe2\x80\x99ll wait a bit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, all right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey stood by the gate awhile, Gabriel listlessly staring at the\r\nground. His mind sped into the future, and saw there enacted in years\r\nof leisure the scenes of repentance that would ensue from this work of\r\nhaste. That they were married he had instantly decided. Why had it been\r\nso mysteriously managed? It had become known that she had had a fearful\r\njourney to Bath, owing to her miscalculating the distance: that the\r\nhorse had broken down, and that she had been more than two days getting\r\nthere. It was not Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s way to do things furtively. With all her\r\nfaults, she was candour itself. Could she have been entrapped? The\r\nunion was not only an unutterable grief to him: it amazed him,\r\nnotwithstanding that he had passed the preceding week in a suspicion\r\nthat such might be the issue of Troy\xe2\x80\x99s meeting her away from home. Her\r\nquiet return with Liddy had to some extent dispersed the dread. Just as\r\nthat imperceptible motion which appears like stillness is infinitely\r\ndivided in its properties from stillness itself, so had his hope\r\nundistinguishable from despair differed from despair indeed.\r\n\r\nIn a few minutes they moved on again towards the house. The sergeant\r\nstill looked from the window.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMorning, comrades!\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted, in a cheery voice, when they came up.\r\n\r\nCoggan replied to the greeting. \xe2\x80\x9cBain\xe2\x80\x99t ye going to answer the man?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nthen said to Gabriel. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d say good morning\xe2\x80\x94you needn\xe2\x80\x99t spend a hapenny\r\nof meaning upon it, and yet keep the man civil.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGabriel soon decided too that, since the deed was done, to put the best\r\nface upon the matter would be the greatest kindness to her he loved.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood morning, Sergeant Troy,\xe2\x80\x9d he returned, in a ghastly voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA rambling, gloomy house this,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy\xe2\x80\x94they _may_ not be married!\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Coggan. \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps she\xe2\x80\x99s not\r\nthere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGabriel shook his head. The soldier turned a little towards the east,\r\nand the sun kindled his scarlet coat to an orange glow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it is a nice old house,\xe2\x80\x9d responded Gabriel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94I suppose so; but I feel like new wine in an old bottle here. My\r\nnotion is that sash-windows should be put throughout, and these old\r\nwainscoted walls brightened up a bit; or the oak cleared quite away,\r\nand the walls papered.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be a pity, I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, no. A philosopher once said in my hearing that the old builders,\r\nwho worked when art was a living thing, had no respect for the work of\r\nbuilders who went before them, but pulled down and altered as they\r\nthought fit; and why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t we? \xe2\x80\x98Creation and preservation don\xe2\x80\x99t do\r\nwell together,\xe2\x80\x99 says he, \xe2\x80\x98and a million of antiquarians can\xe2\x80\x99t invent a\r\nstyle.\xe2\x80\x99 My mind exactly. I am for making this place more modern, that\r\nwe may be cheerful whilst we can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe military man turned and surveyed the interior of the room, to\r\nassist his ideas of improvement in this direction. Gabriel and Coggan\r\nbegan to move on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Coggan,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy, as if inspired by a recollection \xe2\x80\x9cdo you know\r\nif insanity has ever appeared in Mr. Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s family?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJan reflected for a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI once heard that an uncle of his was queer in his head, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow the rights o\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is of no importance,\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy, lightly. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I shall be down in\r\nthe fields with you some time this week; but I have a few matters to\r\nattend to first. So good-day to you. We shall, of course, keep on just\r\nas friendly terms as usual. I\xe2\x80\x99m not a proud man: nobody is ever able to\r\nsay that of Sergeant Troy.
