Troy hastily shifted his position, after\r\nhaving recognized in the scrutineer the knavish bailiff Pennyways, his\r\nwife\xe2\x80\x99s sworn enemy, who still hung about the outskirts of Weatherbury.\r\n\r\nAt first Troy resolved to take no notice and abide by circumstances.\r\nThat he had been recognized by this man was highly probable; yet there\r\nwas room for a doubt. Then the great objection he had felt to allowing\r\nnews of his proximity to precede him to Weatherbury in the event of his\r\nreturn, based on a feeling that knowledge of his present occupation\r\nwould discredit him still further in his wife\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, returned in full\r\nforce. Moreover, should he resolve not to return at all, a tale of his\r\nbeing alive and being in the neighbourhood would be awkward; and he was\r\nanxious to acquire a knowledge of his wife\xe2\x80\x99s temporal affairs before\r\ndeciding which to do.\r\n\r\nIn this dilemma Troy at once went out to reconnoitre. It occurred to\r\nhim that to find Pennyways, and make a friend of him if possible, would\r\nbe a very wise act. He had put on a thick beard borrowed from the\r\nestablishment, and in this he wandered about the fair-field. It was now\r\nalmost dark, and respectable people were getting their carts and gigs\r\nready to go home.\r\n\r\nThe largest refreshment booth in the fair was provided by an innkeeper\r\nfrom a neighbouring town. This was considered an unexceptionable place\r\nfor obtaining the necessary food and rest: Host Trencher (as he was\r\njauntily called by the local newspaper) being a substantial man of high\r\nrepute for catering through all the country round. The tent was divided\r\ninto first and second-class compartments, and at the end of the\r\nfirst-class division was a yet further enclosure for the most\r\nexclusive, fenced off from the body of the tent by a luncheon-bar,\r\nbehind which the host himself stood bustling about in white apron and\r\nshirt-sleeves, and looking as if he had never lived anywhere but under\r\ncanvas all his life. In these penetralia were chairs and a table,\r\nwhich, on candles being lighted, made quite a cozy and luxurious show,\r\nwith an urn, plated tea and coffee pots, china teacups, and plum cakes.\r\n\r\nTroy stood at the entrance to the booth, where a gipsy-woman was frying\r\npancakes over a little fire of sticks and selling them at a penny\r\na-piece, and looked over the heads of the people within. He could see\r\nnothing of Pennyways, but he soon discerned Bathsheba through an\r\nopening into the reserved space at the further end. Troy thereupon\r\nretreated, went round the tent into the darkness, and listened. He\r\ncould hear Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s voice immediately inside the canvas; she was\r\nconversing with a man. A warmth overspread his face: surely she was not\r\nso unprincipled as to flirt in a fair! He wondered if, then, she\r\nreckoned upon his death as an absolute certainty. To get at the root of\r\nthe matter, Troy took a penknife from his pocket and softly made two\r\nlittle cuts crosswise in the cloth, which, by folding back the corners\r\nleft a hole the size of a wafer. Close to this he placed his face,\r\nwithdrawing it again in a movement of surprise; for his eye had been\r\nwithin twelve inches of the top of Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s head. It was too near to\r\nbe convenient. He made another hole a little to one side and lower\r\ndown, in a shaded place beside her chair, from which it was easy and\r\nsafe to survey her by looking horizontally.\r\n\r\nTroy took in the scene completely now. She was leaning back, sipping a\r\ncup of tea that she held in her hand, and the owner of the male voice\r\nwas Boldwood, who had apparently just brought the cup to her,\r\nBathsheba, being in a negligent mood, leant so idly against the canvas\r\nthat it was pressed to the shape of her shoulder, and she was, in fact,\r\nas good as in Troy\xe2\x80\x99s arms; and he was obliged to keep his breast\r\ncarefully backward that she might not feel its warmth through the cloth\r\nas he gazed in.\r\n\r\nTroy found unexpected chords of feeling to be stirred again within him\r\nas they had been stirred earlier in the day. She was handsome as ever,\r\nand she was his. It was some minutes before he could counteract his\r\nsudden wish to go in, and claim her. Then he thought how the proud girl\r\nwho had always looked down upon him even whilst it was to love him,\r\nwould hate him on discovering him to be a strolling player. Were he to\r\nmake himself known, that chapter of his life must at all risks be kept\r\nfor ever from her and from the Weatherbury people, or his name would be\r\na byword throughout the parish. He would be nicknamed \xe2\x80\x9cTurpin\xe2\x80\x9d as long\r\nas he lived. Assuredly before he could claim her these few past months\r\nof his existence must be entirely blotted out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I get you another cup before you start, ma\xe2\x80\x99am?\xe2\x80\x9d said Farmer\r\nBoldwood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I must be going at once. It was great\r\nneglect in that man to keep me waiting here till so late. I should have\r\ngone two hours ago, if it had not been for him. I had no idea of coming\r\nin here; but there\xe2\x80\x99s nothing so refreshing as a cup of tea, though I\r\nshould never have got one if you hadn\xe2\x80\x99t helped me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTroy scrutinized her cheek as lit by the candles, and watched each\r\nvarying shade thereon, and the white shell-like sinuosities of her\r\nlittle ear. She took out her purse and was insisting to Boldwood on\r\npaying for her tea for herself, when at this moment Pennyways entered\r\nthe tent. Troy trembled: here was his scheme for respectability\r\nendangered at once. He was about to leave his hole of espial, attempt\r\nto follow Pennyways, and find out if the ex-bailiff had recognized him,\r\nwhen he was arrested by the conversation, and found he was too late.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x9d said Pennyways; \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve some private information for\r\nyour ear alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot hear it now,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, coldly. That Bathsheba could not\r\nendure this man was evident; in fact, he was continually coming to her\r\nwith some tale or other, by which he might creep into favour at the\r\nexpense of persons maligned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll write it down,\xe2\x80\x9d said Pennyways, confidently. He stooped over the\r\ntable, pulled a leaf from a warped pocket-book, and wrote upon the\r\npaper, in a round hand\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Your husband is here. I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen him. Who\xe2\x80\x99s the fool now?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis he folded small, and handed towards her. Bathsheba would not read\r\nit; she would not even put out her hand to take it. Pennyways, then,\r\nwith a laugh of derision, tossed it into her lap, and, turning away,\r\nleft her.\r\n\r\nFrom the words and action of Pennyways, Troy, though he had not been\r\nable to see what the ex-bailiff wrote, had not a moment\xe2\x80\x99s doubt that\r\nthe note referred to him. Nothing that he could think of could be done\r\nto check the exposure. \xe2\x80\x9cCurse my luck!\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered, and added\r\nimprecations which rustled in the gloom like a pestilent wind.\r\nMeanwhile Boldwood said, taking up the note from her lap\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you wish to read it, Mrs. Troy? If not, I\xe2\x80\x99ll destroy it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, carelessly, \xe2\x80\x9cperhaps it is unjust not to\r\nread it; but I can guess what it is about. He wants me to recommend\r\nhim, or it is to tell me of some little scandal or another connected\r\nwith my work-people. He\xe2\x80\x99s always doing that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba held the note in her right hand. Boldwood handed towards her\r\na plate of cut bread-and-butter; when, in order to take a slice, she\r\nput the note into her left hand, where she was still holding the purse,\r\nand then allowed her hand to drop beside her close to the canvas. The\r\nmoment had come for saving his game, and Troy impulsively felt that he\r\nwould play the card. For yet another time he looked at the fair hand,\r\nand saw the pink finger-tips, and the blue veins of the wrist,\r\nencircled by a bracelet of coral chippings which she wore: how familiar\r\nit all was to him! Then, with the lightning action in which he was such\r\nan adept, he noiselessly slipped his hand under the bottom of the\r\ntent-cloth, which was far from being pinned tightly down, lifted it a\r\nlittle way, keeping his eye to the hole, snatched the note from her\r\nfingers, dropped the canvas, and ran away in the gloom towards the bank\r\nand ditch, smiling at the scream of astonishment which burst from her.\r\nTroy then slid down on the outside of the rampart, hastened round in\r\nthe bottom of the entrenchment to a distance of a hundred yards,\r\nascended again, and crossed boldly in a slow walk towards the front\r\nentrance of the tent. His object was now to get to Pennyways, and\r\nprevent a repetition of the announcement until such time as he should\r\nchoose.\r\n\r\nTroy reached the tent door, and standing among the groups there\r\ngathered, looked anxiously for Pennyways, evidently not wishing to make\r\nhimself prominent by inquiring for him. One or two men were speaking of\r\na daring attempt that had just been made to rob a young lady by lifting\r\nthe canvas of the tent beside her. It was supposed that the rogue had\r\nimagined a slip of paper which she held in her hand to be a bank note,\r\nfor he had seized it, and made off with it, leaving her purse behind.\r\nHis chagrin and disappointment at discovering its worthlessness would\r\nbe a good joke, it was said. However, the occurrence seemed to have\r\nbecome known to few, for it had not interrupted a fiddler, who had\r\nlately begun playing by the door of the tent, nor the four bowed old\r\nmen with grim countenances and walking-sticks in hand, who were dancing\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMajor Malley\xe2\x80\x99s Reel\xe2\x80\x9d to the tune. Behind these stood Pennyways. Troy\r\nglided up to him, beckoned, and whispered a few words; and with a\r\nmutual glance of concurrence the two men went into the night together.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER LI\r\nBATHSHEBA TALKS WITH HER OUTRIDER\r\n\r\n\r\nThe arrangement for getting back again to Weatherbury had been that Oak\r\nshould take the place of Poorgrass in Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s conveyance and drive\r\nher home, it being discovered late in the afternoon that Joseph was\r\nsuffering from his old complaint, a multiplying eye, and was,\r\ntherefore, hardly trustworthy as coachman and protector to a woman. But\r\nOak had found himself so occupied, and was full of so many cares\r\nrelative to those portions of Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s flocks that were not disposed\r\nof, that Bathsheba, without telling Oak or anybody, resolved to drive\r\nhome herself, as she had many times done from Casterbridge Market, and\r\ntrust to her good angel for performing the journey unmolested. But\r\nhaving fallen in with Farmer Boldwood accidentally (on her part at\r\nleast) at the refreshment-tent, she found it impossible to refuse his\r\noffer to ride on horseback beside her as escort. It had grown twilight\r\nbefore she was aware, but Boldwood assured her that there was no cause\r\nfor uneasiness, as the moon would be up in half-an-hour.\r\n\r\nImmediately after the incident in the tent, she had risen to go\xe2\x80\x94now\r\nabsolutely alarmed and really grateful for her old lover\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nprotection\xe2\x80\x94though regretting Gabriel\xe2\x80\x99s absence, whose company she would\r\nhave much preferred, as being more proper as well as more pleasant,\r\nsince he was her own managing-man and servant. This, however, could not\r\nbe helped; she would not, on any consideration, treat Boldwood harshly,\r\nhaving once already ill-used him, and the moon having risen, and the\r\ngig being ready, she drove across the hilltop in the wending way\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhich led downwards\xe2\x80\x94to oblivious obscurity, as it seemed, for the moon\r\nand the hill it flooded with light were in appearance on a level, the\r\nrest of the world lying as a vast shady concave between them. Boldwood\r\nmounted his horse, and followed in close attendance behind. Thus they\r\ndescended into the lowlands, and the sounds of those left on the hill\r\ncame like voices from the sky, and the lights were as those of a camp\r\nin heaven. They soon passed the merry stragglers in the immediate\r\nvicinity of the hill, traversed Kingsbere, and got upon the high road.\r\n\r\nThe keen instincts of Bathsheba had perceived that the farmer\xe2\x80\x99s staunch\r\ndevotion to herself was still undiminished, and she sympathized deeply.\r\nThe sight had quite depressed her this evening; had reminded her of her\r\nfolly; she wished anew, as she had wished many months ago, for some\r\nmeans of making reparation for her fault. Hence her pity for the man\r\nwho so persistently loved on to his own injury and permanent gloom had\r\nbetrayed Bathsheba into an injudicious considerateness of manner, which\r\nappeared almost like tenderness, and gave new vigour to the exquisite\r\ndream of a Jacob\xe2\x80\x99s seven years service in poor Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s mind.\r\n\r\nHe soon found an excuse for advancing from his position in the rear,\r\nand rode close by her side. They had gone two or three miles in the\r\nmoonlight, speaking desultorily across the wheel of her gig concerning\r\nthe fair, farming, Oak\xe2\x80\x99s usefulness to them both, and other indifferent\r\nsubjects, when Boldwood said suddenly and simply\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Troy, you will marry again some day?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis point-blank query unmistakably confused her, and it was not till a\r\nminute or more had elapsed that she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI have not seriously thought\r\nof any such subject.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI quite understand that. Yet your late husband has been dead nearly\r\none year, and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou forget that his death was never absolutely proved, and may not\r\nhave taken place; so that I may not be really a widow,\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\ncatching at the straw of escape that the fact afforded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot absolutely proved, perhaps, but it was proved circumstantially. A\r\nman saw him drowning, too. No reasonable person has any doubt of his\r\ndeath; nor have you, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, I should imagine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have none now, or I should have acted differently,\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\ngently. \xe2\x80\x9cI certainly, at first, had a strange unaccountable feeling\r\nthat he could not have perished, but I have been able to explain that\r\nin several ways since. But though I am fully persuaded that I shall see\r\nhim no more, I am far from thinking of marriage with another. I should\r\nbe very contemptible to indulge in such a thought.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were silent now awhile, and having struck into an unfrequented\r\ntrack across a common, the creaks of Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s saddle and her gig\r\nsprings were all the sounds to be heard. Boldwood ended the pause.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you remember when I carried you fainting in my arms into the King\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nArms, in Casterbridge? Every dog has his day: that was mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know\xe2\x80\x94I know it all,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, hurriedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI, for one, shall never cease regretting that events so fell out as to\r\ndeny you to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI, too, am very sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, and then checked herself. \xe2\x80\x9cI mean,\r\nyou know, I am sorry you thought I\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have always this dreary pleasure in thinking over those past times\r\nwith you\xe2\x80\x94that I was something to you before _he_ was anything, and that\r\nyou belonged _almost_ to me. It is difficult for a woman\r\nto define her feelings in language which is chiefly made by men to\r\nexpress theirs. My treatment of you was thoughtless, inexcusable,\r\nwicked! I shall eternally regret it. If there had been anything I could\r\nhave done to make amends I would most gladly have done it\xe2\x80\x94there was\r\nnothing on earth I so longed to do as to repair the error. But don\xe2\x80\x99t talk of\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94seven or six years\xe2\x80\x94where may we all be by that time?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey will soon glide by, and it will seem an astonishingly short time\r\nto look back upon when they are past\xe2\x80\x94much less than to look forward to\r\nnow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes; I have found that in my own experience.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow listen once more,\xe2\x80\x9d Boldwood pleaded. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I wait that time, will\r\nyou marry me? You own that you owe me amends\xe2\x80\x94let that be your way of\r\nmaking them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, Mr. Boldwood\xe2\x80\x94six years\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you want to be the wife of any other man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo indeed! I mean, that I don\xe2\x80\x99t like to talk about this matter now.\r\nPerhaps it is not proper, and I ought not to allow it. Haven\xe2\x80\x99t you been almost mine once\r\nalready? Surely you can say to me as much as this, you will have me\r\nback again should circumstances permit? Now, pray speak! O Bathsheba,\r\npromise\xe2\x80\x94it is only a little promise\xe2\x80\x94that if you marry again, you will\r\nmarry me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis tone was so excited that she almost feared him at this moment, even\r\nwhilst she sympathized. I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlove you, and I much fear that I never shall love you as much as a\r\nwoman ought to love a husband. If you, sir, know that, and I can yet\r\ngive you happiness by a mere promise to marry at the end of six years,\r\nif my husband should not come back, it is a great honour to me. I mean soon. Christmas, we\xe2\x80\x99ll say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChristmas!\xe2\x80\x9d He said nothing further till he added: \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ll say no\r\nmore to you about it till that time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba was in a very peculiar state of mind, which showed how\r\nentirely the soul is the slave of the body, the ethereal spirit\r\ndependent for its quality upon the tangible flesh and blood. It is\r\nhardly too much to say that she felt coerced by a force stronger than\r\nher own will, not only into the act of promising upon this singularly\r\nremote and vague matter, but into the emotion of fancying that she\r\nought to promise. When the weeks intervening between the night of this\r\nconversation and Christmas day began perceptibly to diminish, her\r\nanxiety and perplexity increased.\r\n\r\nOne day she was led by an accident into an oddly confidential dialogue\r\nwith Gabriel about her difficulty. It afforded her a little relief\xe2\x80\x94of a\r\ndull and cheerless kind. They were auditing accounts, and something\r\noccurred in the course of their labours which led Oak to say, speaking\r\nof Boldwood, \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99ll never forget you, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, never.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen out came her trouble before she was aware; and she told him how\r\nshe had again got into the toils; what Boldwood had asked her, and how\r\nhe was expecting her assent. \xe2\x80\x9cThe most mournful reason of all for my\r\nagreeing to it,\xe2\x80\x9d she said sadly, \xe2\x80\x9cand the true reason why I think to do\r\nso for good or for evil, is this\xe2\x80\x94it is a thing I have not breathed to a\r\nliving soul as yet\xe2\x80\x94I believe that if I don\xe2\x80\x99t give my word, he\xe2\x80\x99ll go out\r\nof his mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally, do ye?\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel, gravely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe this,\xe2\x80\x9d she continued, with reckless frankness; \xe2\x80\x9cand Heaven\r\nknows I say it in a spirit the very reverse of vain, for I am grieved\r\nand troubled to my soul about it\xe2\x80\x94I believe I hold that man\xe2\x80\x99s future in\r\nmy hand. O\r\nGabriel, I tremble at my responsibility, for it is terrible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I think this much, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, as I told you years ago,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthat his life is a total blank whenever he isn\xe2\x80\x99t hoping for \xe2\x80\x99ee; but I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t suppose\xe2\x80\x94I hope that nothing so dreadful hangs on to it as you\r\nfancy. And six\r\nyears\xe2\x80\x94why we may all be in our graves by that time, even if Mr. Troy\r\ndoes not come back again, which he may not impossibly do! Such thoughts\r\ngive a sort of absurdity to the scheme. Now, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it preposterous,\r\nGabriel? However he came to dream of it, I cannot think. But is it\r\nwrong? If wild heat had to do wi\xe2\x80\x99 it, making\r\nye long to over-come the awkwardness about your husband\xe2\x80\x99s vanishing, it\r\nmid be wrong; but a cold-hearted agreement to oblige a man seems\r\ndifferent, somehow. If I had never played a trick\r\nupon him, he would never have wanted to marry me. Well, there\xe2\x80\x99s the debt, which can only\r\nbe discharged in one way, and I believe I am bound to do it if it\r\nhonestly lies in my power, without any consideration of my own future\r\nat all. When a rake gambles away his expectations, the fact that it is\r\nan inconvenient debt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t make him the less liable. I\xe2\x80\x99ve been a\r\nrake, and the single point I ask you is, considering that my own\r\nscruples, and the fact that in the eye of the law my husband is only\r\nmissing, will keep any man from marrying me until seven years have\r\npassed\xe2\x80\x94am I free to entertain such an idea, even though \xe2\x80\x99tis a sort of\r\npenance\xe2\x80\x94for it will be that? I well know what would have\r\nbrought him back long before this time if he had lived.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, in a religious sense you will be as free to _think_ o\xe2\x80\x99\r\nmarrying again as any real widow of one year\xe2\x80\x99s standing. But why don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nye ask Mr. Thirdly\xe2\x80\x99s advice on how to treat Mr. Boldwood?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. When I want a broad-minded opinion for general enlightenment,\r\ndistinct from special advice, I never go to a man who deals in the\r\nsubject professionally. So I like the parson\xe2\x80\x99s opinion on law, the\r\nlawyer\xe2\x80\x99s on doctoring, the doctor\xe2\x80\x99s on business, and my\r\nbusiness-man\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94that is, yours\xe2\x80\x94on morals.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd on love\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid there\xe2\x80\x99s a hitch in that argument,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak, with a grave\r\nsmile.\r\n\r\nShe did not reply at once, and then saying, \xe2\x80\x9cGood evening, Mr. Oak,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nwent away.\r\n\r\nShe had spoken frankly, and neither asked nor expected any reply from\r\nGabriel more satisfactory than that she had obtained. Yet in the\r\ncentremost parts of her complicated heart there existed at this minute\r\na little pang of disappointment, for a reason she would not allow\r\nherself to recognize. Oak had not once wished her free that he might\r\nmarry her himself\xe2\x80\x94had not once said, \xe2\x80\x9cI could wait for you as well as\r\nhe.\xe2\x80\x9d That was the insect sting. Not that she would have listened to any\r\nsuch hypothesis. O no\xe2\x80\x94for wasn\xe2\x80\x99t she saying all the time that such\r\nthoughts of the future were improper, and wasn\xe2\x80\x99t Gabriel far too poor a\r\nman to speak sentiment to her? Yet he might have just hinted about that\r\nold love of his, and asked, in a playful off-hand way, if he might\r\nspeak of it. It would have seemed pretty and sweet, if no more; and\r\nthen she would have shown how kind and inoffensive a woman\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x9d can\r\nsometimes be. But to give such cool advice\xe2\x80\x94the very advice she had\r\nasked for\xe2\x80\x94it ruffled our heroine all the afternoon.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER LII\r\nCONVERGING COURSES\r\n\r\nI\r\n\r\nChristmas-eve came, and a party that Boldwood was to give in the\r\nevening was the great subject of talk in Weatherbury. It was not that\r\nthe rarity of Christmas parties in the parish made this one a wonder,\r\nbut that Boldwood should be the giver. The announcement had had an\r\nabnormal and incongruous sound, as if one should hear of\r\ncroquet-playing in a cathedral aisle, or that some much-respected judge\r\nwas going upon the stage. That the party was intended to be a truly\r\njovial one there was no room for doubt. A large bough of mistletoe had\r\nbeen brought from the woods that day, and suspended in the hall of the\r\nbachelor\xe2\x80\x99s home. Holly and ivy had followed in armfuls. From six that\r\nmorning till past noon the huge wood fire in the kitchen roared and\r\nsparkled at its highest, the kettle, the saucepan, and the three-legged\r\npot appearing in the midst of the flames like Shadrach, Meshach, and\r\nAbednego; moreover, roasting and basting operations were continually\r\ncarried on in front of the genial blaze.\r\n\r\nAs it grew later the fire was made up in the large long hall into which\r\nthe staircase descended, and all encumbrances were cleared out for\r\ndancing. The log which was to form the back-brand of the evening fire\r\nwas the uncleft trunk of a tree, so unwieldy that it could be neither\r\nbrought nor rolled to its place; and accordingly two men were to be\r\nobserved dragging and heaving it in by chains and levers as the hour of\r\nassembly drew near.\r\n\r\nIn spite of all this, the spirit of revelry was wanting in the\r\natmosphere of the house. Such a thing had never been attempted before\r\nby its owner, and it was now done as by a wrench. Intended gaieties\r\nwould insist upon appearing like solemn grandeurs, the organization of\r\nthe whole effort was carried out coldly, by hirelings, and a shadow\r\nseemed to move about the rooms, saying that the proceedings were\r\nunnatural to the place and the lone man who lived therein, and hence\r\nnot good.\r\n\r\nII\r\n\r\nBathsheba was at this time in her room, dressing for the event. She had\r\ncalled for candles, and Liddy entered and placed one on each side of\r\nher mistress\xe2\x80\x99s glass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t go away, Liddy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, almost timidly. \xe2\x80\x9cI am foolishly\r\nagitated\xe2\x80\x94I cannot tell why. I wish I had not been obliged to go to this\r\ndance; but there\xe2\x80\x99s no escaping now. I have not spoken to Mr. Boldwood\r\nsince the autumn, when I promised to see him at Christmas on business,\r\nbut I had no idea there was to be anything of this kind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I would go now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Liddy, who was going with her; for Boldwood\r\nhad been indiscriminate in his invitations.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I shall make my appearance, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I am\r\n_the cause_ of the party, and that upsets me!\xe2\x80\x94Don\xe2\x80\x99t tell, Liddy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, ma\xe2\x80\x99am. You the cause of it, ma\xe2\x80\x99am?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I am the reason of the party\xe2\x80\x94I. If it had not been for me, there\r\nwould never have been one. I can\xe2\x80\x99t explain any more\xe2\x80\x94there\xe2\x80\x99s no more to\r\nbe explained. I wish I had never seen Weatherbury.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s wicked of you\xe2\x80\x94to wish to be worse off than you are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Liddy. I have never been free from trouble since I have lived\r\nhere, and this party is likely to bring me more. Now, fetch my black\r\nsilk dress, and see how it sits upon me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you will leave off that, surely, ma\xe2\x80\x99am? You have been a widow-lady\r\nfourteen months, and ought to brighten up a little on such a night as\r\nthis.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it necessary? No; I will appear as usual, for if I were to wear any\r\nlight dress people would say things about me, and I should seem to be\r\nrejoicing when I am solemn all the time. The party doesn\xe2\x80\x99t suit me a\r\nbit; but never mind, stay and help to finish me off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIII\r\n\r\nBoldwood was dressing also at this hour. A tailor from Casterbridge was\r\nwith him, assisting him in the operation of trying on a new coat that\r\nhad just been brought home.\r\n\r\nNever had Boldwood been so fastidious, unreasonable about the fit, and\r\ngenerally difficult to please. The tailor walked round and round him,\r\ntugged at the waist, pulled the sleeve, pressed out the collar, and for\r\nthe first time in his experience Boldwood was not bored. Times had been\r\nwhen the farmer had exclaimed against all such niceties as childish,\r\nbut now no philosophic or hasty rebuke whatever was provoked by this\r\nman for attaching as much importance to a crease in the coat as to an\r\nearthquake in South America. Boldwood at last expressed himself nearly\r\nsatisfied, and paid the bill, the tailor passing out of the door just\r\nas Oak came in to report progress for the day.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Oak,\xe2\x80\x9d said Boldwood. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall of course see you here to-night.\r\nMake yourself merry. I am determined that neither expense nor trouble\r\nshall be spared.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll try to be here, sir, though perhaps it may not be very early,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Gabriel, quietly. \xe2\x80\x9cI am glad indeed to see such a change in \xe2\x80\x99ee\r\nfrom what it used to be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94I must own it\xe2\x80\x94I am bright to-night: cheerful and more than\r\ncheerful\xe2\x80\x94so much so that I am almost sad again with the sense that all\r\nof it is passing away. And sometimes, when I am excessively hopeful and\r\nblithe, a trouble is looming in the distance: so that I often get to\r\nlook upon gloom in me with content, and to fear a happy mood. Still\r\nthis may be absurd\xe2\x80\x94I feel that it is absurd. Perhaps my day is dawning\r\nat last.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope it \xe2\x80\x99ill be a long and a fair one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you\xe2\x80\x94thank you. Yet perhaps my cheerfulness rests on a slender\r\nhope. And yet I trust my hope. It is faith, not hope. I think this time\r\nI reckon with my host.\xe2\x80\x94Oak, my hands are a little shaky, or something;\r\nI can\xe2\x80\x99t tie this neckerchief properly. Perhaps you will tie it for me.\r\nThe fact is, I have not been well lately, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry to hear that, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s nothing. I want it done as well as you can, please. Is there\r\nany late knot in fashion, Oak?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak. His tone had sunk to sadness.\r\n\r\nBoldwood approached Gabriel, and as Oak tied the neckerchief the farmer\r\nwent on feverishly\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes a woman keep her promise, Gabriel?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it is not inconvenient to her she may.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94Or rather an implied promise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t answer for her implying,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak, with faint bitterness.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a word as full o\xe2\x80\x99 holes as a sieve with them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOak, don\xe2\x80\x99t talk like that. You have got quite cynical lately\xe2\x80\x94how is\r\nit? We seem to have shifted our positions: I have become the young and\r\nhopeful man, and you the old and unbelieving one. However, does a woman\r\nkeep a promise, not to marry, but to enter on an engagement to marry at\r\nsome time? Now you know women better than I\xe2\x80\x94tell me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afeard you honour my understanding too much. However, she may\r\nkeep such a promise, if it is made with an honest meaning to repair a\r\nwrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt has not gone far yet, but I think it will soon\xe2\x80\x94yes, I know it\r\nwill,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, in an impulsive whisper. \xe2\x80\x9cI have pressed her upon the\r\nsubject, and she inclines to be kind to me, and to think of me as a\r\nhusband at a long future time, and that\xe2\x80\x99s enough for me. How can I\r\nexpect more? She has a notion that a woman should not marry within\r\nseven years of her husband\xe2\x80\x99s disappearance\xe2\x80\x94that her own self shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t,\r\nI mean\xe2\x80\x94because his body was not found. It may be merely this legal\r\nreason which influences her, or it may be a religious one, but she is\r\nreluctant to talk on the point. Yet she has promised\xe2\x80\x94implied\xe2\x80\x94that she\r\nwill ratify an engagement to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeven years,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Oak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s no such thing!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with impatience. \xe2\x80\x9cFive years,\r\nnine months, and a few days. Fifteen months nearly have passed since he\r\nvanished, and is there anything so wonderful in an engagement of little\r\nmore than five years?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems long in a forward view. Don\xe2\x80\x99t build too much upon such\r\npromises, sir. Remember, you have once be\xe2\x80\x99n deceived. Her meaning may\r\nbe good; but there\xe2\x80\x94she\xe2\x80\x99s young yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDeceived? Never!\xe2\x80\x9d said Boldwood, vehemently. \xe2\x80\x9cShe never promised me at\r\nthat first time, and hence she did not break her promise! If she\r\npromises me, she\xe2\x80\x99ll marry me. Bathsheba is a woman to her word.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIV\r\n\r\nTroy was sitting in a corner of The White Hart tavern at Casterbridge,\r\nsmoking and drinking a steaming mixture from a glass. A knock was given\r\nat the door, and Pennyways entered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, have you seen him?\xe2\x80\x9d Troy inquired, pointing to a chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBoldwood?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94Lawyer Long.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe wadn\xe2\x80\x99 at home. I went there first, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a nuisance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis rather, I suppose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet I don\xe2\x80\x99t see that, because a man appears to be drowned and was not,\r\nhe should be liable for anything. I shan\xe2\x80\x99t ask any lawyer\xe2\x80\x94not I.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that\xe2\x80\x99s not it, exactly. If a man changes his name and so forth,\r\nand takes steps to deceive the world and his own wife, he\xe2\x80\x99s a cheat,\r\nand that in the eye of the law is ayless a rogue, and that is ayless a\r\nlammocken vagabond; and that\xe2\x80\x99s a punishable situation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHa-ha! Well done, Pennyways,\xe2\x80\x9d Troy had laughed, but it was with some\r\nanxiety that he said, \xe2\x80\x9cNow, what I want to know is this, do you think\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s really anything going on between her and Boldwood? Upon my\r\nsoul, I should never have believed it! How she must detest me! Have you\r\nfound out whether she has encouraged him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI haen\xe2\x80\x99t been able to learn. There\xe2\x80\x99s a deal of feeling on his side\r\nseemingly, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t answer for her. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know a word about any\r\nsuch thing till yesterday, and all I heard then was that she was gwine\r\nto the party at his house to-night. This is the first time she has ever\r\ngone there, they say. And they say that she\xe2\x80\x99ve not so much as spoke to\r\nhim since they were at Greenhill Fair: but what can folk believe o\xe2\x80\x99t?\r\nHowever, she\xe2\x80\x99s not fond of him\xe2\x80\x94quite offish and quite careless, I\r\nknow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not so sure of that.... She\xe2\x80\x99s a handsome woman, Pennyways, is she\r\nnot? Own that you never saw a finer or more splendid creature in your\r\nlife. Upon my honour, when I set eyes upon her that day I wondered what\r\nI could have been made of to be able to leave her by herself so long.\r\nAnd then I was hampered with that bothering show, which I\xe2\x80\x99m free of at\r\nlast, thank the stars.\xe2\x80\x9d He smoked on awhile, and then added, \xe2\x80\x9cHow did\r\nshe look when you passed by yesterday?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, she took no great heed of me, ye may well fancy; but she looked\r\nwell enough, far\xe2\x80\x99s I know. Just flashed her haughty eyes upon my poor\r\nscram body, and then let them go past me to what was yond, much as if\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99d been no more than a leafless tree. She had just got off her mare to\r\nlook at the last wring-down of cider for the year; she had been riding,\r\nand so her colours were up and her breath rather quick, so that her\r\nbosom plimmed and fell\xe2\x80\x94plimmed and fell\xe2\x80\x94every time plain to my eye. Ay,\r\nand there were the fellers round her wringing down the cheese and\r\nbustling about and saying, \xe2\x80\x98Ware o\xe2\x80\x99 the pommy, ma\xe2\x80\x99am: \xe2\x80\x99twill spoil yer\r\ngown.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Never mind me,\xe2\x80\x99 says she. Then Gabe brought her some of the new\r\ncider, and she must needs go drinking it through a strawmote, and not\r\nin a nateral way at all. \xe2\x80\x98Liddy,\xe2\x80\x99 says she, \xe2\x80\x98bring indoors a few\r\ngallons, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll make some cider-wine.\xe2\x80\x99 Sergeant, I was no more to her\r\nthan a morsel of scroff in the fuel-house!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must go and find her out at once\xe2\x80\x94O yes, I see that\xe2\x80\x94I must go. Oak is\r\nhead man still, isn\xe2\x80\x99t he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, \xe2\x80\x99a b\xe2\x80\x99lieve. And at Little Weatherbury Farm too. He manages\r\neverything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Twill puzzle him to manage her, or any other man of his compass!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know about that. She can\xe2\x80\x99t do without him, and knowing it well\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s pretty independent. And she\xe2\x80\x99ve a few soft corners to her mind,\r\nthough I\xe2\x80\x99ve never been able to get into one, the devil\xe2\x80\x99s in\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, baily, she\xe2\x80\x99s a notch above you, and you must own it: a higher\r\nclass of animal\xe2\x80\x94a finer tissue. However, stick to me, and neither this\r\nhaughty goddess, dashing piece of womanhood, Juno-wife of mine (Juno\r\nwas a goddess, you know), nor anybody else shall hurt you. But all this\r\nwants looking into, I perceive. What with one thing and another, I see\r\nthat my work is well cut out for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nV\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do I look to-night, Liddy?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, giving a final\r\nadjustment to her dress before leaving the glass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never saw you look so well before. Yes\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you when you looked\r\nlike it\xe2\x80\x94that night, a year and a half ago, when you came in so\r\nwildlike, and scolded us for making remarks about you and Mr. Troy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverybody will think that I am setting myself to captivate Mr.\r\nBoldwood, I suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cAt least they\xe2\x80\x99ll say so. I wish I could have continued quite alone as I have\r\nbeen for the last year or so, with no hopes and no fears, and no\r\npleasure and no grief.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow just suppose Mr. Boldwood should ask you\xe2\x80\x94only just suppose it\xe2\x80\x94to\r\nrun away with him, what would you do, ma\xe2\x80\x99am?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLiddy\xe2\x80\x94none of that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, gravely. \xe2\x80\x9cMind, I won\xe2\x80\x99t hear\r\njoking on any such matter. But knowing what rum things we women be, I just\r\nsaid\xe2\x80\x94however, I won\xe2\x80\x99t speak of it again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo marrying for me yet for many a year; if ever, \xe2\x80\x99twill be for reasons\r\nvery, very different from those you think, or others will believe! Now\r\nget my cloak, for it is time to go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nVI\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOak,\xe2\x80\x9d said Boldwood, \xe2\x80\x9cbefore you go I want to mention what has been\r\npassing in my mind lately\xe2\x80\x94that little arrangement we made about your\r\nshare in the farm I mean. That share is small, too small, considering\r\nhow little I attend to business now, and how much time and thought you\r\ngive to it. Well, since the world is brightening for me, I want to show\r\nmy sense of it by increasing your proportion in the partnership. I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nmake a memorandum of the arrangement which struck me as likely to be\r\nconvenient, for I haven\xe2\x80\x99t time to talk about it now; and then we\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ndiscuss it at our leisure. My intention is ultimately to retire from\r\nthe management altogether, and until you can take all the expenditure\r\nupon your shoulders, I\xe2\x80\x99ll be a sleeping partner in the stock. Then, if\r\nI marry her\xe2\x80\x94and I hope\xe2\x80\x94I feel I shall, why\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray don\xe2\x80\x99t speak of it, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak, hastily. \xe2\x80\x9cWe don\xe2\x80\x99t know what\r\nmay happen. But you have behaved like a man, and I, as a sort of\r\nsuccessful rival\xe2\x80\x94successful partly through your goodness of\r\nheart\xe2\x80\x94should like definitely to show my sense of your friendship under\r\nwhat must have been a great pain to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO that\xe2\x80\x99s not necessary, thank \xe2\x80\x99ee,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak, hurriedly. \xe2\x80\x9cI must get\r\nused to such as that; other men have, and so shall I.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOak then left him. He was uneasy on Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s account, for he saw anew\r\nthat this constant passion of the farmer made him not the man he once\r\nhad been.\r\n\r\nAs Boldwood continued awhile in his room alone\xe2\x80\x94ready and dressed to\r\nreceive his company\xe2\x80\x94the mood of anxiety about his appearance seemed to\r\npass away, and to be succeeded by a deep solemnity. He looked out of\r\nthe window, and regarded the dim outline of the trees upon the sky, and\r\nthe twilight deepening to darkness.\r\n\r\nThen he went to a locked closet, and took from a locked drawer therein\r\na small circular case the size of a pillbox, and was about to put it\r\ninto his pocket. But he lingered to open the cover and take a momentary\r\nglance inside. It contained a woman\xe2\x80\x99s finger-ring, set all the way\r\nround with small diamonds, and from its appearance had evidently been\r\nrecently purchased. Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s eyes dwelt upon its many sparkles a long\r\ntime, though that its material aspect concerned him little was plain\r\nfrom his manner and mien, which were those of a mind following out the\r\npresumed thread of that jewel\xe2\x80\x99s future history.\r\n\r\nThe noise of wheels at the front of the house became audible. Boldwood\r\nclosed the box, stowed it away carefully in his pocket, and went out\r\nupon the landing. The old man who was his indoor factotum came at the\r\nsame moment to the foot of the stairs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey be coming, sir\xe2\x80\x94lots of \xe2\x80\x99em\xe2\x80\x94a-foot and a-driving!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was coming down this moment. Yes; of course I have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not write to her? \xe2\x80\x99Tis a very queer corner that you have got into,\r\nsergeant. Now that\xe2\x80\x99s my outspoke\r\nmind, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve been called a long-headed feller here and there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll nonsense!\xe2\x80\x9d said Troy, angrily. \xe2\x80\x9cThere she is with plenty of money,\r\nand a house and farm, and horses, and comfort, and here am I living\r\nfrom hand to mouth\xe2\x80\x94a needy adventurer. She\xe2\x80\x99s bad enough for anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPennyways, mind who you are talking to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, sergeant, all I say is this, that if I were you I\xe2\x80\x99d go abroad\r\nagain where I came from\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99tisn\xe2\x80\x99t too late to do it now. Well, what is there\r\nbesides? A stick\xe2\x80\x94I must have a walking-stick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPennyways now felt himself to be in something of a difficulty, for\r\nshould Bathsheba and Troy become reconciled it would be necessary to\r\nregain her good opinion if he would secure the patronage of her\r\nhusband. \xe2\x80\x9cI sometimes think she likes you yet, and is a good woman at\r\nbottom,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as a saving sentence. \xe2\x80\x9cBut there\xe2\x80\x99s no telling to a\r\ncertainty from a body\xe2\x80\x99s outside. I shall not hurry\r\nalong the road, and shall be there then before nine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER LIII\r\nCONCURRITUR\xe2\x80\x94HOR\xc3\x86 MOMENTO\r\n\r\n\r\nOutside the front of Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s house a group of men stood in the dark,\r\nwith their faces towards the door, which occasionally opened and closed\r\nfor the passage of some guest or servant, when a golden rod of light\r\nwould stripe the ground for the moment and vanish again, leaving\r\nnothing outside but the glowworm shine of the pale lamp amid the\r\nevergreens over the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was seen in Casterbridge this afternoon\xe2\x80\x94so the boy said,\xe2\x80\x9d one of\r\nthem remarked in a whisper. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I for one believe it. A scoundrel to play a poor wife such tricks.\r\nNothing has prospered in Weatherbury since he came here. And now I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nno heart to go in.
