When he opened it the hum of bustle rolled\r\nout as a wave upon a still strand\xe2\x80\x94the assemblage being immediately\r\ninside the hall\xe2\x80\x94and was deadened to a murmur as he closed it again.\r\nEach man waited intently, and looked around at the dark tree tops\r\ngently rocking against the sky and occasionally shivering in a slight\r\nwind, as if he took interest in the scene, which neither did. One of\r\nthem began walking up and down, and then came to where he started from\r\nand stopped again, with a sense that walking was a thing not worth\r\ndoing now.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think Laban must have seen mistress by this time,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nSmallbury, breaking the silence. \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps she won\xe2\x80\x99t come and speak to\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe door opened. Tall appeared, and joined them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d said both.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t like to ask for her after all,\xe2\x80\x9d Laban faltered out. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\r\nwere all in such a stir, trying to put a little spirit into the party.\r\nSomehow the fun seems to hang fire, though everything\xe2\x80\x99s there that a\r\nheart can desire, and I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t for my soul interfere and throw damp\r\nupon it\xe2\x80\x94if \xe2\x80\x99twas to save my life, I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose we had better all go in together,\xe2\x80\x9d said Samway, gloomily.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps I may have a chance of saying a word to master.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo the men entered the hall, which was the room selected and arranged\r\nfor the gathering because of its size. The younger men and maids were\r\nat last just beginning to dance. Bathsheba had been perplexed how to\r\nact, for she was not much more than a slim young maid herself, and the\r\nweight of stateliness sat heavy upon her. Sometimes she thought she\r\nought not to have come under any circumstances; then she considered\r\nwhat cold unkindness that would have been, and finally resolved upon\r\nthe middle course of staying for about an hour only, and gliding off\r\nunobserved, having from the first made up her mind that she could on no\r\naccount dance, sing, or take any active part in the proceedings.\r\n\r\nHer allotted hour having been passed in chatting and looking on,\r\nBathsheba told Liddy not to hurry herself, and went to the small\r\nparlour to prepare for departure, which, like the hall, was decorated\r\nwith holly and ivy, and well lighted up.\r\n\r\nNobody was in the room, but she had hardly been there a moment when the\r\nmaster of the house entered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Troy\xe2\x80\x94you are not going?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve hardly begun!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you\xe2\x80\x99ll excuse me, I should like to go now.\xe2\x80\x9d Her manner was restive,\r\nfor she remembered her promise, and imagined what he was about to say.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut as it is not late,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, \xe2\x80\x9cI can walk home, and leave my man\r\nand Liddy to come when they choose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve been trying to get an opportunity of speaking to you,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nBoldwood. \xe2\x80\x9cYou know perhaps what I long to say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba silently looked on the floor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou do give it?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, eagerly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, that\xe2\x80\x99s evasion! Why, the promise. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to intrude upon\r\nyou at all, or to let it become known to anybody. But do give your\r\nword! A mere business compact, you know, between two people who are\r\nbeyond the influence of passion.\xe2\x80\x9d Boldwood knew how false this picture\r\nwas as regarded himself; but he had proved that it was the only tone in\r\nwhich she would allow him to approach her. \xe2\x80\x9cA promise to marry me at\r\nthe end of five years and three-quarters. You owe it to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI feel that I do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba; \xe2\x80\x9cthat is, if you demand it. But I\r\nam a changed woman\xe2\x80\x94an unhappy woman\xe2\x80\x94and not\xe2\x80\x94not\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are still a very beautiful woman,\xe2\x80\x9d said Boldwood. Honesty and pure\r\nconviction suggested the remark, unaccompanied by any perception that\r\nit might have been adopted by blunt flattery to soothe and win her.\r\n\r\nHowever, it had not much effect now, for she said, in a passionless\r\nmurmur which was in itself a proof of her words: \xe2\x80\x9cI have no feeling in\r\nthe matter at all. And I don\xe2\x80\x99t at all know what is right to do in my\r\ndifficult position, and I have nobody to advise me. But I give my\r\npromise, if I must. I give it as the rendering of a debt,\r\nconditionally, of course, on my being a widow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll marry me between five and six years hence?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t press me too hard. I\xe2\x80\x99ll marry nobody else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut surely you will name the time, or there\xe2\x80\x99s nothing in the promise\r\nat all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know, pray let me go!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, her bosom beginning to\r\nrise. \xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid what to do! I want to be just to you, and to be that\r\nseems to be wronging myself, and perhaps it is breaking the\r\ncommandments. There is considerable doubt of his death, and then it is\r\ndreadful; let me ask a solicitor, Mr. Boldwood, if I ought or no!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay the words, dear one, and the subject shall be dismissed; a\r\nblissful loving intimacy of six years, and then marriage\xe2\x80\x94O Bathsheba,\r\nsay them!\xe2\x80\x9d he begged in a husky voice, unable to sustain the forms of\r\nmere friendship any longer. \xe2\x80\x9cPromise yourself to me; I deserve it,\r\nindeed I do, for I have loved you more than anybody in the world! And\r\nif I said hasty words and showed uncalled-for heat of manner towards\r\nyou, believe me, dear, I did not mean to distress you; I was in agony,\r\nBathsheba, and I did not know what I said. You wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t let a dog\r\nsuffer what I have suffered, could you but know it! Sometimes I shrink\r\nfrom your knowing what I have felt for you, and sometimes I am\r\ndistressed that all of it you never will know. Be gracious, and give up\r\na little to me, when I would give up my life for you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe trimmings of her dress, as they quivered against the light, showed\r\nhow agitated she was, and at last she burst out crying. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nnot\xe2\x80\x94press me\xe2\x80\x94about anything more\xe2\x80\x94if I say in five or six years?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsobbed, when she had power to frame the words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, then I\xe2\x80\x99ll leave it to time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe waited a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cVery well. I\xe2\x80\x99ll marry you in six years from this\r\nday, if we both live,\xe2\x80\x9d she said solemnly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you\xe2\x80\x99ll take this as a token from me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBoldwood had come close to her side, and now he clasped one of her\r\nhands in both his own, and lifted it to his breast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it? Oh I cannot wear a ring!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, on seeing what he\r\nheld; \xe2\x80\x9cbesides, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have a soul know that it\xe2\x80\x99s an engagement!\r\nPerhaps it is improper? Besides, we are not engaged in the usual sense,\r\nare we? Don\xe2\x80\x99t insist, Mr. Boldwood\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d In her trouble at not being\r\nable to get her hand away from him at once, she stamped passionately on\r\nthe floor with one foot, and tears crowded to her eyes again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt means simply a pledge\xe2\x80\x94no sentiment\xe2\x80\x94the seal of a practical\r\ncompact,\xe2\x80\x9d he said more quietly, but still retaining her hand in his\r\nfirm grasp. \xe2\x80\x9cCome, now!\xe2\x80\x9d And Boldwood slipped the ring on her finger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot wear it,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, weeping as if her heart would break. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nfrighten me, almost. So wild a scheme! Please let me go home!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly to-night: wear it just to-night, to please me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba sat down in a chair, and buried her face in her handkerchief,\r\nthough Boldwood kept her hand yet. At length she said, in a sort of\r\nhopeless whisper\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then, I will to-night, if you wish it so earnestly. Now\r\nloosen my hand; I will, indeed I will wear it to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd it shall be the beginning of a pleasant secret courtship of six\r\nyears, with a wedding at the end?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must be, I suppose, since you will have it so!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, fairly\r\nbeaten into non-resistance.\r\n\r\nBoldwood pressed her hand, and allowed it to drop in her lap. \xe2\x80\x9cI am\r\nhappy now,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cGod bless you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe left the room, and when he thought she might be sufficiently\r\ncomposed sent one of the maids to her. Bathsheba cloaked the effects of\r\nthe late scene as she best could, followed the girl, and in a few\r\nmoments came downstairs with her hat and cloak on, ready to go. To get\r\nto the door it was necessary to pass through the hall, and before doing\r\nso she paused on the bottom of the staircase which descended into one\r\ncorner, to take a last look at the gathering.\r\n\r\nThere was no music or dancing in progress just now. At the lower end,\r\nwhich had been arranged for the work-folk specially, a group conversed\r\nin whispers, and with clouded looks. Boldwood was standing by the\r\nfireplace, and he, too, though so absorbed in visions arising from her\r\npromise that he scarcely saw anything, seemed at that moment to have\r\nobserved their peculiar manner, and their looks askance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it you are in doubt about, men?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nOne of them turned and replied uneasily: \xe2\x80\x9cIt was something Laban heard\r\nof, that\xe2\x80\x99s all, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNews? Anybody married or engaged, born or dead?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired the farmer,\r\ngaily. \xe2\x80\x9cTell it to us, Tall. One would think from your looks and\r\nmysterious ways that it was something very dreadful indeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, sir, nobody is dead,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tall.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish somebody was,\xe2\x80\x9d said Samway, in a whisper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you say, Samway?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Boldwood, somewhat sharply. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you\r\nhave anything to say, speak out; if not, get up another dance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Troy has come downstairs,\xe2\x80\x9d said Samway to Tall. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you want to\r\ntell her, you had better do it now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know what they mean?\xe2\x80\x9d the farmer asked Bathsheba, across the\r\nroom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t in the least,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba.\r\n\r\nThere was a smart rapping at the door. One of the men opened it\r\ninstantly, and went outside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Troy is wanted,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, on returning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite ready,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba. \xe2\x80\x9cThough I didn\xe2\x80\x99t tell them to send.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a stranger, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x9d said the man by the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA stranger?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsk him to come in,\xe2\x80\x9d said Boldwood.\r\n\r\nThe message was given, and Troy, wrapped up to his eyes as we have seen\r\nhim, stood in the doorway.\r\n\r\nThere was an unearthly silence, all looking towards the newcomer. Those\r\nwho had just learnt that he was in the neighbourhood recognized him\r\ninstantly; those who did not were perplexed. Nobody noted Bathsheba.\r\nShe was leaning on the stairs. Her brow had heavily contracted; her\r\nwhole face was pallid, her lips apart, her eyes rigidly staring at\r\ntheir visitor.\r\n\r\nBoldwood was among those who did not notice that he was Troy. \xe2\x80\x9cCome in,\r\ncome in!\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated, cheerfully, \xe2\x80\x9cand drain a Christmas beaker with\r\nus, stranger!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTroy next advanced into the middle of the room, took off his cap,\r\nturned down his coat-collar, and looked Boldwood in the face. Even then\r\nBoldwood did not recognize that the impersonator of Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s persistent\r\nirony towards him, who had once before broken in upon his bliss,\r\nscourged him, and snatched his delight away, had come to do these\r\nthings a second time. Troy began to laugh a mechanical laugh: Boldwood\r\nrecognized him now.\r\n\r\nTroy turned to Bathsheba. The poor girl\xe2\x80\x99s wretchedness at this time was\r\nbeyond all fancy or narration. She had sunk down on the lowest stair;\r\nand there she sat, her mouth blue and dry, and her dark eyes fixed\r\nvacantly upon him, as if she wondered whether it were not all a\r\nterrible illusion.\r\n\r\nThen Troy spoke. \xe2\x80\x9cBathsheba, I come here for you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe made no reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome home with me: come!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba moved her feet a little, but did not rise. Troy went across\r\nto her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, madam, do you hear what I say?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, peremptorily.\r\n\r\nA strange voice came from the fireplace\xe2\x80\x94a voice sounding far off and\r\nconfined, as if from a dungeon. Hardly a soul in the assembly\r\nrecognized the thin tones to be those of Boldwood. Sudden despair had\r\ntransformed him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBathsheba, go with your husband!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNevertheless, she did not move. The truth was that Bathsheba was beyond\r\nthe pale of activity\xe2\x80\x94and yet not in a swoon. She was in a state of\r\nmental _gutta serena_; her mind was for the minute totally deprived of\r\nlight at the same time no obscuration was apparent from without.\r\n\r\nTroy stretched out his hand to pull her towards him, when she quickly\r\nshrank back. This visible dread of him seemed to irritate Troy, and he\r\nseized her arm and pulled it sharply. Whether his grasp pinched her, or\r\nwhether his mere touch was the cause, was never known, but at the\r\nmoment of his seizure she writhed, and gave a quick, low scream.\r\n\r\nThe scream had been heard but a few seconds when it was followed by\r\nsudden deafening report that echoed through the room and stupefied them\r\nall. The oak partition shook with the concussion, and the place was\r\nfilled with grey smoke.\r\n\r\nIn bewilderment they turned their eyes to Boldwood. At his back, as\r\nstood before the fireplace, was a gun-rack, as is usual in farmhouses,\r\nconstructed to hold two guns. When Bathsheba had cried out in her\r\nhusband\xe2\x80\x99s grasp, Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s face of gnashing despair had changed. The\r\nveins had swollen, and a frenzied look had gleamed in his eye. He had\r\nturned quickly, taken one of the guns, cocked it, and at once\r\ndischarged it at Troy.\r\n\r\nTroy fell. The distance apart of the two men was so small that the\r\ncharge of shot did not spread in the least, but passed like a bullet\r\ninto his body. He uttered a long guttural sigh\xe2\x80\x94there was a\r\ncontraction\xe2\x80\x94an extension\xe2\x80\x94then his muscles relaxed, and he lay still.\r\n\r\nBoldwood was seen through the smoke to be now again engaged with the\r\ngun. It was double-barrelled, and he had, meanwhile, in some way\r\nfastened his hand-kerchief to the trigger, and with his foot on the\r\nother end was in the act of turning the second barrel upon himself.\r\nSamway his man was the first to see this, and in the midst of the\r\ngeneral horror darted up to him. Boldwood had already twitched the\r\nhandkerchief, and the gun exploded a second time, sending its contents,\r\nby a timely blow from Samway, into the beam which crossed the ceiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it makes no difference!\xe2\x80\x9d Boldwood gasped. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is another way\r\nfor me to die.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he broke from Samway, crossed the room to Bathsheba, and kissed\r\nher hand. He put on his hat, opened the door, and went into the\r\ndarkness, nobody thinking of preventing him.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER LIV\r\nAFTER THE SHOCK\r\n\r\n\r\nBoldwood passed into the high road and turned in the direction of\r\nCasterbridge. Here he walked at an even, steady pace over Yalbury Hill,\r\nalong the dead level beyond, mounted Mellstock Hill, and between eleven\r\nand twelve o\xe2\x80\x99clock crossed the Moor into the town. The streets were\r\nnearly deserted now, and the waving lamp-flames only lighted up rows of\r\ngrey shop-shutters, and strips of white paving upon which his step\r\nechoed as his passed along. He turned to the right, and halted before\r\nan archway of heavy stonework, which was closed by an iron studded pair\r\nof doors. This was the entrance to the gaol, and over it a lamp was\r\nfixed, the light enabling the wretched traveller to find a bell-pull.\r\n\r\nThe small wicket at last opened, and a porter appeared. Boldwood\r\nstepped forward, and said something in a low tone, when, after a delay,\r\nanother man came. Boldwood entered, and the door was closed behind him,\r\nand he walked the world no more.\r\n\r\nLong before this time Weatherbury had been thoroughly aroused, and the\r\nwild deed which had terminated Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s merrymaking became known to\r\nall. Of those out of the house Oak was one of the first to hear of the\r\ncatastrophe, and when he entered the room, which was about five minutes\r\nafter Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s exit, the scene was terrible. All the female guests\r\nwere huddled aghast against the walls like sheep in a storm, and the\r\nmen were bewildered as to what to do. As for Bathsheba, she had\r\nchanged. She was sitting on the floor beside the body of Troy, his head\r\npillowed in her lap, where she had herself lifted it. With one hand she\r\nheld her handkerchief to his breast and covered the wound, though\r\nscarcely a single drop of blood had flowed, and with the other she\r\ntightly clasped one of his. The household convulsion had made her\r\nherself again. The temporary coma had ceased, and activity had come\r\nwith the necessity for it. Deeds of endurance, which seem ordinary in\r\nphilosophy, are rare in conduct, and Bathsheba was astonishing all\r\naround her now, for her philosophy was her conduct, and she seldom\r\nthought practicable what she did not practise. She was of the stuff of\r\nwhich great men\xe2\x80\x99s mothers are made. She was indispensable to high\r\ngeneration, hated at tea parties, feared in shops, and loved at crises.\r\nTroy recumbent in his wife\xe2\x80\x99s lap formed now the sole spectacle in the\r\nmiddle of the spacious room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGabriel,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, automatically, when he entered, turning up a face\r\nof which only the well-known lines remained to tell him it was hers,\r\nall else in the picture having faded quite. \xe2\x80\x9cRide to Casterbridge\r\ninstantly for a surgeon. It is, I believe, useless, but go. Mr.\r\nBoldwood has shot my husband.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer statement of the fact in such quiet and simple words came with more\r\nforce than a tragic declamation, and had somewhat the effect of setting\r\nthe distorted images in each mind present into proper focus. Oak,\r\nalmost before he had comprehended anything beyond the briefest abstract\r\nof the event, hurried out of the room, saddled a horse and rode away.\r\nNot till he had ridden more than a mile did it occur to him that he\r\nwould have done better by sending some other man on this errand,\r\nremaining himself in the house. What had become of Boldwood? He should\r\nhave been looked after. Was he mad\xe2\x80\x94had there been a quarrel? Then how\r\nhad Troy got there? Where had he come from? How did this remarkable\r\nreappearance effect itself when he was supposed by many to be at the\r\nbottom of the sea? Oak had in some slight measure been prepared for the\r\npresence of Troy by hearing a rumour of his return just before entering\r\nBoldwood\xe2\x80\x99s house; but before he had weighed that information, this\r\nfatal event had been superimposed. However, it was too late now to\r\nthink of sending another messenger, and he rode on, in the excitement\r\nof these self-inquiries not discerning, when about three miles from\r\nCasterbridge, a square-figured pedestrian passing along under the dark\r\nhedge in the same direction as his own.\r\n\r\nThe miles necessary to be traversed, and other hindrances incidental to\r\nthe lateness of the hour and the darkness of the night, delayed the\r\narrival of Mr. Aldritch, the surgeon; and more than three hours passed\r\nbetween the time at which the shot was fired and that of his entering\r\nthe house. Oak was additionally detained in Casterbridge through having\r\nto give notice to the authorities of what had happened; and he then\r\nfound that Boldwood had also entered the town, and delivered himself\r\nup.\r\n\r\nIn the meantime the surgeon, having hastened into the hall at\r\nBoldwood\xe2\x80\x99s, found it in darkness and quite deserted. He went on to the\r\nback of the house, where he discovered in the kitchen an old man, of\r\nwhom he made inquiries.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s had him took away to her own house, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said his informant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho has?\xe2\x80\x9d said the doctor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Troy. \xe2\x80\x99A was quite dead, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was astonishing information. \xe2\x80\x9cShe had no right to do that,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nthe doctor. \xe2\x80\x9cThere will have to be an inquest, and she should have\r\nwaited to know what to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir; it was hinted to her that she had better wait till the law\r\nwas known. But she said law was nothing to her, and she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t let\r\nher dear husband\xe2\x80\x99s corpse bide neglected for folks to stare at for all\r\nthe crowners in England.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Aldritch drove at once back again up the hill to Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s. The\r\nfirst person he met was poor Liddy, who seemed literally to have\r\ndwindled smaller in these few latter hours. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat has been done?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Liddy, with suspended breath. \xe2\x80\x9cMy mistress\r\nhas done it all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is she?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpstairs with him, sir. When he was brought home and taken upstairs,\r\nshe said she wanted no further help from the men. And then she called\r\nme, and made me fill the bath, and after that told me I had better go\r\nand lie down because I looked so ill. Then she locked herself into the\r\nroom alone with him, and would not let a nurse come in, or anybody at\r\nall. But I thought I\xe2\x80\x99d wait in the next room in case she should want\r\nme. I heard her moving about inside for more than an hour, but she only\r\ncame out once, and that was for more candles, because hers had burnt\r\ndown into the socket. She said we were to let her know when you or Mr.\r\nThirdly came, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOak entered with the parson at this moment, and they all went upstairs\r\ntogether, preceded by Liddy Smallbury. Everything was silent as the\r\ngrave when they paused on the landing. Liddy knocked, and Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndress was heard rustling across the room: the key turned in the lock,\r\nand she opened the door. Her looks were calm and nearly rigid, like a\r\nslightly animated bust of Melpomene.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Mr. Aldritch, you have come at last,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured from her lips\r\nmerely, and threw back the door. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, and Mr. Thirdly. Well, all is\r\ndone, and anybody in the world may see him now.\xe2\x80\x9d She then passed by\r\nhim, crossed the landing, and entered another room.\r\n\r\nLooking into the chamber of death she had vacated they saw by the light\r\nof the candles which were on the drawers a tall straight shape lying at\r\nthe further end of the bedroom, wrapped in white. Everything around was\r\nquite orderly. The doctor went in, and after a few minutes returned to\r\nthe landing again, where Oak and the parson still waited.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is all done, indeed, as she says,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Mr. Aldritch, in a\r\nsubdued voice. \xe2\x80\x9cThe body has been undressed and properly laid out in\r\ngrave clothes. Gracious Heaven\xe2\x80\x94this mere girl! She must have the nerve\r\nof a stoic!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe heart of a wife merely,\xe2\x80\x9d floated in a whisper about the ears of\r\nthe three, and turning they saw Bathsheba in the midst of them. Then,\r\nas if at that instant to prove that her fortitude had been more of will\r\nthan of spontaneity, she silently sank down between them and was a\r\nshapeless heap of drapery on the floor. The simple consciousness that\r\nsuperhuman strain was no longer required had at once put a period to\r\nher power to continue it.\r\n\r\nThey took her away into a further room, and the medical attendance\r\nwhich had been useless in Troy\xe2\x80\x99s case was invaluable in Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s,\r\nwho fell into a series of fainting-fits that had a serious aspect for a\r\ntime. The sufferer was got to bed, and Oak, finding from the bulletins\r\nthat nothing really dreadful was to be apprehended on her score, left\r\nthe house. Liddy kept watch in Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s chamber, where she heard her\r\nmistress, moaning in whispers through the dull slow hours of that\r\nwretched night: \xe2\x80\x9cOh it is my fault\xe2\x80\x94how can I live! O Heaven, how can I\r\nlive!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER LV\r\nTHE MARCH FOLLOWING\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cBATHSHEBA BOLDWOOD\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nWe pass rapidly on into the month of March, to a breezy day without\r\nsunshine, frost, or dew. On Yalbury Hill, about midway between\r\nWeatherbury and Casterbridge, where the turnpike road passes over the\r\ncrest, a numerous concourse of people had gathered, the eyes of the\r\ngreater number being frequently stretched afar in a northerly\r\ndirection. The groups consisted of a throng of idlers, a party of\r\njavelin-men, and two trumpeters, and in the midst were carriages, one\r\nof which contained the high sheriff. With the idlers, many of whom had\r\nmounted to the top of a cutting formed for the road, were several\r\nWeatherbury men and boys\xe2\x80\x94among others Poorgrass, Coggan, and Cain Ball.\r\n\r\nAt the end of half-an-hour a faint dust was seen in the expected\r\nquarter, and shortly after a travelling-carriage, bringing one of the\r\ntwo judges on the Western Circuit, came up the hill and halted on the\r\ntop. The judge changed carriages whilst a flourish was blown by the\r\nbig-cheeked trumpeters, and a procession being formed of the vehicles\r\nand javelin-men, they all proceeded towards the town, excepting the\r\nWeatherbury men, who as soon as they had seen the judge move off\r\nreturned home again to their work.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJoseph, I seed you squeezing close to the carriage,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coggan, as\r\nthey walked. \xe2\x80\x9cDid ye notice my lord judge\xe2\x80\x99s face?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poorgrass. \xe2\x80\x9cI looked hard at en, as if I would read his\r\nvery soul; and there was mercy in his eyes\xe2\x80\x94or to speak with the exact\r\ntruth required of us at this solemn time, in the eye that was towards\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I hope for the best,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coggan, \xe2\x80\x9cthough bad that must be.\r\nHowever, I shan\xe2\x80\x99t go to the trial, and I\xe2\x80\x99d advise the rest of ye that\r\nbain\xe2\x80\x99t wanted to bide away. \xe2\x80\x99Twill disturb his mind more than anything\r\nto see us there staring at him as if he were a show.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe very thing I said this morning,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Joseph, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Justice is\r\ncome to weigh him in the balances,\xe2\x80\x99 I said in my reflectious way, \xe2\x80\x98and\r\nif he\xe2\x80\x99s found wanting, so be it unto him,\xe2\x80\x99 and a bystander said \xe2\x80\x98Hear,\r\nhear! A man who can talk like that ought to be heard.\xe2\x80\x99 But I don\xe2\x80\x99t like\r\ndwelling upon it, for my few words are my few words, and not much;\r\nthough the speech of some men is rumoured abroad as though by nature\r\nformed for such.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo \xe2\x80\x99tis, Joseph. And now, neighbours, as I said, every man bide at\r\nhome.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe resolution was adhered to; and all waited anxiously for the news\r\nnext day. Their suspense was diverted, however, by a discovery which\r\nwas made in the afternoon, throwing more light on Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s conduct\r\nand condition than any details which had preceded it.\r\n\r\nThat he had been from the time of Greenhill Fair until the fatal\r\nChristmas Eve in excited and unusual moods was known to those who had\r\nbeen intimate with him; but nobody imagined that there had shown in him\r\nunequivocal symptoms of the mental derangement which Bathsheba and Oak,\r\nalone of all others and at different times, had momentarily suspected.\r\nIn a locked closet was now discovered an extraordinary collection of\r\narticles. There were several sets of ladies\xe2\x80\x99 dresses in the piece, of\r\nsundry expensive materials; silks and satins, poplins and velvets, all\r\nof colours which from Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s style of dress might have been judged\r\nto be her favourites. There were two muffs, sable and ermine. Above all\r\nthere was a case of jewellery, containing four heavy gold bracelets and\r\nseveral lockets and rings, all of fine quality and manufacture. These\r\nthings had been bought in Bath and other towns from time to time, and\r\nbrought home by stealth. They were all carefully packed in paper, and\r\neach package was labelled \xe2\x80\x9cBathsheba Boldwood,\xe2\x80\x9d a date being subjoined\r\nsix years in advance in every instance.\r\n\r\nThese somewhat pathetic evidences of a mind crazed with care and love\r\nwere the subject of discourse in Warren\xe2\x80\x99s malt-house when Oak entered\r\nfrom Casterbridge with tidings of the sentence. He came in the\r\nafternoon, and his face, as the kiln glow shone upon it, told the tale\r\nsufficiently well. Boldwood, as every one supposed he would do, had\r\npleaded guilty, and had been sentenced to death.\r\n\r\nThe conviction that Boldwood had not been morally responsible for his\r\nlater acts now became general. Facts elicited previous to the trial had\r\npointed strongly in the same direction, but they had not been of\r\nsufficient weight to lead to an order for an examination into the state\r\nof Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s mind. It was astonishing, now that a presumption of\r\ninsanity was raised, how many collateral circumstances were remembered\r\nto which a condition of mental disease seemed to afford the only\r\nexplanation\xe2\x80\x94among others, the unprecedented neglect of his corn stacks\r\nin the previous summer.\r\n\r\nA petition was addressed to the Home Secretary, advancing the\r\ncircumstances which appeared to justify a request for a reconsideration\r\nof the sentence. It was not \xe2\x80\x9cnumerously signed\xe2\x80\x9d by the inhabitants of\r\nCasterbridge, as is usual in such cases, for Boldwood had never made\r\nmany friends over the counter. The shops thought it very natural that a\r\nman who, by importing direct from the producer, had daringly set aside\r\nthe first great principle of provincial existence, namely that God made\r\ncountry villages to supply customers to county towns, should have\r\nconfused ideas about the Decalogue. The prompters were a few merciful\r\nmen who had perhaps too feelingly considered the facts latterly\r\nunearthed, and the result was that evidence was taken which it was\r\nhoped might remove the crime in a moral point of view, out of the\r\ncategory of wilful murder, and lead it to be regarded as a sheer\r\noutcome of madness.\r\n\r\nThe upshot of the petition was waited for in Weatherbury with\r\nsolicitous interest. The execution had been fixed for eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock on\r\na Saturday morning about a fortnight after the sentence was passed, and\r\nup to Friday afternoon no answer had been received. At that time\r\nGabriel came from Casterbridge Gaol, whither he had been to wish\r\nBoldwood good-bye, and turned down a by-street to avoid the town. When\r\npast the last house he heard a hammering, and lifting his bowed head he\r\nlooked back for a moment. Over the chimneys he could see the upper part\r\nof the gaol entrance, rich and glowing in the afternoon sun, and some\r\nmoving figures were there. They were carpenters lifting a post into a\r\nvertical position within the parapet. He withdrew his eyes quickly, and\r\nhastened on.\r\n\r\nIt was dark when he reached home, and half the village was out to meet\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo tidings,\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel said, wearily. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid there\xe2\x80\x99s no hope.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve been with him more than two hours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo ye think he _really_ was out of his mind when he did it?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nSmallbury.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t honestly say that I do,\xe2\x80\x9d Oak replied. \xe2\x80\x9cHowever, that we can\r\ntalk of another time. Has there been any change in mistress this\r\nafternoon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs she downstairs?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. And getting on so nicely as she was too. She\xe2\x80\x99s but very little\r\nbetter now again than she was at Christmas. She keeps on asking if you\r\nbe come, and if there\xe2\x80\x99s news, till one\xe2\x80\x99s wearied out wi\xe2\x80\x99 answering her.\r\nShall I go and say you\xe2\x80\x99ve come?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a chance yet; but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t stay in town any\r\nlonger\xe2\x80\x94after seeing him too. So Laban\xe2\x80\x94Laban is here, isn\xe2\x80\x99t he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tall.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat I\xe2\x80\x99ve arranged is, that you shall ride to town the last thing\r\nto-night; leave here about nine, and wait a while there, getting home\r\nabout twelve. If nothing has been received by eleven to-night, they say\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s no chance at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do so hope his life will be spared,\xe2\x80\x9d said Liddy. \xe2\x80\x9cIf it is not,\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99ll go out of her mind too. Poor thing; her sufferings have been\r\ndreadful; she deserves anybody\xe2\x80\x99s pity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs she altered much?\xe2\x80\x9d said Coggan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you haven\xe2\x80\x99t seen poor mistress since Christmas, you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nher,\xe2\x80\x9d said Liddy. \xe2\x80\x9cHer eyes are so miserable that she\xe2\x80\x99s not the same\r\nwoman. Only two years ago she was a romping girl, and now she\xe2\x80\x99s this!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLaban departed as directed, and at eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock that night several of\r\nthe villagers strolled along the road to Casterbridge and awaited his\r\narrival\xe2\x80\x94among them Oak, and nearly all the rest of Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s men.\r\nGabriel\xe2\x80\x99s anxiety was great that Boldwood might be saved, even though\r\nin his conscience he felt that he ought to die; for there had been\r\nqualities in the farmer which Oak loved. At last, when they all were\r\nweary the tramp of a horse was heard in the distance\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nFirst dead, as if on turf it trode,\r\nThen, clattering on the village road\r\nIn other pace than forth he yode.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall soon know now, one way or other.\xe2\x80\x9d said Coggan, and they all\r\nstepped down from the bank on which they had been standing into the\r\nroad, and the rider pranced into the midst of them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that you, Laban?\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99tis come. He\xe2\x80\x99s not to die. \xe2\x80\x99Tis confinement during Her Majesty\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npleasure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHurrah!\xe2\x80\x9d said Coggan, with a swelling heart. \xe2\x80\x9cGod\xe2\x80\x99s above the devil\r\nyet!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER LVI\r\nBEAUTY IN LONELINESS\xe2\x80\x94AFTER ALL\r\n\r\n\r\nBathsheba revived with the spring. The utter prostration that had\r\nfollowed the low fever from which she had suffered diminished\r\nperceptibly when all uncertainty upon every subject had come to an end.\r\n\r\nBut she remained alone now for the greater part of her time, and stayed\r\nin the house, or at furthest went into the garden. She shunned every\r\none, even Liddy, and could be brought to make no confidences, and to\r\nask for no sympathy.\r\n\r\nAs the summer drew on she passed more of her time in the open air, and\r\nbegan to examine into farming matters from sheer necessity, though she\r\nnever rode out or personally superintended as at former times. One\r\nFriday evening in August she walked a little way along the road and\r\nentered the village for the first time since the sombre event of the\r\npreceding Christmas. None of the old colour had as yet come to her\r\ncheek, and its absolute paleness was heightened by the jet black of her\r\ngown, till it appeared preternatural. When she reached a little shop at\r\nthe other end of the place, which stood nearly opposite to the\r\nchurchyard, Bathsheba heard singing inside the church, and she knew\r\nthat the singers were practising. She crossed the road, opened the\r\ngate, and entered the graveyard, the high sills of the church windows\r\neffectually screening her from the eyes of those gathered within. Her\r\nstealthy walk was to the nook wherein Troy had worked at planting\r\nflowers upon Fanny Robin\xe2\x80\x99s grave, and she came to the marble tombstone.\r\n\r\nA motion of satisfaction enlivened her face as she read the complete\r\ninscription. First came the words of Troy himself:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nErected by Francis Troy\r\nIn Beloved Memory of\r\nFanny Robin\r\nWho died October 9, 18\xe2\x80\x94,\r\nAged 20 years.\r\n\r\n\r\nUnderneath this was now inscribed in new letters:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nIn the Same Grave lie\r\nThe Remains of the aforesaid\r\nFrancis Troy,\r\nWho died December 24th, 18\xe2\x80\x94,\r\nAged 26 years.\r\n\r\n\r\nWhilst she stood and read and meditated the tones of the organ began\r\nagain in the church, and she went with the same light step round to the\r\nporch and listened. The door was closed, and the choir was learning a\r\nnew hymn. Bathsheba was stirred by emotions which latterly she had\r\nassumed to be altogether dead within her. The little attenuated voices\r\nof the children brought to her ear in distinct utterance the words they\r\nsang without thought or comprehension\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nLead, kindly Light, amid the encircling gloom,\r\nLead Thou me on.\r\n\r\n\r\nBathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s feeling was always to some extent dependent upon her whim,\r\nas is the case with many other women. Something big came into her\r\nthroat and an uprising to her eyes\xe2\x80\x94and she thought that she would allow\r\nthe imminent tears to flow if they wished. They did flow and\r\nplenteously, and one fell upon the stone bench beside her. Once that\r\nshe had begun to cry for she hardly knew what, she could not leave off\r\nfor crowding thoughts she knew too well. She would have given anything\r\nin the world to be, as those children were, unconcerned at the meaning\r\nof their words, because too innocent to feel the necessity for any such\r\nexpression. All the impassioned scenes of her brief experience seemed\r\nto revive with added emotion at that moment, and those scenes which had\r\nbeen without emotion during enactment had emotion then. Yet grief came\r\nto her rather as a luxury than as the scourge of former times.\r\n\r\nOwing to Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s face being buried in her hands she did not notice\r\na form which came quietly into the porch, and on seeing her, first\r\nmoved as if to retreat, then paused and regarded her. Bathsheba did not\r\nraise her head for some time, and when she looked round her face was\r\nwet, and her eyes drowned and dim. \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Oak,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed she,\r\ndisconcerted, \xe2\x80\x9chow long have you been here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA few minutes, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak, respectfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you going in?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba; and there came from within the\r\nchurch as from a prompter\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nI loved the garish day, and, spite of fears,\r\nPride ruled my will: remember not past years.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel. \xe2\x80\x9cI am one of the bass singers, you know. I have\r\nsung bass for several months.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed: I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t aware of that. I\xe2\x80\x99ll leave you, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhich I have loved long since, and lost awhile,\r\n\r\n\r\nsang the children.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t let me drive you away, mistress. I think I won\xe2\x80\x99t go in\r\nto-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no\xe2\x80\x94you don\xe2\x80\x99t drive me away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen they stood in a state of some embarrassment, Bathsheba trying to\r\nwipe her dreadfully drenched and inflamed face without his noticing\r\nher. At length Oak said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve not seen you\xe2\x80\x94I mean spoken to you\xe2\x80\x94since\r\never so long, have I?\xe2\x80\x9d But he feared to bring distressing memories\r\nback, and interrupted himself with: \xe2\x80\x9cWere you going into church?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI came to see the tombstone privately\xe2\x80\x94to see if they\r\nhad cut the inscription as I wished. Mr. Oak, you needn\xe2\x80\x99t mind speaking\r\nto me, if you wish to, on the matter which is in both our minds at this\r\nmoment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd have they done it as you wished?\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Come and see it, if you have not already.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo together they went and read the tomb. \xe2\x80\x9cEight months ago!\xe2\x80\x9d Gabriel\r\nmurmured when he saw the date. \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems like yesterday to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd to me as if it were years ago\xe2\x80\x94long years, and I had been dead\r\nbetween. And now I am going home, Mr. Oak.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOak walked after her. \xe2\x80\x9cI wanted to name a small matter to you as soon\r\nas I could,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with hesitation. \xe2\x80\x9cMerely about business, and I\r\nthink I may just mention it now, if you\xe2\x80\x99ll allow me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, certainly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is that I may soon have to give up the management of your farm,\r\nMrs. Troy. The fact is, I am thinking of leaving England\xe2\x80\x94not yet, you\r\nknow\xe2\x80\x94next spring.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLeaving England!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, in surprise and genuine disappointment.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Gabriel, what are you going to do that for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ve thought it best,\xe2\x80\x9d Oak stammered out. \xe2\x80\x9cCalifornia is the\r\nspot I\xe2\x80\x99ve had in my mind to try.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it is understood everywhere that you are going to take poor Mr.\r\nBoldwood\xe2\x80\x99s farm on your own account.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve had the refusal o\xe2\x80\x99 it \xe2\x80\x99tis true; but nothing is settled yet, and\r\nI have reasons for giving up. I shall finish out my year there as\r\nmanager for the trustees, but no more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what shall I do without you? Oh, Gabriel, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think you ought\r\nto go away. You\xe2\x80\x99ve been with me so long\xe2\x80\x94through bright times and dark\r\ntimes\xe2\x80\x94such old friends as we are\xe2\x80\x94that it seems unkind almost. I had\r\nfancied that if you leased the other farm as master, you might still\r\ngive a helping look across at mine. And now going away!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would have willingly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet now that I am more helpless than ever you go away!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that\xe2\x80\x99s the ill fortune o\xe2\x80\x99 it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel, in a distressed\r\ntone. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd it is because of that very helplessness that I feel bound to\r\ngo. Good afternoon, ma\xe2\x80\x99am\xe2\x80\x9d he concluded, in evident anxiety to get\r\naway, and at once went out of the churchyard by a path she could follow\r\non no pretence whatever.\r\n\r\nBathsheba went home, her mind occupied with a new trouble, which being\r\nrather harassing than deadly was calculated to do good by diverting her\r\nfrom the chronic gloom of her life. She was set thinking a great deal\r\nabout Oak and of his wish to shun her; and there occurred to Bathsheba\r\nseveral incidents of her latter intercourse with him, which, trivial\r\nwhen singly viewed, amounted together to a perceptible disinclination\r\nfor her society. It broke upon her at length as a great pain that her\r\nlast old disciple was about to forsake her and flee. He who had\r\nbelieved in her and argued on her side when all the rest of the world\r\nwas against her, had at last like the others become weary and\r\nneglectful of the old cause, and was leaving her to fight her battles\r\nalone.\r\n\r\nThree weeks went on, and more evidence of his want of interest in her\r\nwas forthcoming. She noticed that instead of entering the small parlour\r\nor office where the farm accounts were kept, and waiting, or leaving a\r\nmemorandum as he had hitherto done during her seclusion, Oak never came\r\nat all when she was likely to be there, only entering at unseasonable\r\nhours when her presence in that part of the house was least to be\r\nexpected. Whenever he wanted directions he sent a message, or note with\r\nneither heading nor signature, to which she was obliged to reply in the\r\nsame offhand style. Poor Bathsheba began to suffer now from the most\r\ntorturing sting of all\xe2\x80\x94a sensation that she was despised.\r\n\r\nThe autumn wore away gloomily enough amid these melancholy conjectures,\r\nand Christmas-day came, completing a year of her legal widowhood, and\r\ntwo years and a quarter of her life alone. On examining her heart it\r\nappeared beyond measure strange that the subject of which the season\r\nmight have been supposed suggestive\xe2\x80\x94the event in the hall at\r\nBoldwood\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94was not agitating her at all; but instead, an agonizing\r\nconviction that everybody abjured her\xe2\x80\x94for what she could not tell\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nthat Oak was the ringleader of the recusants. Coming out of church that\r\nday she looked round in hope that Oak, whose bass voice she had heard\r\nrolling out from the gallery overhead in a most unconcerned manner,\r\nmight chance to linger in her path in the old way. There he was, as\r\nusual, coming down the path behind her. But on seeing Bathsheba turn,\r\nhe looked aside, and as soon as he got beyond the gate, and there was\r\nthe barest excuse for a divergence, he made one, and vanished.\r\n\r\nThe next morning brought the culminating stroke; she had been expecting\r\nit long. It was a formal notice by letter from him that he should not\r\nrenew his engagement with her for the following Lady-day.\r\n\r\nBathsheba actually sat and cried over this letter most bitterly. She\r\nwas aggrieved and wounded that the possession of hopeless love from\r\nGabriel, which she had grown to regard as her inalienable right for\r\nlife, should have been withdrawn just at his own pleasure in this way.\r\nShe was bewildered too by the prospect of having to rely on her own\r\nresources again: it seemed to herself that she never could again\r\nacquire energy sufficient to go to market, barter, and sell. Since\r\nTroy\xe2\x80\x99s death Oak had attended all sales and fairs for her, transacting\r\nher business at the same time with his own. What should she do now? Her\r\nlife was becoming a desolation.\r\n\r\nSo desolate was Bathsheba this evening, that in an absolute hunger for\r\npity and sympathy, and miserable in that she appeared to have outlived\r\nthe only true friendship she had ever owned, she put on her bonnet and\r\ncloak and went down to Oak\xe2\x80\x99s house just after sunset, guided on her way\r\nby the pale primrose rays of a crescent moon a few days old.\r\n\r\nA lively firelight shone from the window, but nobody was visible in the\r\nroom. She tapped nervously, and then thought it doubtful if it were\r\nright for a single woman to call upon a bachelor who lived alone,\r\nalthough he was her manager, and she might be supposed to call on\r\nbusiness without any real impropriety. Gabriel opened the door, and the\r\nmoon shone upon his forehead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Oak,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, faintly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; I am Mr. Oak,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel. \xe2\x80\x9cWho have I the honour\xe2\x80\x94O how stupid\r\nof me, not to know you, mistress!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall not be your mistress much longer, shall I Gabriel?\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\nin pathetic tones.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, no. I suppose\xe2\x80\x94But come in, ma\xe2\x80\x99am. Oh\xe2\x80\x94and I\xe2\x80\x99ll get a light,\xe2\x80\x9d Oak\r\nreplied, with some awkwardness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; not on my account.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is so seldom that I get a lady visitor that I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid I haven\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nproper accommodation. Will you sit down, please? Here\xe2\x80\x99s a chair, and\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s one, too. I am sorry that my chairs all have wood seats, and\r\nare rather hard, but I\xe2\x80\x94was thinking of getting some new ones.\xe2\x80\x9d Oak\r\nplaced two or three for her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are quite easy enough for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo down she sat, and down sat he, the fire dancing in their faces, and\r\nupon the old furniture,\r\n\r\nall a-sheenen\r\nWi\xe2\x80\x99 long years o\xe2\x80\x99 handlen,[3]\r\n\r\n\r\nthat formed Oak\xe2\x80\x99s array of household possessions, which sent back a\r\ndancing reflection in reply. It was very odd to these two persons, who\r\nknew each other passing well, that the mere circumstance of their\r\nmeeting in a new place and in a new way should make them so awkward and\r\nconstrained. In the fields, or at her house, there had never been any\r\nembarrassment; but now that Oak had become the entertainer their lives\r\nseemed to be moved back again to the days when they were strangers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll think it strange that I have come, but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no; not at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I thought\xe2\x80\x94Gabriel, I have been uneasy in the belief that I have\r\noffended you, and that you are going away on that account. It grieved\r\nme very much and I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help coming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOffended me! As if you could do that, Bathsheba!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t I?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, gladly. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, what are you going away for\r\nelse?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not going to emigrate, you know; I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t aware that you would\r\nwish me not to when I told \xe2\x80\x99ee or I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ha\xe2\x80\x99 thought of doing it,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said, simply. \xe2\x80\x9cI have arranged for Little Weatherbury Farm and shall\r\nhave it in my own hands at Lady-day. You know I\xe2\x80\x99ve had a share in it\r\nfor some time. Still, that wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t prevent my attending to your\r\nbusiness as before, hadn\xe2\x80\x99t it been that things have been said about\r\nus.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, in surprise. \xe2\x80\x9cThings said about you and me!\r\nWhat are they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot tell you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be wiser if you were to, I think. The top and tail o\xe2\x80\x99t is\r\nthis\xe2\x80\x94that I am sniffing about here, and waiting for poor Boldwood\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfarm, with a thought of getting you some day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGetting me! I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t know it was that you meant,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, quietly. \xe2\x80\x9cSuch a thing as\r\nthat is too absurd\xe2\x80\x94too soon\xe2\x80\x94to think of, by far!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; of course, it is too absurd. It is too absurd, as\r\nyou say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Too\xe2\x80\x94s-s-soon\xe2\x80\x99 were the words I used.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must beg your pardon for correcting you, but you said, \xe2\x80\x98too absurd,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nand so do I.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon too!\xe2\x80\x9d she returned, with tears in her eyes. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Too\r\nsoon\xe2\x80\x99 was what I said. It was very cruel of you,\r\nconsidering I was the first sweetheart that you ever had, and you were\r\nthe first I ever had; and I shall not forget it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Bathsheba, was ever anybody so provoking,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, laughing.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know it was purely that I, as an unmarried man, carrying on a\r\nbusiness for you as a very taking young woman, had a proper hard part\r\nto play\xe2\x80\x94more particular that people knew I had a sort of feeling for\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ee; and I fancied, from the way we were mentioned together, that it\r\nmight injure your good name. Nobody knows the heat and fret I have been\r\ncaused by it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd was that all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, how glad I am I came!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, thankfully, as she rose from\r\nher seat. \xe2\x80\x9cI have thought so much more of you since I fancied you did\r\nnot want even to see me again. But I must be going now, or I shall be\r\nmissed. Why Gabriel,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with a slight laugh, as they went to\r\nthe door, \xe2\x80\x9cit seems exactly as if I had come courting you\xe2\x80\x94how\r\ndreadful!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd quite right too,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve danced at your skittish heels,\r\nmy beautiful Bathsheba, for many a long mile, and many a long day; and\r\nit is hard to begrudge me this one visit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe accompanied her up the hill, explaining to her the details of his\r\nforthcoming tenure of the other farm. They spoke very little of their\r\nmutual feeling; pretty phrases and warm expressions being probably\r\nunnecessary between such tried friends. Theirs was that substantial\r\naffection which arises (if any arises at all) when the two who are\r\nthrown together begin first by knowing the rougher sides of each\r\nother\xe2\x80\x99s character, and not the best till further on, the romance\r\ngrowing up in the interstices of a mass of hard prosaic reality. This\r\ngood-fellowship\xe2\x80\x94_camaraderie_\xe2\x80\x94usually occurring through similarity of\r\npursuits, is unfortunately seldom superadded to love between the sexes,\r\nbecause men and women associate, not in their labours, but in their\r\npleasures merely. Where, however, happy circumstance permits its\r\ndevelopment, the compounded feeling proves itself to be the only love\r\nwhich is strong as death\xe2\x80\x94that love which many waters cannot quench, nor\r\nthe floods drown, beside which the passion usually called by the name\r\nis evanescent as steam.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER LVII\r\nA FOGGY NIGHT AND MORNING\xe2\x80\x94CONCLUSION\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe most private, secret, plainest wedding that it is possible to\r\nhave.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThose had been Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s words to Oak one evening, some time after\r\nthe event of the preceding chapter, and he meditated a full hour by the\r\nclock upon how to carry out her wishes to the letter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA license\xe2\x80\x94O yes, it must be a license,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself at last.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then; first, a license.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn a dark night, a few days later, Oak came with mysterious steps from\r\nthe surrogate\xe2\x80\x99s door, in Casterbridge. On the way home he heard a heavy\r\ntread in front of him, and, overtaking the man, found him to be Coggan.\r\nThey walked together into the village until they came to a little lane\r\nbehind the church, leading down to the cottage of Laban Tall, who had\r\nlately been installed as clerk of the parish, and was yet in mortal\r\nterror at church on Sundays when he heard his lone voice among certain\r\nhard words of the Psalms, whither no man ventured to follow him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, good-night, Coggan,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going down this way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Coggan, surprised; \xe2\x80\x9cwhat\xe2\x80\x99s going on to-night then, make so\r\nbold Mr. Oak?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt seemed rather ungenerous not to tell Coggan, under the\r\ncircumstances, for Coggan had been true as steel all through the time\r\nof Gabriel\xe2\x80\x99s unhappiness about Bathsheba, and Gabriel said, \xe2\x80\x9cYou can\r\nkeep a secret, Coggan?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve proved me, and you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I have, and I do know. Well, there, \xe2\x80\x99tis no\r\nconsarn of of mine, and I wish \xe2\x80\x99ee joy o\xe2\x80\x99 her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, Coggan. Bathsheba has a great wish that all the parish shall not be in\r\nchurch, looking at her\xe2\x80\x94she\xe2\x80\x99s shy-like and nervous about it, in fact\xe2\x80\x94so\r\nI be doing this to humour her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, I see: quite right, too, I suppose I must say. And you be now\r\ngoing down to the clerk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; you may as well come with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afeard your labour in keeping it close will be throwed away,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Coggan, as they walked along. \xe2\x80\x9cLabe Tall\xe2\x80\x99s old woman will horn it\r\nall over parish in half-an-hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo she will, upon my life; I never thought of that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak,\r\npausing. \xe2\x80\x9cYet I must tell him to-night, I suppose, for he\xe2\x80\x99s working so\r\nfar off, and leaves early.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell \xe2\x80\x99ee how we could tackle her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coggan. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll knock and\r\nask to speak to Laban outside the door, you standing in the background.\r\nThen he\xe2\x80\x99ll come out, and you can tell yer tale. She\xe2\x80\x99ll never guess what\r\nI want en for; and I\xe2\x80\x99ll make up a few words about the farm-work, as a\r\nblind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis scheme was considered feasible; and Coggan advanced boldly, and\r\nrapped at Mrs. Tall\xe2\x80\x99s door. Mrs. Tall herself opened it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wanted to have a word with Laban.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s not at home, and won\xe2\x80\x99t be this side of eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock. He\xe2\x80\x99ve been\r\nforced to go over to Yalbury since shutting out work. I shall do quite\r\nas well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hardly think you will. Stop a moment;\xe2\x80\x9d and Coggan stepped round the\r\ncorner of the porch to consult Oak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s t\xe2\x80\x99other man, then?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Tall.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly a friend,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coggan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay he\xe2\x80\x99s wanted to meet mistress near church-hatch to-morrow morning\r\nat ten,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak, in a whisper. \xe2\x80\x9cThat he must come without fail, and\r\nwear his best clothes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe clothes will floor us as safe as houses!\xe2\x80\x9d said Coggan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt can\xe2\x80\x99t be helped,\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak. \xe2\x80\x9cTell her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo Coggan delivered the message. \xe2\x80\x9cMind, het or wet, blow or snow, he\r\nmust come,\xe2\x80\x9d added Jan. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis very particular, indeed. The fact is, \xe2\x80\x99tis\r\nto witness her sign some law-work about taking shares wi\xe2\x80\x99 another\r\nfarmer for a long span o\xe2\x80\x99 years. There, that\xe2\x80\x99s what \xe2\x80\x99tis, and now I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\ntold \xe2\x80\x99ee, Mother Tall, in a way I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ha\xe2\x80\x99 done if I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t loved\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ee so hopeless well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCoggan retired before she could ask any further; and next they called\r\nat the vicar\xe2\x80\x99s in a manner which excited no curiosity at all. Then\r\nGabriel went home, and prepared for the morrow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLiddy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, on going to bed that night, \xe2\x80\x9cI want you to\r\ncall me at seven o\xe2\x80\x99clock to-morrow, in case I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t wake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you always do wake afore then, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but I have something important to do, which I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you of when\r\nthe time comes, and it\xe2\x80\x99s best to make sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba, however, awoke voluntarily at four, nor could she by any\r\ncontrivance get to sleep again. About six, being quite positive that\r\nher watch had stopped during the night, she could wait no longer. She\r\nwent and tapped at Liddy\xe2\x80\x99s door, and after some labour awoke her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I thought it was I who had to call you?\xe2\x80\x9d said the bewildered\r\nLiddy. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd it isn\xe2\x80\x99t six yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed it is; how can you tell such a story, Liddy? I know it must be\r\never so much past seven. Come to my room as soon as you can; I want you\r\nto give my hair a good brushing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen Liddy came to Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s room her mistress was already waiting.\r\nLiddy could not understand this extraordinary promptness. \xe2\x80\x9cWhatever\r\n_is_ going on, ma\xe2\x80\x99am?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba, with a mischievous smile in her\r\nbright eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cFarmer Oak is coming here to dine with me to-day!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFarmer Oak\xe2\x80\x94and nobody else?\xe2\x80\x94you two alone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut is it safe, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, after what\xe2\x80\x99s been said?\xe2\x80\x9d asked her companion,\r\ndubiously. \xe2\x80\x9cA woman\xe2\x80\x99s good name is such a perishable article that\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBathsheba laughed with a flushed cheek, and whispered in Liddy\xe2\x80\x99s ear,\r\nalthough there was nobody present. Then Liddy stared and exclaimed,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSouls alive, what news! It makes my heart go quite bumpity-bump!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt makes mine rather furious, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bathsheba. \xe2\x80\x9cHowever, there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nno getting out of it now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was a damp disagreeable morning. Nevertheless, at twenty minutes to\r\nten o\xe2\x80\x99clock, Oak came out of his house, and\r\n\r\nWent up the hill side\r\nWith that sort of stride\r\nA man puts out when walking in search of a bride,\r\n\r\n\r\nand knocked at Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s door. Ten minutes later a large and a\r\nsmaller umbrella might have been seen moving from the same door, and\r\nthrough the mist along the road to the church. The distance was not\r\nmore than a quarter of a mile, and these two sensible persons deemed it\r\nunnecessary to drive. An observer must have been very close indeed to\r\ndiscover that the forms under the umbrellas were those of Oak and\r\nBathsheba, arm-in-arm for the first time in their lives, Oak in a\r\ngreatcoat extending to his knees, and Bathsheba in a cloak that reached\r\nher clogs. Yet, though so plainly dressed, there was a certain\r\nrejuvenated appearance about her:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nAs though a rose should shut and be a bud again.\r\n\r\n\r\nRepose had again incarnadined her cheeks; and having, at Gabriel\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nrequest, arranged her hair this morning as she had worn it years ago on\r\nNorcombe Hill, she seemed in his eyes remarkably like a girl of that\r\nfascinating dream, which, considering that she was now only three or\r\nfour-and-twenty, was perhaps not very wonderful. In the church were\r\nTall, Liddy, and the parson, and in a remarkably short space of time\r\nthe deed was done.\r\n\r\nThe two sat down very quietly to tea in Bathsheba\xe2\x80\x99s parlour in the\r\nevening of the same day, for it had been arranged that Farmer Oak\r\nshould go there to live, since he had as yet neither money, house, nor\r\nfurniture worthy of the name, though he was on a sure way towards them,\r\nwhilst Bathsheba was, comparatively, in a plethora of all three.\r\n\r\nJust as Bathsheba was pouring out a cup of tea, their ears were greeted\r\nby the firing of a cannon, followed by what seemed like a tremendous\r\nblowing of trumpets, in the front of the house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere!\xe2\x80\x9d said Oak, laughing, \xe2\x80\x9cI knew those fellows were up to\r\nsomething, by the look on their faces.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOak took up the light and went into the porch, followed by Bathsheba\r\nwith a shawl over her head. The rays fell upon a group of male figures\r\ngathered upon the gravel in front, who, when they saw the newly-married\r\ncouple in the porch, set up a loud \xe2\x80\x9cHurrah!\xe2\x80\x9d and at the same moment\r\nbang again went the cannon in the background, followed by a hideous\r\nclang of music from a drum, tambourine, clarionet, serpent, hautboy,\r\ntenor-viol, and double-bass\xe2\x80\x94the only remaining relics of the true and\r\noriginal Weatherbury band\xe2\x80\x94venerable worm-eaten instruments, which had\r\ncelebrated in their own persons the victories of Marlborough, under the\r\nfingers of the forefathers of those who played them now. The performers\r\ncame forward, and marched up to the front.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose bright boys, Mark Clark and Jan, are at the bottom of all this,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Oak. \xe2\x80\x9cCome in, souls, and have something to eat and drink wi\xe2\x80\x99 me\r\nand my wife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot to-night,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Clark, with evident self-denial. \xe2\x80\x9cThank ye all\r\nthe same; but we\xe2\x80\x99ll call at a more seemly time. However, we couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthink of letting the day pass without a note of admiration of some\r\nsort. If ye could send a drop of som\xe2\x80\x99at down to Warren\xe2\x80\x99s, why so it is.\r\nHere\xe2\x80\x99s long life and happiness to neighbour Oak and his comely bride!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank ye; thank ye all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gabriel. \xe2\x80\x9cA bit and a drop shall be sent\r\nto Warren\xe2\x80\x99s for ye at once. I had a thought that we might very likely\r\nget a salute of some sort from our old friends, and I was saying so to\r\nmy wife but now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith,\xe2\x80\x9d said Coggan, in a critical tone, turning to his companions,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthe man hev learnt to say \xe2\x80\x98my wife\xe2\x80\x99 in a wonderful naterel way,\r\nconsidering how very youthful he is in wedlock as yet\xe2\x80\x94hey, neighbours\r\nall?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never heerd a skilful old married feller of twenty years\xe2\x80\x99 standing\r\npipe \xe2\x80\x98my wife\xe2\x80\x99 in a more used note than \xe2\x80\x99a did,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jacob Smallbury.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt might have been a little more true to nater if\xe2\x80\x99t had been spoke a\r\nlittle chillier, but that wasn\xe2\x80\x99t to be expected just now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat improvement will come wi\xe2\x80\x99 time,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jan, twirling his eye.\r\n\r\nThen Oak laughed, and Bathsheba smiled (for she never laughed readily\r\nnow), and their friends turned to go.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; I suppose that\xe2\x80\x99s the size o\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Joseph Poorgrass with a\r\ncheerful sigh as they moved away; \xe2\x80\x9cand I wish him joy o\xe2\x80\x99 her; though I\r\nwere once or twice upon saying to-day with holy Hosea, in my scripture\r\nmanner, which is my second nature, \xe2\x80\x98Ephraim is joined to idols: let him\r\nalone.\xe2\x80\x99 But since \xe2\x80\x99tis as \xe2\x80\x99tis, why, it might have been worse, and I\r\nfeel my thanks accordingly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nNOTES\r\n\r\n [1] This phrase is a conjectural emendation of the unintelligible\r\n expression, \xe2\x80\x9cas the Devil said to the Owl,\xe2\x80\x9d used by the natives.\r\n\r\n [2] The local tower and churchyard do not answer precisely to the\r\n foregoing description.\r\n\r\n [3] W.
